   <TITLE>the conclusion</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>The Conclusion</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Now, Reader, I have told my dream to thee;



<P>See if thou canst interpret it to me,



<P>Or to thyself, or neighbour; but take heed



<P>Of misinterpreting; for that, instead



<P>Of doing good, will but thyself abuse:



<P>By misinterpreting, evil ensues.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Take heed, also, that thou be not extreme,



<P>In playing with the outside of my dream:



<P>Nor let my figure or similitude



<P>Put thee into a laughter or a feud.



<P>Leave this for boys and fools; but as for thee,



<P>Do thou the substance of my matter see.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Put by the curtains, look within my veil,



<P>Turn up my metaphors, and do not fail,



<P>There, if thou seekest them, such things to find,



<P>As will be helpful to an honest mind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>What of my dross thou findest there, be bold



<P>To throw away, but yet preserve the gold;



<P>What if my gold be wrapped up in ore?--



<P>None throws away the apple for the core.



<P>But if thou shalt cast all away as vain,



<P>I know not but 'twill make me dream again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<UL>

<UL><B><FONT SIZE=+1>End</FONT></B></UL>

</UL>



<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

by John Bunyan</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>p251-p300</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B><FONT SIZE=+2></FONT></B>



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Page251--Page300</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>{251}



<P>SAVE-ALL.&nbsp; That is bad, but we read of some that are righteous

overmuch;



<P>and such men's rigidness prevails with them to judge and condemn



<P>all but themselves.&nbsp; But, I pray, what, and how many,



<P>were the things wherein you differed?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; Why, they, after their headstrong manner,



<P>conclude that it is duty to rush on their journey all weathers;



<P>and I am for waiting for wind and tide.&nbsp; They are for hazarding

all



<P>for God at a clap; and I am for taking all advantages to secure



<P>my life and estate.&nbsp; They are for holding their notions,



<P>though all other men are against them; but I am for religion in what,



<P>and so far as the times, and my safety, will bear it.&nbsp; They are

for



<P>religion when in rags and contempt; but I am for him when he walks



<P>in his golden slippers, in the sunshine, and with applause.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{252}



<P>HOLD-THE-WORLD.&nbsp; Ay, and hold you there still, good Mr. By-ends;



<P>for, for my part, I can count him but a fool, that, having the liberty



<P>to keep what he has, shall be so unwise as to lose it.



<P>Let us be wise as serpents; it is best to make hay when the sun shines;



<P>you see how the bee lieth still all winter, and bestirs her only



<P>when she can have profit with pleasure.&nbsp; God sends sometimes rain,



<P>and sometimes sunshine; if they be such fools to go through the first,



<P>yet let us be content to take fair weather along with us.&nbsp; For

my part,



<P>I like that religion best that will stand with the security



<P>of God's good blessings unto us; for who can imagine,



<P>that is ruled by his reason, since God has bestowed upon us



<P>the good things of this life, but that he would have us keep them



<P>for his sake?&nbsp; Abraham and Solomon grew rich in religion.



<P>And Job says, that a good man shall lay up gold as dust.



<P>But he must not be such as the men before us, if they be



<P>as you have described them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>SAVE-ALL.&nbsp; I think that we are all agreed in this matter,



<P>and therefore there needs no more words about it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>MONEY-LOVE.&nbsp; No, there needs no more words about this matter,



<P>indeed; for he that believes neither Scripture nor reason



<P>(and you see we have both on our side) neither knows his own liberty,



<P>nor seeks his own safety.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{253}



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; My brethren, we are, as you see, going all on pilgrimage;



<P>and, for our better diversion from things that are bad,



<P>give me leave to propound unto you this question:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Suppose a man, a minister, or a tradesman, &amp;c., should have



<P>an advantage lie before him, to get the good blessings of this life,



<P>yet so as that he can by no means come by them except,



<P>in appearance at least, he becomes extraordinarily zealous



<P>in some points of religion that he meddled not with before,



<P>may he not use these means to attain his end, and yet be



<P>a right honest man?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{254}



<P>MONEY-LOVE.&nbsp; I see the bottom of your question; and,



<P>with these gentlemen's good leave, I will endeavour to shape you



<P>an answer.&nbsp; And first, to speak to your question as it concerns



<P>a minister himself:&nbsp; Suppose a minister, a worthy man,



<P>possessed but of a very small benefice, and has in his eye a greater,



<P>more fat, and plump by far; he has also now an opportunity



<P>of getting of it, yet so as by being more studious, by preaching



<P>more frequently and zealously, and, because the temper of the people



<P>requires it, by altering of some of his principles; for my part,



<P>I see no reason but a man may do this, (provided he has a call), ay,



<P>and more a great deal besides, and yet be an honest man.&nbsp; For why--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{255}



<P>1.&nbsp; His desire of a greater benefice is lawful, (this cannot



<P>be contradicted), since it is set before him by Providence; so then,



<P>he may get it, if he can, making no question for conscience' sake.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{256}



<P>2.&nbsp; Besides, his desire after that benefice makes him more studious,



<P>a more zealous preacher, &amp;c., and so makes him a better man;



<P>yea, makes him better improve his parts, which is according to



<P>the mind of God.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{257}



<P>3.&nbsp; Now, as for his complying with the temper of his people,



<P>by dissenting, to serve them, some of his principles,



<P>this argueth, (1) That he is of a self-denying, temper;



<P>(2) Of a sweet and winning deportment; and so (3) more fit



<P>for the ministerial function.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{258}



<P>4.&nbsp; I conclude, then, that a minister that changes a small for

a great,



<P>should not, for so doing, be judged as covetous; but rather,



<P>since he has improved in his parts and industry thereby,



<P>be counted as one that pursues his call, and the opportunity



<P>put into his hands to do good.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{259}



<P>And now to the second part of the question, which concerns



<P>the tradesman you mentioned.&nbsp; Suppose such a one to have



<P>but a poor employ in the world, but by becoming religious,



<P>he may mend his market, perhaps get a rich wife, or more and far better



<P>customers to his shop; for my part, I see no reason but that this



<P>may be lawfully done.&nbsp; For why--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>1.&nbsp; To become religious is a virtue, by what means soever



<P>a man becomes so.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>2.&nbsp; Nor is it unlawful to get a rich wife, or more custom to my

shop.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>3.&nbsp; Besides, the man that gets these by becoming religious, gets

that



<P>which is good, of them that are good, by becoming good himself;



<P>so then here is a good wife, and good customers, and good gain,



<P>and all these by becoming religious, which is good; therefore,



<P>to become religious, to get all these, is a good and profitable design.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{260}



<P>This answer, thus made by this Mr. Money-love to Mr. By-ends's question,



<P>was highly applauded by them all; wherefore they concluded



<P>upon the whole, that it was most wholesome and advantageous.



<P>And because, as they thought, no man was able to contradict it,



<P>and because Christian and Hopeful were yet within call,



<P>they jointly agreed to assault them with the question as soon



<P>as they overtook them; and the rather because they had opposed



<P>Mr. By-ends before.&nbsp; So they called after them, and they stopped,



<P>and stood still till they came up to them; but they concluded,



<P>as they went, that not Mr. By-ends, but old Mr. Hold-the-world,



<P>should propound the question to them, because, as they supposed,



<P>their answer to him would be without the remainder of that heat



<P>that was kindled betwixt Mr. By-ends and them, at their parting



<P>a little before.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{260}



<P>So they came up to each other, and after a short salutation,



<P>Mr. Hold-the-world propounded the question to Christian and his fellow,



<P>and bid them to answer it if they could.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then said Christian, Even a babe in religion may answer



<P>ten thousand such questions.&nbsp; For if it be unlawful to follow Christ



<P>for loaves, (as it is in the sixth of John), how much more



<P>abominable is it to make of him and religion a stalking-horse



<P>to get and enjoy the world!&nbsp; Nor do we find any other than heathens,



<P>hypocrites, devils, and witches, that are of this opinion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{261}



<P>1.&nbsp; Heathens; for when Hamor and Shechem had a mind to the daughter



<P>and cattle of Jacob, and saw that there was no way for them



<P>to come at them, but by becoming circumcised, they say



<P>to their companions, If every male of us be circumcised,



<P>as they are circumcised, shall not their cattle, and their substance,



<P>and every beast of theirs, be ours?&nbsp; Their daughter and their cattle



<P>were that which they sought to obtain, and their religion



<P>the stalking-horse they made use of to come at them.



<P>Read the whole story.&nbsp; [Gen. 34:20-23]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{262}



<P>2.&nbsp; The hypocritical Pharisees were also of this religion;



<P>long prayers were their pretence, but to get widows' houses



<P>was their intent; and greater damnation was from God their judgment.



<P>[Luke 20:46-47]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{263}



<P>3.&nbsp; Judas the devil was also of this religion; he was religious



<P>for the bag, that he might be possessed of what was therein;



<P>but he was lost, cast away, and the very son of perdition.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{264}



<P>4.&nbsp; Simon the witch was of this religion too; for he would have

had



<P>the Holy Ghost, that he might have got money therewith;



<P>and his sentence from Peter's mouth was according.&nbsp; [Acts 8:19-22]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{265}



<P>5.&nbsp; Neither will it out of my mind, but that that man that



<P>takes up religion for the world, will throw away religion for the world;



<P>for so surely as Judas resigned the world in becoming religious,



<P>so surely did he also sell religion and his Master for the same.



<P>To answer the question, therefore, affirmatively, as I perceive



<P>you have done, and to accept of, as authentic, such answer,



<P>is both heathenish, hypocritical, and devilish; and your reward will

be



<P>according to your works.&nbsp; Then they stood staring one upon another,



<P>but had not wherewith to answer Christian.&nbsp; Hopeful also approved

of



<P>the soundness of Christian's answer; so there was a great silence



<P>among them.&nbsp; Mr. By-ends and his company also staggered and kept

behind,



<P>that Christian and Hopeful might outgo them.&nbsp; Then said Christian



<P>to his fellow, If these men cannot stand before the sentence of men,



<P>what will they do with the sentence of God?&nbsp; And if they are mute



<P>when dealt with by vessels of clay, what will they do when they shall

be



<P>rebuked by the flames of a devouring fire?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{266}



<P>&lt;The ease that pilgrims have is but little in this life>



<P>Then Christian and Hopeful outwent them again, and went till they came



<P>to a delicate plain called Ease, where they went with much content;



<P>but that plain was but narrow, so they were quickly got over it.



<P>&lt;Lucre Hill a dangerous hill>&nbsp; Now at the further side of that

plain



<P>was a little hill called Lucre, and in that hill a silver mine,



<P>which some of them that had formerly gone that way,



<P>because of the rarity of it, had turned aside to see; but going too

near



<P>the brink of the pit, the ground being deceitful under them, broke,



<P>and they were slain; some also had been maimed there, and could not,



<P>to their dying day, be their own men again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{267}



<P>&lt;Demas at the Hill Lucre.&nbsp; He calls to Christian and Hopeful



<P>to come to him>



<P>Then I saw in my dream, that a little off the road,



<P>over against the silver mine, stood Demas (gentlemanlike)



<P>to call to passengers to come and see; who said to Christian



<P>and his fellow, Ho! turn aside hither, and I will show you a thing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; What thing so deserving as to turn us out of the way to see

it?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>DEMAS.&nbsp; Here is a silver mine, and some digging in it for treasure.



<P>If you will come, with a little pains you may richly provide



<P>for yourselves.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{268}



<P>&lt;Hopeful tempted to go, but Christian holds him back>



<P>Hope. Then said Hopeful, Let us go see.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Not I, said Christian, I have heard of this place before

now;



<P>and how many have there been slain; and besides that,



<P>treasure is a snare to those that seek it; for it hindereth them



<P>in their pilgrimage.&nbsp; Then Christian called to Demas, saying,



<P>Is not the place dangerous?&nbsp; Hath it not hindered many



<P>in their pilgrimage?&nbsp; [Hos. 14:8]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>DEMAS.&nbsp; Not very dangerous, except to those that are careless,



<P>(but withal, he blushed as he spake).

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then said Christian to Hopeful, Let us not stir a step,



<P>but still keep on our way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; I will warrant you, when By-ends comes up, if he hath



<P>the same invitation as we, he will turn in thither to see.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; No doubt thereof, for his principles lead him that way,



<P>and a hundred to one but he dies there.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>DEMAS.&nbsp; Then Demas called again, saying, But will you not



<P>come over and see?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{269}



<P>&lt;Christian roundeth up Demas>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then Christian roundly answered, saying, Demas,



<P>thou art an enemy to the right ways of the Lord of this way,



<P>and hast been already condemned for thine own turning aside,



<P>by one of His Majesty's judges [2 Tim. 4:10]; and why seekest thou



<P>to bring us into the like condemnation?&nbsp; Besides, if we at all



<P>turn aside, our Lord and King will certainly hear thereof,



<P>and will there put us to shame, where we would stand with boldness



<P>before him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Demas cried again, that he also was one of their fraternity;



<P>and that if they would tarry a little, he also himself



<P>would walk with them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{270}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then said Christian, What is thy name?&nbsp; Is it not the

same



<P>by the which I have called thee?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>DEMAS.&nbsp; Yes, my name is Demas; I am the son of Abraham.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I know you; Gehazi was your great-grandfather,



<P>and Judas your father; and you have trod in their steps.



<P>[2 Kings 5:20, Matt. 26:14,15, 27:1-5]&nbsp; It is but a devilish prank



<P>that thou usest; thy father was hanged for a traitor,



<P>and thou deservest no better reward.&nbsp; Assure thyself,



<P>that when we come to the King, we will do him word of this



<P>thy behaviour.&nbsp; Thus they went their way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{271}



<P>&lt;By-ends goes over to Demas>



<P>By this time By-ends and his companions were come again within sight,



<P>and they, at the first beck, went over to Demas.&nbsp; Now,



<P>whether they fell into the pit by looking over the brink thereof,



<P>or whether they went down to dig, or whether they were smothered



<P>in the bottom by the damps that commonly arise, of these things



<P>I am not certain; but this I observed, that they never were seen again



<P>in the way.&nbsp; Then sang Christian--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; By-ends and silver Demas both agree;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; One calls, the other runs, that he may be



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; A sharer in his lucre; so these do



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Take up in this world, and no further go.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{272}



<P>&lt;They see a strange monument>



<P>Now I saw that, just on the other side of this plain,



<P>the pilgrims came to a place where stood an old monument,



<P>hard by the highway side, at the sight of which they were



<P>both concerned, because of the strangeness of the form thereof;



<P>for it seemed to them as if it had been a woman transformed



<P>into the shape of a pillar; here, therefore they stood looking,



<P>and looking upon it, but could not for a time tell what



<P>they should make thereof.&nbsp; At last Hopeful espied written above



<P>the head thereof, a writing in an unusual hand; but he being no scholar,



<P>called to Christian (for he was learned) to see if he could pick out



<P>the meaning; so he came, and after a little laying of letters together,



<P>he found the same to be this, "Remember Lot's Wife".&nbsp; So he read

it



<P>to his fellow; after which they both concluded that that was



<P>the pillar of salt into which Lot's wife was turned,



<P>for her looking back with a covetous heart, when she was going



<P>from Sodom for safety.&nbsp; [Gen. 19:26]&nbsp;&nbsp; Which sudden and

amazing sight



<P>gave them occasion of this discourse.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{273}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Ah, my brother! this is a seasonable sight;



<P>it came opportunely to us after the invitation which Demas gave us



<P>to come over to view the Hill Lucre; and had we gone over,



<P>as he desired us, and as thou wast inclining to do, my brother,



<P>we had, for aught I know, been made ourselves like this woman,



<P>a spectacle for those that shall come after to behold.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; I am sorry that I was so foolish, and am made to wonder



<P>that I am not now as Lot's wife; for wherein was the difference betwixt



<P>her sin and mine?&nbsp; She only looked back; and I had a desire to

go see.



<P>Let grace be adored, and let me be ashamed that ever such a thing



<P>should be in mine heart.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{274}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Let us take notice of what we see here, for our help



<P>for time to come.&nbsp; This woman escaped one judgment,



<P>for she fell not by the destruction of Sodom; yet she was destroyed



<P>by another, as we see she is turned into a pillar of salt.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; True; and she may be to us both caution and example;



<P>caution, that we should shun her sin; or a sign of what judgment



<P>will overtake such as shall not be prevented by this caution;



<P>so Korah, Dathan, and Abiram, with the two hundred and fifty men



<P>that perished in their sin, did also become a sign or example to others



<P>to beware.&nbsp; [Num. 26:9,10]&nbsp; But above all, I muse at one thing,

to wit,



<P>how Demas and his fellows can stand so confidently yonder to look for



<P>that treasure, which this woman, but for looking behind her after,



<P>(for we read not that she stepped one foot out of the way) was turned



<P>into a pillar of salt; especially since the judgment which overtook

her



<P>did make her an example, within sight of where they are;



<P>for they cannot choose but see her, did they but lift up their eyes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{275}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; It is a thing to be wondered at, and it argueth that



<P>their hearts are grown desperate in the case; and I cannot tell who



<P>to compare them to so fitly, as to them that pick pockets



<P>in the presence of the judge, or that will cut purses under the gallows.



<P>It is said of the men of Sodom, that they were sinners exceedingly,



<P>because they were sinners before the Lord, that is, in his eyesight,



<P>and notwithstanding the kindnesses that he had showed them [Gen. 13:13];



<P>for the land of Sodom was now like the garden of Eden heretofore.



<P>[Gen. 13:10]&nbsp; This, therefore, provoked him the more to jealousy,



<P>and made their plague as hot as the fire of the Lord out of heaven



<P>could make it.&nbsp; And it is most rationally to be concluded, that

such,



<P>even such as these are, that shall sin in the sight, yea,



<P>and that too in despite of such examples that are set continually



<P>before them, to caution them to the contrary, must be partakers



<P>of severest judgments.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Doubtless thou hast said the truth; but what a mercy is

it,



<P>that neither thou, but especially I, am not made myself this example!



<P>This ministereth occasion to us to thank God, to fear before him,



<P>and always to remember Lot's wife.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{276}



<P>&lt;A river>



<P>I saw, then, that they went on their way to a pleasant river;



<P>which David the king called "the river of God", but John,



<P>"the river of the water of life".&nbsp; [Ps. 65:9, Rev. 22, Ezek. 47]



<P>Now their way lay just upon the bank of the river; here, therefore,



<P>Christian and his companion walked with great delight;



<P>they drank also of the water of the river, which was pleasant,



<P>and enlivening to their weary spirits:&nbsp; &lt;Trees by the river.



<P>The fruit and leaves of the tree> besides, on the banks of this river,



<P>on either side, were green trees, that bore all manner of fruit;



<P>and the leaves of the trees were good for medicine;



<P>with the fruit of these trees they were also much delighted;



<P>and the leaves they eat to prevent surfeits, and other diseases



<P>that are incident to those that heat their blood by travels.



<P>&lt;A meadow in which they lie down to sleep>&nbsp; On either side of

the river



<P>was also a meadow, curiously beautified with lilies, and it was green



<P>all the year long.&nbsp; In this meadow they lay down, and slept;



<P>for here they might lie down safely.&nbsp; When they awoke,



<P>they gathered again of the fruit of the trees, and drank again



<P>of the water of the river, and then lay down again to sleep.



<P>[Ps. 23:2, Isa. 14:30]&nbsp; Thus they did several days and nights.



<P>Then they sang--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Behold ye how these crystal streams do

glide,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; To comfort pilgrims by the highway side;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The meadows green, beside their fragrant

smell,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Yield dainties for them; and he that

can tell



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; What pleasant fruit, yea, leaves, these

trees do yield,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Will soon sell all, that he may buy this

field.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So when they were disposed to go on, (for they were not, as yet,



<P>at their journey's end,) they ate and drank, and departed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{277}



<P>&lt;By-path Meadow>



<P>Now, I beheld in my dream, that they had not journeyed far,



<P>but the river and the way for a time parted; at which they were not



<P>a little sorry; yet they durst not go out of the way.



<P>Now the way from the river was rough, and their feet tender,



<P>by reason of their travels; so the souls of the pilgrims



<P>were much discouraged because of the way.&nbsp; [Num. 21:4]&nbsp; Wherefore,



<P>still as they went on, they wished for better way.&nbsp; Now,



<P>a little before them, there was on the left hand of the road a meadow,



<P>and a stile to go over into it; and that meadow is called



<P>By-path Meadow.&nbsp; &lt;One temptation does make way for another>&nbsp;

Then said



<P>Christian to his fellow, If this meadow lieth along by our wayside,



<P>let us go over into it.&nbsp; Then he went to the stile to see, and

behold,



<P>a path lay along by the way, on the other side of the fence.



<P>It is according to my wish, said Christian.&nbsp; Here is the easiest

going;



<P>come, good Hopeful, and let us go over.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{278}



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; But how if this path should lead us out of the way?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Strong Christians may lead weak ones out of the way>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; That is not like, said the other.&nbsp; Look, doth it not

go along



<P>by the wayside?&nbsp; So Hopeful, being persuaded by his fellow,



<P>went after him over the stile.&nbsp; &lt;See what it is too suddenly



<P>to fall in with strangers>&nbsp; When they were gone over,



<P>and were got into the path, they found it very easy for their feet;



<P>and withal, they, looking before them, espied a man walking as they

did,



<P>(and his name was Vain-confidence); so they called after him,



<P>and asked him whither that way led.&nbsp; He said, To the Celestial

Gate.



<P>Look, said Christian, did not I tell you so?&nbsp; By this you may see



<P>we are right.&nbsp; So they followed, and he went before them.



<P>But, behold, the night came on, and it grew very dark;



<P>so that they that were behind lost the sight of him that went before.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{279}



<P>&lt;A pit to catch the vain-glorious in>



<P>He, therefore, that went before, (Vain-confidence by name),



<P>not seeing the way before him, fell into a deep pit [Isa. 9:16],



<P>which was on purpose there made, by the Prince of those grounds,



<P>to catch vain-glorious fools withal, and was dashed in pieces



<P>with his fall.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{280}



<P>&lt;Reasoning between Christian and Hopeful>



<P>Now Christian and his fellow heard him fall.&nbsp; So they called to

know



<P>the matter, but there was none to answer, only they heard a groaning.



<P>Then said Hopeful, Where are we now?&nbsp; Then was his fellow silent,



<P>as mistrusting that he had led him out of the way; and now it began



<P>to rain, and thunder, and lighten in a very dreadful manner;



<P>and the water rose amain.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then Hopeful groaned in himself, saying, Oh, that I had kept on my way!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{281}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Who could have thought that this path should have led us



<P>out of the way?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; I was afraid on it at the very first, and therefore gave

you



<P>that gentle caution.&nbsp; I would have spoken plainer, but that you



<P>are older than I.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Christian's repentance for leading of his brother out of the way>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Good brother, be not offended; I am sorry I have brought

thee



<P>out of the way, and that I have put thee into such imminent danger;



<P>pray, my brother, forgive me; I did not do it of an evil intent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Be comforted, my brother, for I forgive thee; and believe,

too,



<P>that this shall be for our good.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I am glad I have with me a merciful brother; but we must

not



<P>stand thus: let us try to go back again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; But, good brother, let me go before.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; No, if you please, let me go first, that if there be any

danger,



<P>I may be first therein, because by my means we are both gone out



<P>of the way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{282}



<P>&lt;They are in danger of drowning as they go back>



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; No, said Hopeful, you shall not go first; for your mind



<P>being troubled may lead you out of the way again.&nbsp; Then,



<P>for their encouragement, they heard the voice of one saying,



<P>"Set thine heart toward the highway, even the way which thou wentest;



<P>turn again."&nbsp; [Jer. 31:21]&nbsp; But by this time the waters



<P>were greatly risen, by reason of which the way of going back



<P>was very dangerous.&nbsp; (Then I thought that it is easier going out



<P>of the way, when we are in, than going in when we are out.)



<P>Yet they adventured to go back, but it was so dark,



<P>and the flood was so high, that in their going back they had like



<P>to have been drowned nine or ten times.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{283}



<P>&lt;They sleep in the grounds of Giant Despair>



<P>Neither could they, with all the skill they had, get again to the stile



<P>that night.&nbsp; Wherefore, at last, lighting under a little shelter,



<P>they sat down there until the daybreak; but, being weary,



<P>they fell asleep.&nbsp; &lt;He finds them in his grounds, and carries

them



<P>to Doubting Castle>&nbsp; Now there was, not far from the place



<P>where they lay, a castle called Doubting Castle, the owner whereof



<P>was Giant Despair; and it was in his grounds they now were sleeping:



<P>wherefore he, getting up in the morning early, and walking up and down



<P>in his fields, caught Christian and Hopeful asleep in his grounds.



<P>Then, with a grim and surly voice, he bid them awake; and asked them



<P>whence they were, and what they did in his grounds.&nbsp; They told

him



<P>they were pilgrims, and that they had lost their way.



<P>Then said the Giant, You have this night trespassed on me,



<P>by trampling in and lying on my grounds, and therefore you must go along



<P>with me.&nbsp; So they were forced to go, because he was stronger than

they.



<P>They also had but little to say, for they knew themselves in a fault.



<P>&lt;The grievousness of their imprisonment>&nbsp; The Giant, therefore,



<P>drove them before him, and put them into his castle,



<P>into a very dark dungeon, nasty and stinking to the spirits of these



<P>two men.&nbsp; [Ps. 88:18]&nbsp; Here, then, they lay from Wednesday

morning



<P>till Saturday night, without one bit of bread, or drop of drink,



<P>or light, or any to ask how they did; they were, therefore,



<P>here in evil case, and were far from friends and acquaintance.



<P>Now in this place Christian had double sorrow, because it was through



<P>his unadvised counsel that they were brought into this distress.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The pilgrims now, to gratify the flesh,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Will seek its ease; but oh! how they afresh



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Do thereby plunge themselves new griefs into!



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Who seek to please the flesh, themselves undo.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{284}



<P>&lt;On Thursday, Giant Despair beats his prisoners>



<P>Now, Giant Despair had a wife, and her name was Diffidence.



<P>So when he was gone to bed, he told his wife what he had done; to wit,



<P>that he had taken a couple of prisoners and cast them into his dungeon,



<P>for trespassing on his grounds.&nbsp; Then he asked her also what he



<P>had best to do further to them.&nbsp; So she asked him what they were,



<P>whence they came, and whither they were bound; and he told her.



<P>Then she counselled him that when he arose in the morning



<P>he should beat them without any mercy.&nbsp; So, when he arose,



<P>he getteth him a grievous crab-tree cudgel, and goes down into



<P>the dungeon to them, and there first falls to rating of them



<P>as if they were dogs, although they never gave him a word of distaste.



<P>Then he falls upon them, and beats them fearfully, in such sort that



<P>they were not able to help themselves, or to turn them upon the floor.



<P>This done, he withdraws and leaves them there to condole their misery



<P>and to mourn under their distress.&nbsp; So all that day they spent

the time



<P>in nothing but sighs and bitter lamentations.&nbsp; The next night,

she,



<P>talking with her husband about them further, and understanding they

were



<P>yet alive, did advise him to counsel them to make away themselves.



<P>&lt;On Friday, Giant Despair counsels them to kill themselves>



<P>So when morning was come, he goes to them in a surly manner as before,



<P>and perceiving them to be very sore with the stripes that he had



<P>given them the day before, he told them, that since they were



<P>never like to come out of that place, their only way would be forthwith



<P>to make an end of themselves, either with knife, halter, or poison,



<P>for why, said he, should you choose life, seeing it is attended



<P>with so much bitterness?&nbsp; But they desired him to let them go.



<P>&lt;The giant sometimes has fits>&nbsp; With that he looked ugly upon

them,



<P>and, rushing to them, had doubtless made an end of them himself,



<P>but that he fell into one of his fits, (for he sometimes,



<P>in sunshiny weather, fell into fits), and lost for a time the use



<P>of his hand; wherefore he withdrew, and left them as before, to consider



<P>what to do.&nbsp; Then did the prisoners consult between themselves



<P>whether it was best to take his counsel or no; and thus they began



<P>to discourse:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{285}



<P>&lt;Christian crushed>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Brother, said Christian, what shall we do?&nbsp; The life

that



<P>we now live is miserable.&nbsp; For my part I know not whether is best,



<P>to live thus, or to die out of hand.&nbsp; "My soul chooseth strangling



<P>rather than life", and the grave is more easy for me than this dungeon.



<P>[Job 7:15]&nbsp; Shall we be ruled by the Giant?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{286}



<P>&lt;Hopeful comforts him>



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Indeed, our present condition is dreadful, and death would

be



<P>far more welcome to me than thus for ever to abide; but yet,



<P>let us consider, the Lord of the country to which we are going



<P>hath said, Thou shalt do no murder:&nbsp; no, not to another man's person;



<P>much more, then, are we forbidden to take his counsel to kill ourselves.



<P>Besides, he that kills another, can but commit murder upon his body;



<P>but for one to kill himself is to kill body and soul at once.



<P>And, moreover, my brother, thou talkest of ease in the grave;



<P>but hast thou forgotten the hell, for certain the murderers go?



<P>"For no murderer hath eternal life," &amp;c.&nbsp; And let us consider,

again,



<P>that all the law is not in the hand of Giant Despair.&nbsp; Others,



<P>so far as I can understand, have been taken by him, as well as we;



<P>and yet have escaped out of his hand.&nbsp; Who knows, but the God



<P>that made the world may cause that Giant Despair may die? or that,



<P>at some time or other, he may forget to lock us in? or that he may,



<P>in a short time, have another of his fits before us, and may lose



<P>the use of his limbs? and if ever that should come to pass again,



<P>for my part, I am resolved to pluck up the heart of a man,



<P>and to try my utmost to get from under his hand.&nbsp; I was a fool



<P>that I did not try to do it before; but, however, my brother,



<P>let us be patient, and endure a while.&nbsp; The time may come



<P>that may give us a happy release; but let us not be our own murderers.



<P>With these words Hopeful at present did moderate the mind



<P>of his brother; so they continued together (in the dark) that day,



<P>in their sad and doleful condition.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{287}



<P>Well, towards evening, the Giant goes down into the dungeon again,



<P>to see if his prisoners had taken his counsel; but when he came there



<P>he found them alive; and truly, alive was all; for now,



<P>what for want of bread and water, and by reason of the wounds



<P>they received when he beat them, they could do little but breathe.



<P>But, I say, he found them alive; at which he fell into a grievous rage,



<P>and told them that, seeing they had disobeyed his counsel,



<P>it should be worse with them than if they had never been born.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{288}



<P>&lt;Christian still dejected>



<P>At this they trembled greatly, and I think that Christian



<P>fell into a swoon; but, coming a little to himself again,



<P>they renewed their discourse about the Giant's counsel; and whether

yet



<P>they had best to take it or no.&nbsp; Now Christian again seemed to

be



<P>for doing it, but Hopeful made his second reply as followeth:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{289}



<P>&lt;Hopeful comforts him again, by calling former things to remembrance>



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; My brother, said he, rememberest thou not how valiant



<P>thou hast been heretofore?&nbsp; Apollyon could not crush thee,



<P>nor could all that thou didst hear, or see, or feel,



<P>in the Valley of the Shadow of Death.&nbsp; What hardship, terror,



<P>and amazement hast thou already gone through!&nbsp; And art thou now



<P>nothing but fear!&nbsp; Thou seest that I am in the dungeon with thee,



<P>a far weaker man by nature than thou art; also, this Giant has wounded



<P>me as well as thee, and hath also cut off the bread and water



<P>from my mouth; and with thee I mourn without the light.



<P>But let us exercise a little more patience; remember how thou



<P>playedst the man at Vanity Fair, and wast neither afraid of the chain,



<P>nor cage, nor yet of bloody death.&nbsp; Wherefore let us (at least



<P>to avoid the shame, that becomes not a Christian to be found in)



<P>bear up with patience as well as we can.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{290}



<P>Now, night being come again, and the Giant and his wife being in bed,



<P>she asked him concerning the prisoners, and if they had taken



<P>his counsel.&nbsp; To which he replied, They are sturdy rogues,



<P>they choose rather to bear all hardship, than to make away themselves.



<P>Then said she, Take them into the castle-yard to-morrow, and show them



<P>the bones and skulls of those that thou hast already despatched,



<P>and make them believe, ere a week comes to an end, thou also wilt



<P>tear them in pieces, as thou hast done their fellows before them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{291}



<P>&lt;On Saturday, the Giant threatened that shortly he would



<P>pull them in pieces>



<P>So when the morning was come, the Giant goes to them again,



<P>and takes them into the castle-yard, and shows them, as his wife



<P>had bidden him.&nbsp; These, said he, were pilgrims as you are, once,



<P>and they trespassed in my grounds, as you have done;



<P>and when I thought fit, I tore them in pieces, and so, within ten days,



<P>I will do you. Go, get you down to your den again; and with that



<P>he beat them all the way thither.&nbsp; They lay, therefore,



<P>all day on Saturday in a lamentable case, as before.&nbsp; Now,



<P>when night was come, and when Mrs. Diffidence and her husband,



<P>the Giant, were got to bed, they began to renew their discourse



<P>of their prisoners; and withal the old Giant wondered,



<P>that he could neither by his blows nor his counsel bring them to an

end.



<P>And with that his wife replied, I fear, said she, that they live in

hope



<P>that some will come to relieve them, or that they have picklocks



<P>about them, by the means of which they hope to escape.



<P>And sayest thou so, my dear? said the Giant; I will, therefore,



<P>search them in the morning.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{292}



<P>Well, on Saturday, about midnight, they began to pray,



<P>and continued in prayer till almost break of day.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;A key in Christian's bosom, called Promise, opens any lock



<P>in Doubting Castle>



<P>Now, a little before it was day, good Christian, as one half amazed,



<P>brake out in this passionate speech:--&nbsp; What a fool, quoth he,

am I,



<P>thus to lie in a stinking dungeon, when I may as well walk at liberty!



<P>I have a key in my bosom, called Promise, that will, I am persuaded,



<P>open any lock in Doubting Castle.&nbsp; Then said Hopeful,



<P>That is good news, good brother; pluck it out of thy bosom, and try.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{293}



<P>Then Christian pulled it out of his bosom, and began to try



<P>at the dungeon door, whose bolt (as he turned the key) gave back,



<P>and the door flew open with ease, and Christian and Hopeful



<P>both came out.&nbsp; Then he went to the outward door that leads into



<P>the castle-yard, and, with his key, opened that door also.



<P>After, he went to the iron gate, for that must be opened too;



<P>but that lock went damnable hard, yet the key did open it.



<P>Then they thrust open the gate to make their escape with speed,



<P>but that gate, as it opened, made such a creaking, that it waked



<P>Giant Despair, who, hastily rising to pursue his prisoners,



<P>felt his limbs to fail, for his fits took him again,



<P>so that he could by no means go after them.&nbsp; Then they went on,



<P>and came to the King's highway, and so were safe, because they were



<P>out of his jurisdiction.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{294}



<P>&lt;A pillar erected by Christian and his fellow>



<P>Now, when they were over the stile, they began to contrive



<P>with themselves what they should do at that stile to prevent those



<P>that should come after from falling into the hands of Giant Despair.



<P>So they consented to erect there a pillar, and to engrave upon



<P>the side thereof this sentence--"Over this stile is the way



<P>to Doubting Castle, which is kept by Giant Despair,



<P>who despiseth the King of the Celestial Country, and seeks to destroy



<P>his holy pilgrims."&nbsp; Many, therefore, that followed after



<P>read what was written, and escaped the danger.&nbsp; This done,



<P>they sang as follows:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Out of the way we went, and then we found



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; What 'twas to tread upon forbidden ground;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And let them that come after have a care,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lest heedlessness makes them, as we, to fare.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Lest they for trespassing his prisoners are,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Whose castle's Doubting, and whose name's Despair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{295}



<P>&lt;The Delectable Mountains>



<P>They went then till they came to the Delectable Mountains,



<P>which mountains belong to the Lord of that hill of which



<P>we have spoken before; so they went up to the mountains,



<P>to behold the gardens and orchards, the vineyards and fountains



<P>of water; where also they drank and washed themselves,



<P>and did freely eat of the vineyards.&nbsp; &lt;They are refreshed



<P>in the mountains>&nbsp; Now there were on the tops of these mountains



<P>Shepherds feeding their flocks, and they stood by the highway side.



<P>&lt;Talk with the Shepherds>&nbsp; The Pilgrims therefore went to them,



<P>and leaning upon their staves, (as is common with weary pilgrims



<P>when they stand to talk with any by the way), they asked,



<P>Whose Delectable Mountains are these?&nbsp; And whose be the sheep



<P>that feed upon them?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mountains delectable they now ascend,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Where Shepherds be, which to them do commend



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Alluring things, and things that cautious are,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Pilgrims are steady kept by faith and fear.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{296}



<P>SHEP.&nbsp; These mountains are Immanuel's Land, and they are within

sight



<P>of his city; and the sheep also are his, and he laid down his life



<P>for them.&nbsp; [John 10:11]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Is this the way to the Celestial City?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>SHEP.&nbsp; You are just in your way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; How far is it thither?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>SHEP.&nbsp; Too far for any but those that shall get thither indeed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Is the way safe or dangerous?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>SHEP.&nbsp; Safe for those for whom it is to be safe; but the transgressors



<P>shall fall therein.&nbsp; [Hos. 14:9]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Is there, in this place, any relief for pilgrims



<P>that are weary and faint in the way?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>SHEP.&nbsp; The Lord of these mountains hath given us a charge not to

be



<P>forgetful to entertain strangers, therefore the good of the place



<P>is before you.&nbsp; [Heb. 13:1-2]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{297}



<P>&lt;The Shepherds welcome them>



<P>I saw also in my dream, that when the Shepherds perceived



<P>that they were wayfaring men, they also put questions to them,



<P>to which they made answer as in other places; as, Whence came you?



<P>and, How got you into the way? and, By what means have you



<P>so persevered therein?&nbsp; For but few of them that begin to come

hither



<P>do show their face on these mountains.&nbsp; But when the Shepherds

heard



<P>their answers, being pleased therewith, they looked very lovingly



<P>upon them, and said, Welcome to the Delectable Mountains.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{298}



<P>&lt;The names of the Shepherds>



<P>The Shepherds, I say, whose names were Knowledge, Experience, Watchful,



<P>and Sincere, took them by the hand, and had them to their tents,



<P>and made them partake of that which was ready at present.



<P>They said, moreover, We would that ye should stay here awhile,



<P>to be acquainted with us; and yet more to solace yourselves



<P>with the good of these Delectable Mountains.&nbsp; They then told them,



<P>that they were content to stay; so they went to their rest that night,



<P>because it was very late.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{299}



<P>&lt;They are shown wonders>



<P>Then I saw in my dream, that in the morning the Shepherds called up



<P>to Christian and Hopeful to walk with them upon the mountains;



<P>so they went forth with them, and walked a while,



<P>having a pleasant prospect on every side.&nbsp; Then said the Shepherds



<P>one to another, Shall we show these pilgrims some wonders?



<P>&lt;The Mountain of Error>&nbsp; So when they had concluded to do it,



<P>they had them first to the top of a hill called Error,



<P>which was very steep on the furthest side, and bid them look down



<P>to the bottom.&nbsp; So Christian and Hopeful looked down,



<P>and saw at the bottom several men dashed all to pieces by a fall



<P>that they had from the top.&nbsp; Then said Christian, What meaneth

this?



<P>The Shepherds answered, Have you not heard of them that were made to

err



<P>by hearkening to Hymeneus and Philetus as concerning the faith



<P>of the resurrection of the body?&nbsp; [2 Tim. 2:17,18]&nbsp; They answered,

Yes.



<P>Then said the Shepherds, Those that you see lie dashed in pieces



<P>at the bottom of this mountain are they; and they have continued



<P>to this day unburied, as you see, for an example to others



<P>to take heed how they clamber too high, or how they come too near



<P>the brink of this mountain.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{300}



<P>&lt;Mount Caution>



<P>Then I saw that they had them to the top of another mountain,



<P>and the name of that is Caution, and bid them look afar off;



<P>which, when they did, they perceived, as they thought,



<P>several men walking up and down among the tombs that were there;



<P>and they perceived that the men were blind, because they



<P>stumbled sometimes upon the tombs, and because they could not get out



<P>from among them.&nbsp; Then said Christian, What means this?



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>author's apology</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>



<UL>

<UL>

<UL><B><I><FONT SIZE=+3>The Pilgrim's Progress</FONT></I></B>



<P>&nbsp; <B><FONT SIZE=+2>by John Bunyan</FONT></B></UL>

</UL>

</UL>



<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P><FONT SIZE=+1>THE PILGRIM'S PROGRESS From This World To That Which Is

To Come</FONT>

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>DELIVERED UNDER THE SIMILITUDE OF A DREAM BY JOHN BUNYAN

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <B>The Author's Apology

for his Book</B>

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{1}



<P>When at the first I took my pen in hand



<P>Thus for to write, I did not understand



<P>That I at all should make a little book



<P>In such a mode; nay, I had undertook



<P>To make another; which, when almost done,



<P>Before I was aware, I this begun.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And thus it was:&nbsp; I, writing of the way



<P>And race of saints, in this our gospel day,



<P>Fell suddenly into an allegory



<P>About their journey, and the way to glory,



<P>In more than twenty things which I set down.



<P>This done, I twenty more had in my crown;



<P>And they again began to multiply,



<P>Like sparks that from the coals of fire do fly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nay, then, thought I, if that you breed so fast,



<P>I'll put you by yourselves, lest you at last



<P>Should prove ad infinitum, and eat out



<P>The book that I already am about.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Well, so I did; but yet I did not think



<P>To shew to all the world my pen and ink



<P>In such a mode; I only thought to make



<P>I knew not what; nor did I undertake



<P>Thereby to please my neighbour:&nbsp; no, not I;



<P>I did it my own self to gratify.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{2}



<P>Neither did I but vacant seasons spend



<P>In this my scribble; nor did I intend



<P>But to divert myself in doing this



<P>From worser thoughts which make me do amiss.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Thus, I set pen to paper with delight,



<P>And quickly had my thoughts in black and white.



<P>For, having now my method by the end,



<P>Still as I pulled, it came; and so I penned



<P>It down:&nbsp; until it came at last to be,



<P>For length and breadth, the bigness which you see.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Well, when I had thus put mine ends together,



<P>I shewed them others, that I might see whether



<P>They would condemn them, or them justify:



<P>And some said, Let them live; some, Let them die;



<P>Some said, JOHN, print it; others said, Not so;



<P>Some said, It might do good; others said, No.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Now was I in a strait, and did not see



<P>Which was the best thing to be done by me:



<P>At last I thought, Since you are thus divided,



<P>I print it will, and so the case decided.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{3}



<P>For, thought I, some, I see, would have it done,



<P>Though others in that channel do not run:



<P>To prove, then, who advised for the best,



<P>Thus I thought fit to put it to the test.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I further thought, if now I did deny



<P>Those that would have it, thus to gratify.



<P>I did not know but hinder them I might



<P>Of that which would to them be great delight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For those which were not for its coming forth,



<P>I said to them, Offend you I am loth,



<P>Yet, since your brethren pleased with it be,



<P>Forbear to judge till you do further see.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>If that thou wilt not read, let it alone;



<P>Some love the meat, some love to pick the bone.



<P>Yea, that I might them better palliate,



<P>I did too with them thus expostulate:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{4}



<P>May I not write in such a style as this?



<P>In such a method, too, and yet not miss



<P>My end--thy good?&nbsp; Why may it not be done?



<P>Dark clouds bring waters, when the bright bring none.



<P>Yea, dark or bright, if they their silver drops



<P>Cause to descend, the earth, by yielding crops,



<P>Gives praise to both, and carpeth not at either,



<P>But treasures up the fruit they yield together;



<P>Yea, so commixes both, that in her fruit



<P>None can distinguish this from that:&nbsp; they suit



<P>Her well when hungry; but, if she be full,



<P>She spews out both, and makes their blessings null.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>You see the ways the fisherman doth take



<P>To catch the fish; what engines doth he make?



<P>Behold how he engageth all his wits;



<P>Also his snares, lines, angles, hooks, and nets;



<P>Yet fish there be, that neither hook, nor line,



<P>Nor snare, nor net, nor engine can make thine:



<P>They must be groped for, and be tickled too,



<P>Or they will not be catch'd, whate'er you do.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>How does the fowler seek to catch his game



<P>By divers means! all which one cannot name:



<P>His guns, his nets, his lime-twigs, light, and bell:



<P>He creeps, he goes, he stands; yea, who can tell



<P>Of all his postures?&nbsp; Yet there's none of these



<P>Will make him master of what fowls he please.



<P>Yea, he must pipe and whistle to catch this,



<P>Yet, if he does so, that bird he will miss.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>If that a pearl may in a toad's head dwell,



<P>And may be found too in an oyster-shell;



<P>If things that promise nothing do contain



<P>What better is than gold; who will disdain,



<P>That have an inkling of it, there to look,



<P>That they may find it?&nbsp; Now, my little book,



<P>(Though void of all these paintings that may make



<P>It with this or the other man to take)



<P>Is not without those things that do excel



<P>What do in brave but empty notions dwell.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{5}



<P>`Well, yet I am not fully satisfied,



<P>That this your book will stand, when soundly tried.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Why, what's the matter?&nbsp; `It is dark.'&nbsp; What though?



<P>`But it is feigned.'&nbsp; What of that?&nbsp; I trow?



<P>Some men, by feigned words, as dark as mine,



<P>Make truth to spangle and its rays to shine.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>`But they want solidness.'&nbsp; Speak, man, thy mind.



<P>`They drown the weak; metaphors make us blind.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Solidity, indeed, becomes the pen



<P>Of him that writeth things divine to men;



<P>But must I needs want solidness, because



<P>By metaphors I speak?&nbsp; Were not God's laws,



<P>His gospel laws, in olden times held forth



<P>By types, shadows, and metaphors?&nbsp; Yet loth



<P>Will any sober man be to find fault



<P>With them, lest he be found for to assault



<P>The highest wisdom.&nbsp; No, he rather stoops,



<P>And seeks to find out what by pins and loops,



<P>By calves and sheep, by heifers and by rams,



<P>By birds and herbs, and by the blood of lambs,



<P>God speaketh to him; and happy is he



<P>That finds the light and grace that in them be.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{6}



<P>Be not too forward, therefore, to conclude



<P>That I want solidness--that I am rude;



<P>All things solid in show not solid be;



<P>All things in parables despise not we;



<P>Lest things most hurtful lightly we receive,



<P>And things that good are, of our souls bereave.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>My dark and cloudy words, they do but hold



<P>The truth, as cabinets enclose the gold.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The prophets used much by metaphors



<P>To set forth truth; yea, who so considers



<P>Christ, his apostles too, shall plainly see,



<P>That truths to this day in such mantles be.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Am I afraid to say, that holy writ,



<P>Which for its style and phrase puts down all wit,



<P>Is everywhere so full of all these things--



<P>Dark figures, allegories?&nbsp; Yet there springs



<P>From that same book that lustre, and those rays



<P>Of light, that turn our darkest nights to days.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{7}



<P>Come, let my carper to his life now look,



<P>And find there darker lines than in my book



<P>He findeth any; yea, and let him know,



<P>That in his best things there are worse lines too.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>May we but stand before impartial men,



<P>To his poor one I dare adventure ten,



<P>That they will take my meaning in these lines



<P>Far better than his lies in silver shrines.



<P>Come, truth, although in swaddling clouts, I find,



<P>Informs the judgement, rectifies the mind;



<P>Pleases the understanding, makes the will



<P>Submit; the memory too it doth fill



<P>With what doth our imaginations please;



<P>Likewise it tends our troubles to appease.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sound words, I know, Timothy is to use,



<P>And old wives' fables he is to refuse;



<P>But yet grave Paul him nowhere did forbid



<P>The use of parables; in which lay hid



<P>That gold, those pearls, and precious stones that were



<P>Worth digging for, and that with greatest care.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Let me add one word more.&nbsp; O man of God,



<P>Art thou offended?&nbsp; Dost thou wish I had



<P>Put forth my matter in another dress?



<P>Or, that I had in things been more express?



<P>Three things let me propound; then I submit



<P>To those that are my betters, as is fit.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{8}



<P>1.&nbsp; I find not that I am denied the use



<P>Of this my method, so I no abuse



<P>Put on the words, things, readers; or be rude



<P>In handling figure or similitude,



<P>In application; but, all that I may,



<P>Seek the advance of truth this or that way



<P>Denied, did I say?&nbsp; Nay, I have leave



<P>(Example too, and that from them that have



<P>God better pleased, by their words or ways,



<P>Than any man that breatheth now-a-days)



<P>Thus to express my mind, thus to declare



<P>Things unto thee that excellentest are.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>2.&nbsp; I find that men (as high as trees) will write



<P>Dialogue-wise; yet no man doth them slight



<P>For writing so:&nbsp; indeed, if they abuse



<P>Truth, cursed be they, and the craft they use



<P>To that intent; but yet let truth be free



<P>To make her sallies upon thee and me,



<P>Which way it pleases God; for who knows how,



<P>Better than he that taught us first to plough,



<P>To guide our mind and pens for his design?



<P>And he makes base things usher in divine.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>3.&nbsp; I find that holy writ in many places



<P>Hath semblance with this method, where the cases



<P>Do call for one thing, to set forth another;



<P>Use it I may, then, and yet nothing smother



<P>Truth's golden beams:&nbsp; nay, by this method may



<P>Make it cast forth its rays as light as day.



<P>And now before I do put up my pen,



<P>I'll shew the profit of my book, and then



<P>Commit both thee and it unto that Hand



<P>That pulls the strong down, and makes weak ones stand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This book it chalketh out before thine eyes



<P>The man that seeks the everlasting prize;



<P>It shews you whence he comes, whither he goes;



<P>What he leaves undone, also what he does;



<P>It also shows you how he runs and runs,



<P>Till he unto the gate of glory comes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{9}



<P>It shows, too, who set out for life amain,



<P>As if the lasting crown they would obtain;



<P>Here also you may see the reason why



<P>They lose their labour, and like fools do die.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This book will make a traveller of thee,



<P>If by its counsel thou wilt ruled be;



<P>It will direct thee to the Holy Land,



<P>If thou wilt its directions understand:



<P>Yea, it will make the slothful active be;



<P>The blind also delightful things to see.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Art thou for something rare and profitable?



<P>Wouldest thou see a truth within a fable?



<P>Art thou forgetful?&nbsp; Wouldest thou remember



<P>From New-Year's day to the last of December?



<P>Then read my fancies; they will stick like burs,



<P>And may be, to the helpless, comforters.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This book is writ in such a dialect



<P>As may the minds of listless men affect:



<P>It seems a novelty, and yet contains



<P>Nothing but sound and honest gospel strains.



<P>Wouldst thou divert thyself from melancholy?



<P>Wouldst thou be pleasant, yet be far from folly?



<P>Wouldst thou read riddles, and their explanation?



<P>Or else be drowned in thy contemplation?



<P>Dost thou love picking meat?&nbsp; Or wouldst thou see



<P>A man in the clouds, and hear him speak to thee?



<P>Wouldst thou be in a dream, and yet not sleep?



<P>Or wouldst thou in a moment laugh and weep?



<P>Wouldest thou lose thyself and catch no harm,



<P>And find thyself again without a charm?



<P>Wouldst read thyself, and read thou knowest not what,



<P>And yet know whether thou art blest or not,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>By reading the same lines?&nbsp; Oh, then come hither,



<P>And lay my book, thy head, and heart together.

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

JOHN BUNYAN.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>p301--p350</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B><FONT SIZE=+2></FONT></B>



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Page301--Page350</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>{301}



<P>The Shepherds then answered, Did you not see a little below



<P>these mountains a stile, that led into a meadow, on the left hand



<P>of this way?&nbsp; They answered, Yes.&nbsp; Then said the Shepherds,



<P>From that stile there goes a path that leads directly



<P>to Doubting Castle, which is kept by Giant Despair, and these,



<P>pointing to them among the tombs, came once on pilgrimage,



<P>as you do now, even till they came to that same stile;



<P>and because the right way was rough in that place, they chose to go



<P>out of it into that meadow, and there were taken by Giant Despair,



<P>and cast into Doubting Castle; where, after they had been a while



<P>kept in the dungeon, he at last did put out their eyes,



<P>and led them among those tombs, where he has left them to wander



<P>to this very day, that the saying of the wise man might be fulfilled,



<P>"He that wandereth out of the way of understanding, shall remain in



<P>the congregation of the dead."&nbsp; [Pro. 21:16]&nbsp; Then Christian

and Hopeful



<P>looked upon one another, with tears gushing out, but yet said nothing



<P>to the Shepherds.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{302}



<P>&lt;A by-way to hell>



<P>Then I saw in my dream, that the Shepherds had them to another place,



<P>in a bottom, where was a door in the side of a hill,



<P>and they opened the door, and bid them look in.&nbsp; They looked in,



<P>therefore, and saw that within it was very dark and smoky;



<P>they also thought that they heard there a rumbling noise as of fire,



<P>and a cry of some tormented, and that they smelt the scent of brimstone.



<P>Then said Christian, What means this?&nbsp; The Shepherds told them,



<P>This is a by-way to hell, a way that hypocrites go in at; namely,



<P>such as sell their birthright, with Esau; such as sell their master,



<P>with Judas; such as blaspheme the gospel, with Alexander;



<P>and that lie and dissemble, with Ananias and Sapphira his wife.



<P>Then said Hopeful to the Shepherds, I perceive that these had on them,



<P>even every one, a show of pilgrimage, as we have now; had they not?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{303}



<P>SHEP.&nbsp; Yes, and held it a long time too.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; How far might they go on in pilgrimage in their day,



<P>since they notwithstanding were thus miserably cast away?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>SHEP.&nbsp; Some further, and some not so far, as these mountains.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then said the Pilgrims one to another, We have need to cry to the Strong



<P>for strength.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>SHEP.&nbsp; Ay, and you will have need to use it, when you have it,

too.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{304}



<P>&lt;The Shepherds' perspective glass>



<P>By this time the Pilgrims had a desire to go forward,



<P>and the Shepherds a desire they should; so they walked together



<P>towards the end of the mountains.&nbsp; Then said the Shepherds



<P>one to another, Let us here show to the Pilgrims the gates



<P>of the Celestial City, if they have skill to look through



<P>our perspective glass.&nbsp; &lt;The Hill Clear>&nbsp; The Pilgrims

then



<P>lovingly accepted the motion; so they had them to the top



<P>of a high hill, called Clear, and gave them their glass to look.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{305}



<P>&lt;The fruits of servile fear>



<P>Then they essayed to look, but the remembrance of that last thing



<P>that the Shepherds had shown them, made their hands shake;



<P>by means of which impediment, they could not look steadily



<P>through the glass; yet they thought they saw something like the gate,



<P>and also some of the glory of the place.&nbsp; Then they went away,



<P>and sang this song--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Thus, by the Shepherds, secrets are reveal'd,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Which from all other men are kept conceal'd.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Come to the Shepherds, then, if you would see



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Things deep, things hid, and that mysterious

be.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{306}



<P>&lt;A twofold caution>



<P>When they were about to depart, one of the Shepherds gave them a note



<P>of the way.&nbsp; Another of them bid them beware of the Flatterer.



<P>The third bid them take heed that they sleep not upon



<P>the Enchanted Ground.&nbsp; And the fourth bid them God-speed.



<P>So I awoke from my dream.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{307}



<P>&lt;The Country of Conceit, out of which came Ignorance>



<P>And I slept, and dreamed again, and saw the same two Pilgrims



<P>going down the mountains along the highway towards the city.



<P>Now, a little below these mountains, on the left hand,



<P>lieth the country of Conceit; from which country there comes



<P>into the way in which the Pilgrims walked, a little crooked lane.



<P>Here, therefore, they met with a very brisk lad, that came out



<P>of that country; and his name was Ignorance.&nbsp; So Christian asked

him



<P>from what parts he came, and whither he was going.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{308}



<P>&lt;Christian and Ignorance have some talk>



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; Sir, I was born in the country that lieth off there



<P>a little on the left hand, and I am going to the Celestial City.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; But how do you think to get in at the gate? for you may find



<P>some difficulty there.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; As other people do, said he.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; But what have you to show at that gate, that may cause



<P>that the gate should be opened to you?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;The ground of Ignorance's hope>



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; I know my Lord's will, and I have been a good liver;



<P>I pay every man his own; I pray, fast, pay tithes, and give alms,



<P>and have left my country for whither I am going.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{309}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; But thou camest not in at the wicket-gate that is at the

head



<P>of this way; thou camest in hither through that same crooked lane,



<P>and therefore, I fear, however thou mayest think of thyself,



<P>when the reckoning day shall come, thou wilt have laid to thy charge



<P>that thou art a thief and a robber, instead of getting admittance



<P>into the city.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;He saith to every one that he is a fool>



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; Gentlemen, ye be utter strangers to me, I know you not;



<P>be content and follow the religion of your country, and I will follow



<P>the religion of mine.&nbsp; I hope all will be well.&nbsp; And as for

the gate



<P>that you talk of, all the world knows that that is a great way off



<P>of our country.&nbsp; I cannot think that any man in all our parts



<P>doth so much as know the way to it, nor need they matter



<P>whether they do or no, since we have, as you see, a fine,



<P>pleasant green lane, that comes down from our country,



<P>the next way into the way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{310}



<P>&lt;How to carry it to a fool>



<P>When Christian saw that the man was "wise in his own conceit",



<P>he said to Hopeful, whisperingly, "There is more hope of a fool than



<P>of him."&nbsp; [Prov. 26:12]&nbsp; And said, moreover, "When he that

is a fool



<P>walketh by the way, his wisdom faileth him, and he saith to every one



<P>that he is a fool."&nbsp; [Eccl. 10:3]&nbsp; What, shall we talk further

with him,



<P>or out-go him at present, and so leave him to think of what



<P>he hath heard already, and then stop again for him afterwards,



<P>and see if by degrees we can do any good to him?&nbsp; Then said Hopeful--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Let Ignorance a little while now muse



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; On what is said, and let him not refuse



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Good counsel to embrace, lest he remain



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Still ignorant of what's the chiefest gain.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; God saith, those that no understanding have,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Although he made them, them he will not save.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; He further added, It is not good, I think, to say all to

him



<P>at once; let us pass him by, if you will, and talk to him anon,



<P>even as he is able to bear it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{311}



<P>&lt;The destruction of one Turn-away>



<P>So they both went on, and Ignorance he came after.&nbsp; Now when they

had



<P>passed him a little way, they entered into a very dark lane,



<P>where they met a man whom seven devils had bound with



<P>seven strong cords, and were carrying of him back to the door



<P>that they saw on the side of the hill.&nbsp; [Matt. 12:45, Prov. 5:22]



<P>Now good Christian began to tremble, and so did Hopeful his companion;



<P>yet as the devils led away the man, Christian looked to see



<P>if he knew him; and he thought it might be one Turn-away,



<P>that dwelt in the town of Apostasy.&nbsp; But he did not perfectly see



<P>his face, for he did hang his head like a thief that is found.



<P>But being once past, Hopeful looked after him, and espied on his back



<P>a paper with this inscription, "Wanton professor and damnable apostate".



<P>&lt;Christian telleth his companion a story of Little-faith>



<P>Then said Christian to his fellow, Now I call to remembrance,



<P>that which was told me of a thing that happened to a good man hereabout.



<P>The name of the man was Little-faith, but a good man, and he dwelt



<P>in the town of Sincere.&nbsp; The thing was this:--&nbsp; At the entering

in



<P>at this passage, &lt;Broad-way Gate> there comes down from Broad-way

Gate,



<P>&lt;Dead Man's Lane> a lane called Dead Man's Lane; so called because

of



<P>the murders that are commonly done there; and this Little-faith



<P>going on pilgrimage, as we do now, chanced to sit down there, and slept.



<P>Now there happened, at that time, to come down the lane,



<P>from Broad-way Gate, three sturdy rogues, and their names



<P>were Faint-heart, Mistrust, and Guilt, (three brothers),



<P>and they espying Little-faith, where he was, came galloping up



<P>with speed.&nbsp; Now the good man was just awake from his sleep,



<P>and was getting up to go on his journey.&nbsp; So they came up all to

him,



<P>and with threatening language bid him stand.&nbsp; At this



<P>Little-faith looked as white as a clout, and had neither power to fight



<P>nor fly.&nbsp; &lt;Little-faith robbed by Faint-heart, Mistrust and

Guilt>



<P>Then said Faint-heart, Deliver thy purse.&nbsp; &lt;They got away his

silver,



<P>and knocked him down>&nbsp; But he making no haste to do it



<P>(for he was loath to lose his money), Mistrust ran up to him,



<P>and thrusting his hand into his pocket, pulled out thence



<P>a bag of silver.&nbsp; Then he cried out, Thieves! Thieves!&nbsp; With

that Guilt,



<P>with a great club that was in his hand, struck Little-faith on the head,



<P>and with that blow felled him flat to the ground, where he lay bleeding



<P>as one that would bleed to death.&nbsp; All this while the thieves stood

by.



<P>But, at last, they hearing that some were upon the road,



<P>and fearing lest it should be one Great-grace, that dwells in the city



<P>of Good-confidence, they betook themselves to their heels,



<P>and left this good man to shift for himself.&nbsp; Now, after a while,



<P>Little-faith came to himself, and getting up, made shift



<P>to scrabble on his way.&nbsp; This was the story.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{312}



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; But did they take from him all that ever he had?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Little-faith lost not his best things>



<P>Chr. No; the place where his jewels were they never ransacked,



<P>so those he kept still.&nbsp; But, as I was told, the good man



<P>was much afflicted for his loss, for the thieves got most of



<P>his spending-money.&nbsp; &lt;Little-faith forced to beg to his journey's

end>



<P>That which they got not (as I said) were jewels, also he had



<P>a little odd money left, but scarce enough to bring him to



<P>his journey's end [1 Peter 4:18]; nay, if I was not misinformed,



<P>he was forced to beg as he went, to keep himself alive;



<P>for his jewels he might not sell.&nbsp; But beg, and do what he could,



<P>he went (as we say) with many a hungry belly the most part



<P>of the rest of the way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{313}



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; But is it not a wonder they got not from him his certificate,



<P>by which he was to receive his admittance at the Celestial Gate?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;He kept not his best things by his own cunning.&nbsp; [2 Tim. 1:14]>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; It is a wonder; but they got not that, though they missed

it



<P>not through any good cunning of his; for he, being dismayed with



<P>their coming upon him, had neither power nor skill to hide anything;



<P>so it was more by good Providence than by his endeavour,



<P>that they missed of that good thing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{314}



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; But it must needs be a comfort to him, that they got not



<P>his jewels from him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; It might have been great comfort to him, had he used it



<P>as he should; but they that told me the story said, that he made



<P>but little use of it all the rest of the way, and that because



<P>of the dismay that he had in the taking away his money; indeed,



<P>he forgot it a great part of the rest of his journey; and besides,



<P>when at any time it came into his mind, and he began to be



<P>comforted therewith, then would fresh thoughts of his loss come again



<P>upon him, and those thoughts would swallow up all.&nbsp; [1 Peter 1:9]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{315}



<P>&lt;He is pitied by both>



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Alas! poor man! This could not but be a great grief to him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Grief! ay, a grief indeed. Would it not have been so to any

of us,



<P>had we been used as he, to be robbed, and wounded too,



<P>and that in a strange place, as he was?&nbsp; It is a wonder he did

not



<P>die with grief, poor heart!&nbsp; I was told that he scattered almost



<P>all the rest of the way with nothing but doleful and bitter complaints;



<P>telling also to all that overtook him, or that he overtook in the way



<P>as he went, where he was robbed, and how; who they were that did it,



<P>and what he lost; how he was wounded, and that he hardly escaped



<P>with his life.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{316}



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; But it is a wonder that his necessity did not put him upon



<P>selling or pawning some of his jewels, that he might have wherewith



<P>to relieve himself in his journey.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Christian snubbeth his fellow for unadvised speaking>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Thou talkest like one upon whose head is the shell



<P>to this very day; for what should he pawn them, or to whom



<P>should he sell them?&nbsp; In all that country where he was robbed,



<P>his jewels were not accounted of; nor did he want that relief



<P>which could from thence be administered to him.&nbsp; Besides,



<P>had his jewels been missing at the gate of the Celestial City,



<P>he had (and that he knew well enough) been excluded from



<P>an inheritance there; and that would have been worse to him



<P>than the appearance and villainy of ten thousand thieves.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{317}



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Why art thou so tart, my brother?&nbsp; Esau sold his birthright,



<P>and that for a mess of pottage, and that birthright was



<P>his greatest jewel; and if he, why might not Little-faith do so too?



<P>[Heb. 12:16]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;A discourse about Esau and Little-faith>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Esau did sell his birthright indeed, and so do many besides,



<P>and by so doing exclude themselves from the chief blessing,



<P>as also that caitiff did; but you must put a difference betwixt



<P>Esau and Little-faith, and also betwixt their estates.



<P>&lt;Esau was ruled by his lusts>&nbsp; Esau's birthright was typical,



<P>but Little-faith's jewels were not so; Esau's belly was his god,



<P>but Little-faith's belly was not so; Esau's want lay



<P>in his fleshly appetite, Little-faith's did not so.&nbsp; Besides,



<P>Esau could see no further than to the fulfilling of his lusts;



<P>"Behold, I am at the point to die, (said he), and what profit
