<P>CHR.&nbsp; His name is Talkative; he dwelleth in our town.



<P>I wonder that you should be a stranger to him, only I consider



<P>that our town is large.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Whose son is he?&nbsp; And whereabout does he dwell?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; He is the son of one Say-well; he dwelt in Prating Row;



<P>and is known of all that are acquainted with him, by the name of



<P>Talkative in Prating Row; and notwithstanding his fine tongue,



<P>he is but a sorry fellow.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{191}



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Well, he seems to be a very pretty man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; That is, to them who have not thorough acquaintance with

him;



<P>for he is best abroad; near home, he is ugly enough.



<P>Your saying that he is a pretty man, brings to my mind



<P>what I have observed in the work of the painter, whose pictures



<P>show best at a distance, but, very near, more unpleasing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{192}



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; But I am ready to think you do but jest, because you smiled.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; God forbid that I should jest (although I smiled) in this

matter,



<P>or that I should accuse any falsely!&nbsp; I will give you



<P>a further discovery of him.&nbsp; This man is for any company,



<P>and for any talk; as he talketh now with you, so will he talk



<P>when he is on the ale-bench; and the more drink he hath in his crown,



<P>the more of these things he hath in his mouth; religion hath no place



<P>in his heart, or house, or conversation; all he hath



<P>lieth in his tongue, and his religion is, to make a noise therewith.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{193}



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Say you so! then am I in this man greatly deceived.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Talkative talks, but does not>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Deceived! you may be sure of it; remember the proverb,



<P>"They say and do not."&nbsp; [Matt. 23:3]&nbsp; But the kingdom of God



<P>is not in word, but in Power.&nbsp; [1 Cor 4:20]&nbsp; He talketh of

prayer,



<P>of repentance, of faith, and of the new birth; but he knows but only



<P>to talk of them.&nbsp; I have been in his family, and have observed

him



<P>both at home and abroad; and I know what I say of him is the truth.



<P>&lt;His house is empty of religion>&nbsp; His house is as empty of religion



<P>as the white of an egg is of savour.&nbsp; There is there neither prayer



<P>nor sign of repentance for sin; yea, the brute in his kind serves God



<P>far better than he.&nbsp; &lt;He is a stain to religion>&nbsp; He is

the very stain,



<P>reproach, and shame of religion, to all that know him; it can hardly



<P>have a good word in all that end of the town where he dwells,



<P>through him.&nbsp; [Rom. 2:24,25]&nbsp; &lt;The Proverb that goes of

him>



<P>Thus say the common people that know him, A saint abroad,



<P>and a devil at home.&nbsp; His poor family finds it so; he is such a

churl,



<P>such a railer at and so unreasonable with his servants,



<P>that they neither know how to do for or speak to him.



<P>&lt;Men shun to deal with him>&nbsp; Men that have any dealings with

him say



<P>it is better to deal with a Turk than with him; for fairer dealing



<P>they shall have at their hands.&nbsp; This Talkative (if it be possible)



<P>will go beyond them, defraud, beguile, and overreach them.&nbsp; Besides,



<P>he brings up his sons to follow his steps; and if he findeth



<P>in any of them a foolish timorousness, (for so he calls



<P>the first appearance of a tender conscience,) he calls them



<P>fools and blockheads, and by no means will employ them in much,



<P>or speak to their commendations before others.&nbsp; For my part,



<P>I am of opinion, that he has, by his wicked life, caused many



<P>to stumble and fall; and will be, if God prevent not,



<P>the ruin of many more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{194}



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Well, my brother, I am bound to believe you; not only because



<P>you say you know him, but also because, like a Christian,



<P>you make your reports of men.&nbsp; For I cannot think that you



<P>speak these things of ill-will, but because it is even so as you say.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Had I known him no more than you, I might perhaps



<P>have thought of him, as, at the first, you did; yea, had he received



<P>this report at their hands only that are enemies to religion,



<P>I should have thought it had been a slander,--a lot that often falls



<P>from bad men's mouths upon good men's names and professions;



<P>but all these things, yea, and a great many more as bad,



<P>of my own knowledge, I can prove him guilty of.&nbsp; Besides,



<P>good men are ashamed of him; they can neither call him brother,



<P>nor friend; the very naming of him among them makes them blush,



<P>if they know him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{195}



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Well, I see that saying and doing are two things,



<P>and hereafter I shall better observe this distinction.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;The carcass of religion>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; They are two things, indeed, and are as diverse as are



<P>the soul and the body; for as the body without the soul is but



<P>a dead carcass, so saying, if it be alone, is but a dead carcass also.



<P>The soul of religion is the practical part:&nbsp; "Pure religion



<P>and undefiled, before God and the Father, is this, To visit



<P>the fatherless and widows in their affliction, and to keep himself



<P>unspotted from the world."&nbsp; [James 1:27; see vv. 22-26]



<P>This Talkative is not aware of; he thinks that hearing and saying



<P>will make a good Christian, and thus he deceiveth his own soul.



<P>Hearing is but as the sowing of the seed; talking is not sufficient



<P>to prove that fruit is indeed in the heart and life; and let us



<P>assure ourselves, that at the day of doom men shall be judged



<P>according to their fruits.&nbsp; [Matt. 13, 25]&nbsp; It will not be

said then,



<P>Did you believe? but, Were you doers, or talkers only?



<P>and accordingly shall they be judged.&nbsp; The end of the world



<P>is compared to our harvest; and you know men at harvest



<P>regard nothing but fruit.&nbsp; Not that anything can be accepted



<P>that is not of faith, but I speak this to show you how insignificant



<P>the profession of Talkative will be at that day.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{196}



<P>&lt;Faithful convinced of the badness of Talkative>



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; This brings to my mind that of Moses, by which he describeth



<P>the beast that is clean.&nbsp; [Lev. 11:3-7; Deut. 14:6-8]&nbsp; He

is such a one



<P>that parteth the hoof and cheweth the cud; not that parteth



<P>the hoof only, or that cheweth the cud only.&nbsp; The hare cheweth

the cud,



<P>but yet is unclean, because he parteth not the hoof.



<P>And this truly resembleth Talkative; he cheweth the cud,



<P>he seeketh knowledge, he cheweth upon the word; but he divideth not



<P>the hoof, he parteth not with the way of sinners; but, as the hare,



<P>he retaineth the foot of a dog or bear, and therefore he is unclean.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Talkative like to things that sound without life>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; You have spoken, for aught I know, the true gospel sense



<P>of those texts.&nbsp; And I will add another thing:&nbsp; Paul calleth

some men,



<P>yea, and those great talkers, too, sounding brass and tinkling cymbals;



<P>that is, as he expounds them in another place, things without life,



<P>giving sound.&nbsp; [1 Cor. 13:1-3; 14:7]&nbsp; Things without life,

that is,



<P>without the true faith and grace of the gospel; and consequently,



<P>things that shall never be placed in the kingdom of heaven



<P>among those that are the children of life; though their sound,



<P>by their talk, be as if it were the tongue or voice of an angel.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Well, I was not so fond of his company at first,



<P>but I am as sick of it now.&nbsp; What shall we do to be rid of him?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Take my advice, and do as I bid you, and you shall find



<P>that he will soon be sick of your company too, except God



<P>shall touch his heart, and turn it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; What would you have me to do?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Why, go to him, and enter into some serious discourse



<P>about the power of religion; and ask him plainly



<P>(when he has approved of it, for that he will) whether this thing



<P>be set up in his heart, house, or conversation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{197}



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Then Faithful stepped forward again, and said to Talkative,



<P>Come, what cheer?&nbsp; How is it now?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>TALK.&nbsp; Thank you, well.&nbsp; I thought we should have had



<P>a great deal of talk by this time.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{198}



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Well, if you will, we will fall to it now; and since



<P>you left it with me to state the question, let it be this:



<P>How doth the saving grace of God discover itself when it is in



<P>the heart of man?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Talkative's false discovery of a work of grace>



<P>TALK.&nbsp; I perceive, then, that our talk must be about the power



<P>of things.&nbsp; Well, it is a very good question, and I shall be willing



<P>to answer you.&nbsp; And take my answer in brief, thus:&nbsp; First,



<P>Where the grace of God is in the heart, it causeth there



<P>a great outcry against sin.&nbsp; Secondly ----

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Nay, hold, let us consider of one at once.&nbsp; I think

you should



<P>rather say, It shows itself by inclining the soul to abhor its sin.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>TALK.&nbsp; Why, what difference is there between crying out against,



<P>and abhorring of sin?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{199}



<P>&lt;To cry out against sin, no sign of grace>



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Oh, a great deal.&nbsp; A man may cry out against sin of

policy,



<P>but he cannot abhor it, but by virtue of a godly antipathy against it.



<P>I have heard many cry out against sin in the pulpit,



<P>who yet can abide it well enough in the heart, house, and conversation.



<P>Joseph's mistress cried out with a loud voice, as if she had been



<P>very holy; but she would willingly, notwithstanding that,



<P>have committed uncleanness with him.&nbsp; Some cry out against sin



<P>even as the mother cries out against her child in her lap,



<P>when she calleth it slut and naughty girl, and then falls



<P>to hugging and kissing it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>TALK.&nbsp; You lie at the catch, I perceive.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{200}



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; No, not I; I am only for setting things right.



<P>But what is the second thing whereby you would prove a discovery



<P>of a work of grace in the heart?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>TALK.&nbsp; Great knowledge of gospel mysteries.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Great knowledge no sign of grace>



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; This sign should have been first; but first or last,



<P>it is also false; for knowledge, great knowledge, may be obtained



<P>in the mysteries of the gospel, and yet no work of grace in the soul.



<P>[1 Cor. 13]&nbsp; Yea, if a man have all knowledge, he may yet be nothing,



<P>and so consequently be no child of God.&nbsp; When Christ said,



<P>"Do you know all these things?" and the disciples had answered, Yes;



<P>he addeth, "Blessed are ye if ye do them."&nbsp; He doth not lay the

blessing



<P>in the knowing of them, but in the doing of them.&nbsp; For there is



<P>a knowledge that is not attended with doing:&nbsp; He that knoweth



<P>his masters will, and doeth it not.&nbsp; A man may know like an angel,



<P>and yet be no Christian, therefore your sign of it is not true.



<P>Indeed, to know is a thing that pleaseth talkers and boasters,



<P>but to do is that which pleaseth God.&nbsp; Not that the heart can be

good



<P>without knowledge; for without that, the heart is naught.



<P>&lt;Knowledge and knowledge>&nbsp; There is, therefore, knowledge and

knowledge.



<P>Knowledge that resteth in the bare speculation of things;



<P>and knowledge that is accompanied with the grace of faith and love;



<P>which puts a man upon doing even the will of God from the heart:



<P>the first of these will serve the talker; but without the other



<P>the true Christian is not content.&nbsp; &lt;True knowledge attended



<P>with endeavors>&nbsp; "Give me understanding, and I shall keep thy law;



<P>yea, I shall observe it with my whole heart."&nbsp; [Ps. 119:34]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>TALK.&nbsp; You lie at the catch again; this is not for edification.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Well, if you please, propound another sign how



<P>this work of grace discovereth itself where it is.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>TALK.&nbsp; Not I, for I see we shall not agree.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Well, if you will not, will you give me leave to do it?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>TALK.&nbsp; You may use your liberty.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>p351--p403</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Page351--Page403</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>{351}



<P>But I replied, Lord, I am a great, a very great sinner.



<P>And he answered, "My grace is sufficient for thee."&nbsp; [2 Cor.12:9]



<P>Then I said, But, Lord, what is believing?&nbsp; And then I saw



<P>from that saying, "He that cometh to me shall never hunger,



<P>and he that believeth on me shall never thirst", that believing



<P>and coming was all one; and that he that came, that is,



<P>ran out in his heart and affections after salvation by Christ,



<P>he indeed believed in Christ.&nbsp; [John 6:35]&nbsp; Then the water



<P>stood in mine eyes, and I asked further.&nbsp; But, Lord,



<P>may such a great sinner as I am be indeed accepted of thee,



<P>and be saved by thee?&nbsp; And I heard him say, "And him that cometh

to me,



<P>I will in no wise cast out."&nbsp; [John 6:37]&nbsp; Then I said, But

how, Lord,



<P>must I consider of thee in my coming to thee, that my faith may be



<P>placed aright upon thee?&nbsp; Then he said, "Christ Jesus came



<P>into the world to save sinners."&nbsp; [1 Tim. 1:15]&nbsp; "He is the

end



<P>of the law for righteousness to every one that believeth."&nbsp; [Rom.

10:4]



<P>"He died for our sins, and rose again for our justification."



<P>[Rom. 4:25]&nbsp; "He loved us, and washed us from our sins



<P>in his own blood."&nbsp; [Rev. 1:5]&nbsp; "He is mediator betwixt God

and us."



<P>[1 Tim. 2:5]&nbsp; "He ever liveth to make intercession for us."



<P>[Heb. 7:24,25]&nbsp; From all which I gathered, that I must



<P>look for righteousness in his person, and for satisfaction for my sins



<P>by his blood; that what he did in obedience to his Father's law,



<P>and in submitting to the penalty thereof, was not for himself,



<P>but for him that will accept it for his salvation, and be thankful.



<P>And now was my heart full of joy, mine eyes full of tears,



<P>and mine affections running over with love to the name, people,



<P>and ways of Jesus Christ.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{352}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; This was a revelation of Christ to your soul indeed;



<P>but tell me particularly what effect this had upon your spirit.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; It made me see that all the world, notwithstanding all



<P>the righteousness thereof, is in a state of condemnation.



<P>It made me see that God the Father, though he be just,



<P>can justly justify the coming sinner.&nbsp; It made me greatly ashamed



<P>of the vileness of my former life, and confounded me with the sense



<P>of mine own ignorance; for there never came thought into my heart



<P>before now that showed me so the beauty of Jesus Christ.



<P>It made me love a holy life, and long to do something for



<P>the honour and glory of the name of the Lord Jesus; yea,



<P>I thought that had I now a thousand gallons of blood in my body,



<P>I could spill it all for the sake of the Lord Jesus.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{353}



<P>I saw then in my dream that Hopeful looked back and saw Ignorance,



<P>whom they had left behind, coming after.&nbsp; Look, said he to Christian,



<P>how far yonder youngster loitereth behind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Ay, ay, I see him; he careth not for our company.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; But I trow it would not have hurt him had he



<P>kept pace with us hitherto.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; That is true; but I warrant you he thinketh otherwise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; That, I think, he doth; but, however, let us tarry for him.



<P>So they did.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{354}



<P>&lt;Young Ignorance comes up again; their talk>



<P>Then Christian said to him, Come away, man, why do you stay so behind?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; I take my pleasure in walking alone, even more a great

deal



<P>than in company, unless I like it the better.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then said Christian to Hopeful, (but softly), Did I not tell you



<P>he cared not for our company?&nbsp; But, however, said he, come up,



<P>and let us talk away the time in this solitary place.



<P>Then directing his speech to Ignorance, he said, Come, how do you?



<P>How stands it between God and your soul now?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{355}



<P>&lt;Ignorance's hope, and the ground of it>



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; I hope well; for I am always full of good motions,



<P>that come into my mind, to comfort me as I walk.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; What good motions? pray, tell us.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; Why, I think of God and heaven.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; So do the devils and damned souls.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; But I think of them and desire them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; So do many that are never like to come there.



<P>"The soul of the sluggard desireth, and hath nothing."&nbsp; [Prov.

13:4]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; But I think of them, and leave all for them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; That I doubt; for leaving all is a hard matter:&nbsp; yea,



<P>a harder matter than many are aware of.&nbsp; But why, or by what,



<P>art thou persuaded that thou hast left all for God and heaven.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{356}



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; My heart tells me so.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; The wise man says, "He that trusts his own heart is a fool."



<P>[Prov. 28:26]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; This is spoken of an evil heart, but mine is a good one.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; But how dost thou prove that?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; It comforts me in hopes of heaven.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; That may be through its deceitfulness; for a man's heart



<P>may minister comfort to him in the hopes of that thing for which



<P>he yet has no ground to hope.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; But my heart and life agree together, and therefore



<P>my hope is well grounded.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Who told thee that thy heart and life agree together?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; My heart tells me so.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Ask my fellow if I be a thief! Thy heart tells thee so!



<P>Except the Word of God beareth witness in this matter,



<P>other testimony is of no value.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{357}



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; But is it not a good heart that hath good thoughts?



<P>and is not that a good life that is according to God's commandments?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, that is a good heart that hath good thoughts,



<P>and that is a good life that is according to God's commandments;



<P>but it is one thing, indeed, to have these, and another thing



<P>only to think so.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; Pray, what count you good thoughts, and a life according



<P>to God's commandments?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; There are good thoughts of divers kinds; some respecting



<P>ourselves, some God, some Christ, and some other things.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;What are good thoughts>



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; What be good thoughts respecting ourselves?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Such as agree with the Word of God.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{358}



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; When do our thoughts of ourselves agree with the Word of

God?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; When we pass the same judgment upon ourselves



<P>which the Word passes.&nbsp; To explain myself--the Word of God



<P>saith of persons in a natural condition, "There is none righteous,



<P>there is none that doeth good."&nbsp; [Rom. 3]&nbsp; It saith also,



<P>that "every imagination of the heart of man is only evil,



<P>and that continually."&nbsp; [Gen. 6:5]&nbsp; And again, "The imagination



<P>of man's heart is evil from his youth."&nbsp; [Rom. 8:21]&nbsp; Now

then,



<P>when we think thus of ourselves, having sense thereof,



<P>then are our thoughts good ones, because according to the Word of God.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; I will never believe that my heart is thus bad.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Therefore thou never hadst one good thought concerning thyself



<P>in thy life.&nbsp; But let me go on.&nbsp; As the Word passeth a judgment



<P>upon our heart, so it passeth a judgment upon our ways; and when



<P>OUR thoughts of our hearts and ways agree with the judgment which



<P>the Word giveth of both, then are both good, because agreeing thereto.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{359}



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; Make out your meaning.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Why, the Word of God saith that man's ways are crooked ways;



<P>not good, but perverse.&nbsp; [Ps. 125:5, Prov. 2:15]&nbsp; It saith



<P>they are naturally out of the good way, that they have not known it.



<P>[Rom. 3]&nbsp; Now, when a man thus thinketh of his ways,--I say,



<P>when he doth sensibly, and with heart-humiliation, thus think,



<P>then hath he good thoughts of his own ways, because his thoughts



<P>now agree with the judgment of the Word of God.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{360}



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; What are good thoughts concerning God?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Even as I have said concerning ourselves, when our thoughts

of God



<P>do agree with what the Word saith of him; and that is,



<P>when we think of his being and attributes as the Word hath taught,



<P>of which I cannot now discourse at large; but to speak of him



<P>with reference to us:&nbsp; Then we have right thoughts of God,



<P>when we think that he knows us better than we know ourselves,



<P>and can see sin in us when and where we can see none in ourselves;



<P>when we think he knows our inmost thoughts, and that our heart,



<P>with all its depths, is always open unto his eyes; also,



<P>when we think that all our righteousness stinks in his nostrils,



<P>and that, therefore, he cannot abide to see us stand before him



<P>in any confidence, even in all our best performances.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{361}



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; Do you think that I am such a fool as to think God can

see



<P>no further than I? or, that I would come to God in the best



<P>of my performances?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Why, how dost thou think in this matter?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; Why, to be short, I think I must believe in Christ



<P>for justification.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; How! think thou must believe in Christ, when thou seest not

thy



<P>need of him!&nbsp; Thou neither seest thy original nor actual infirmities;



<P>but hast such an opinion of thyself, and of what thou dost,



<P>as plainly renders thee to be one that did never see a necessity



<P>of Christ's personal righteousness to justify thee before God.



<P>How, then, dost thou say, I believe in Christ?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{362}



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; I believe well enough for all that.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; How dost thou believe?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;The faith of Ignorance>



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; I believe that Christ died for sinners, and that I shall

be



<P>justified before God from the curse, through his gracious acceptance



<P>of my obedience to his law.&nbsp; Or thus, Christ makes my duties,



<P>that are religious, acceptable to his Father, by virtue of his merits;



<P>and so shall I be justified.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{363}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Let me give an answer to this confession of thy faith:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>1.&nbsp; Thou believest with a fantastical faith; for this faith is

nowhere



<P>described in the Word.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>2.&nbsp; Thou believest with a false faith; because it taketh justification



<P>from the personal righteousness of Christ, and applies it to thy own.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>3.&nbsp; This faith maketh not Christ a justifier of thy person,



<P>but of thy actions; and of thy person for thy actions' sake,



<P>which is false.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>4.&nbsp; Therefore, this faith is deceitful, even such as will leave

thee



<P>under wrath, in the day of God Almighty; for true justifying faith



<P>puts the soul, as sensible of its condition by the law, upon flying



<P>for refuge unto Christ's righteousness, which righteousness of his



<P>is not an act of grace, by which he maketh for justification,



<P>thy obedience accepted with God; but his personal obedience to the law,



<P>in doing and suffering for us what that required at our hands;



<P>this righteousness, I say, true faith accepteth; under the skirt



<P>of which, the soul being shrouded, and by it presented



<P>as spotless before God, it is accepted, and acquit from condemnation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{364}



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; What! would you have us trust to what Christ, in his own

person,



<P>has done without us?&nbsp; This conceit would loosen the reins of our

lust,



<P>and tolerate us to live as we list; for what matter how we live,



<P>if we may be justified by Christ's personal righteousness from all,



<P>when we believe it?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Ignorance is thy name, and as thy name is, so art thou;



<P>even this thy answer demonstrateth what I say.&nbsp; Ignorant thou art



<P>of what justifying righteousness is, and as ignorant how to secure



<P>thy soul, through the faith of it, from the heavy wrath of God.



<P>Yea, thou also art ignorant of the true effects of saving faith



<P>in this righteousness of Christ, which is, to bow and win over



<P>the heart to God in Christ, to love his name, his word, ways,



<P>and people, and not as thou ignorantly imaginest.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Ask him if ever he had Christ revealed to him from heaven.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{365}



<P>&lt;Ignorance jangles with them>



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; What! you are a man for revelations!&nbsp; I believe that

what



<P>both you, and all the rest of you, say about that matter,



<P>is but the fruit of distracted brains.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Why, man! Christ is so hid in God from the natural apprehensions



<P>of the flesh, that he cannot by any man be savingly known,



<P>unless God the Father reveals him to them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{366}



<P>&lt;He speaks reproachfully of what he knows not>



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; That is your faith, but not mine; yet mine, I doubt not,



<P>is as good as yours, though I have not in my head so many whimsies



<P>as you.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Give me leave to put in a word.&nbsp; You ought not so slightly



<P>to speak of this matter; for this I will boldly affirm,



<P>even as my good companion hath done, that no man can know Jesus Christ



<P>but by the revelation of the Father; [Matt. 11:27] yea, and faith too,



<P>by which the soul layeth hold upon Christ, if it be right,



<P>must be wrought by the exceeding greatness of his mighty power;



<P>the working of which faith, I perceive, poor Ignorance,



<P>thou art ignorant of.&nbsp; [1 Cor. 12:3, Eph. 1:18,19]&nbsp; Be awakened,

then,



<P>see thine own wretchedness, and fly to the Lord Jesus;



<P>and by his righteousness, which is the righteousness of God,



<P>for he himself is God, thou shalt be delivered from condemnation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{367}



<P>&lt;The talk broke up>



<P>IGNOR.&nbsp; You go so fast, I cannot keep pace with you.



<P>Do you go on before; I must stay a while behind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then they said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Well, Ignorance, wilt thou yet foolish be,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; To slight good counsel, ten times given thee?



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And if thou yet refuse it, thou shalt know,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Ere long, the evil of thy doing so.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Remember, man, in time, stoop, do not fear;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Good counsel taken well, saves:&nbsp; therefore

hear.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; But if thou yet shalt slight it, thou wilt

be



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The loser, (Ignorance), I'll warrant thee.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then Christian addressed thus himself to his fellow:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Well, come, my good Hopeful, I perceive that thou and I



<P>must walk by ourselves again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{368}



<P>So I saw in my dream that they went on apace before,



<P>and Ignorance he came hobbling after.&nbsp; Then said Christian



<P>to his companion, It pities me much for this poor man,



<P>it will certainly go ill with him at last.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Alas! there are abundance in our town in his condition,



<P>whole families, yea, whole streets, and that of pilgrims too;



<P>and if there be so many in our parts, how many, think you,



<P>must there be in the place where he was born?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Indeed the Word saith, "He hath blinded their eyes,



<P>lest they should see", &amp;c.&nbsp; But now we are by ourselves,



<P>what do you think of such men?&nbsp; Have they at no time, think you,



<P>convictions of sin, and so consequently fears that their state



<P>is dangerous?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Nay, do you answer that question yourself, for you are



<P>the elder man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then I say, sometimes (as I think) they may; but they being



<P>naturally ignorant, understand not that such convictions tend



<P>to their good; and therefore they do desperately seek to stifle them,



<P>and presumptuously continue to flatter themselves in the way of



<P>their own hearts.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{369}



<P>&lt;The good use of fear>



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; I do believe, as you say, that fear tends much to men's

good,



<P>and to make them right, at their beginning to go on pilgrimage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Without all doubt it doth, if it be right; for so says the

Word,



<P>"The fear of the Lord is the beginning of wisdom."



<P>[Prov. 1:7, 9:10, Job 28:28, Ps. 111:10]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{370}



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; How will you describe right fear?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Right fear>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; True or right fear is discovered by three things:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>1.&nbsp; By its rise; it is caused by saving convictions for sin.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>2.&nbsp; It driveth the soul to lay fast hold of Christ for salvation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>3.&nbsp; It begetteth and continueth in the soul a great reverence of

God,



<P>his Word, and ways, keeping it tender, and making it afraid



<P>to turn from them, to the right hand or to the left,



<P>to anything that may dishonour God, break its peace, grieve the Spirit,



<P>or cause the enemy to speak reproachfully.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Well said; I believe you have said the truth.



<P>Are we now almost got past the Enchanted Ground?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Why, art thou weary of this discourse?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; No, verily, but that I would know where we are.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{371}



<P>&lt;Why ignorant persons stifle convictions>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; We have not now above two miles further to go thereon.



<P>But let us return to our matter.&nbsp; &lt;In general>&nbsp; Now the

ignorant



<P>know not that such convictions as tend to put them in fear



<P>are for their good, and therefore they seek to stifle them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; How do they seek to stifle them?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{372}



<P>&lt;In particular>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; 1.&nbsp; They think that those fears are wrought by the devil,



<P>(though indeed they are wrought of God); and, thinking so,



<P>they resist them as things that directly tend to their overthrow.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>2.&nbsp; They also think that these fears tend to the spoiling



<P>of their faith, when, alas, for them, poor men that they are,



<P>they have none at all! and therefore they harden their hearts



<P>against them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>3.&nbsp; They presume they ought not to fear; and, therefore,



<P>in despite of them, wax presumptuously confident.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>4.&nbsp; They see that those fears tend to take away from them



<P>their pitiful old self-holiness, and therefore they resist them



<P>with all their might.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{373}



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; I know something of this myself; for, before I knew myself,



<P>it was so with me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Well, we will leave, at this time, our neighbour Ignorance



<P>by himself, and fall upon another profitable question.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; With all my heart, but you shall still begin.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Talk about one Temporary>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Well then, did you not know, about ten years ago,



<P>one Temporary in your parts, who was a forward man in religion then?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Where he dwelt>



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Know him! yes, he dwelt in Graceless, a town about two miles



<P>off of Honesty, and he dwelt next door to one Turnback.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{374}



<P>&lt;He was towardly once>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Right, he dwelt under the same roof with him.&nbsp; Well,



<P>that man was much awakened once; I believe that then he had



<P>some sight of his sins, and of the wages that were due thereto.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; I am of your mind, for, my house not being above three miles



<P>from him, he would ofttimes come to me, and that with many tears.



<P>Truly I pitied the man, and was not altogether without hope of him;



<P>but one may see, it is not every one that cries, Lord, Lord.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; He told me once that he was resolved to go on pilgrimage,



<P>as we do now; but all of a sudden he grew acquainted with one Save-self,



<P>and then he became a stranger to me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{375}



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Now, since we are talking about him, let us a little inquire



<P>into the reason of the sudden backsliding of him and such others.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; It may be very profitable, but do you begin.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Reasons why towardly ones go back>



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Well, then, there are in my judgment four reasons for it:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{376}



<P>1.&nbsp; Though the consciences of such men are awakened, yet their

minds



<P>are not changed; therefore, when the power of guilt weareth away,



<P>that which provoked them to be religious ceaseth, wherefore they



<P>naturally turn to their own course again, even as we see the dog



<P>that is sick of what he has eaten, so long as his sickness prevails



<P>he vomits and casts up all; not that he doth this of a free mind



<P>(if we may say a dog has a mind), but because it troubleth his stomach;



<P>but now, when his sickness is over, and so his stomach eased,



<P>his desire being not at all alienate from his vomit, he turns him about



<P>and licks up all, and so it is true which is written, "The dog is turned



<P>to his own vomit again."&nbsp; [2 Pet. 2:22]&nbsp; Thus I say,



<P>being hot for heaven, by virtue only of the sense and fear



<P>of the torments of hell, as their sense of hell and the fears



<P>of damnation chills and cools, so their desires for heaven and salvation



<P>cool also.&nbsp; So then it comes to pass, that when their guilt and

fear



<P>is gone, their desires for heaven and happiness die, and they return



<P>to their course again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{377}



<P>2.&nbsp; Another reason is, they have slavish fears that do overmaster

them;



<P>I speak now of the fears that they have of men, for "the fear of man



<P>bringeth a snare".&nbsp; [Prov. 29:25]&nbsp; So then, though they seem

to be



<P>hot for heaven, so long as the flames of hell are about their ears,



<P>yet when that terror is a little over, they betake themselves



<P>to second thoughts; namely, that it is good to be wise, and not to run



<P>(for they know not what) the hazard of losing all, or, at least,



<P>of bringing themselves into unavoidable and unnecessary troubles,



<P>and so they fall in with the world again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{378}



<P>3. The shame that attends religion lies also as a block in their way;



<P>they are proud and haughty; and religion in their eye



<P>is low and contemptible, therefore, when they have lost their sense



<P>of hell and wrath to come, they return again to their former course.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{379}



<P>4. Guilt, and to meditate terror, are grievous to them.



<P>They like not to see their misery before they come into it;



<P>though perhaps the sight of it first, if they loved that sight,



<P>might make them fly whither the righteous fly and are safe.



<P>But because they do, as I hinted before, even shun the thoughts



<P>of guilt and terror, therefore, when once they are rid



<P>of their awakenings about the terrors and wrath of God,



<P>they harden their hearts gladly, and choose such ways as will



<P>harden them more and more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{380}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; You are pretty near the business, for the bottom of all



<P>is for want of a change in their mind and will.&nbsp; And therefore



<P>they are but like the felon that standeth before the judge,



<P>he quakes and trembles, and seems to repent most heartily,



<P>but the bottom of all is the fear of the halter; not that he hath



<P>any detestation of the offence, as is evident, because,



<P>let but this man have his liberty, and he will be a thief,



<P>and so a rogue still, whereas, if his mind was changed,



<P>he would be otherwise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{381}



<P>HOPE.&nbsp; Now I have showed you the reasons of their going back,



<P>do you show me the manner thereof.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; So I will willingly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;How the apostate goes back>



<P>1.&nbsp; They draw off their thoughts, all that they may,



<P>from the remembrance of God, death, and judgment to come.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>2.&nbsp; Then they cast off by degrees private duties, as closet prayer,



<P>curbing their lusts, watching, sorrow for sin, and the like.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>3.&nbsp; Then they shun the company of lively and warm Christians.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>4.&nbsp; After that they grow cold to public duty, as hearing, reading,



<P>godly conference, and the like.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>5.&nbsp; Then they begin to pick holes, as we say, in the coats



<P>of some of the godly; and that devilishly, that they may have



<P>a seeming colour to throw religion (for the sake of some infirmity



<P>they have espied in them) behind their backs.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>6.&nbsp; Then they begin to adhere to, and associate themselves with,



<P>carnal, loose, and wanton men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>7.&nbsp; Then they give way to carnal and wanton discourses in secret;



<P>and glad are they if they can see such things in any



<P>that are counted honest, that they may the more boldly do it



<P>through their example.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>8.&nbsp; After this they begin to play with little sins openly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>9.&nbsp; And then, being hardened, they show themselves as they are.



<P>Thus, being launched again into the gulf of misery, unless a miracle



<P>of grace prevent it, they everlastingly perish in their own deceivings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{382}



<P>Now I saw in my dream, that by this time the Pilgrims were got over



<P>the Enchanted Ground, and entering into the country of Beulah, whose

air



<P>was very sweet and pleasant, the way lying directly through it,



<P>they solaced themselves there for a season.&nbsp; Yea, here they heard



<P>continually the singing of birds, and saw every day the flowers appear



<P>on the earth, and heard the voice of the turtle in the land.



<P>[Isa. 62:4, Song of Solomon 2:10-12]&nbsp; In this country



<P>the sun shineth night and day; wherefore this was beyond



<P>the Valley of the Shadow of Death, and also out of the reach



<P>of Giant Despair, neither could they from this place so much as see



<P>Doubting Castle.&nbsp; &lt;Angels>&nbsp; Here they were within sight

of the city



<P>they were going to, also here met them some of the inhabitants thereof;



<P>for in this land the Shining Ones commonly walked, because it was



<P>upon the borders of heaven.&nbsp; In this land also, the contract between



<P>the bride and the bridegroom was renewed; yea, here, "As the bridegroom



<P>rejoiceth over the bride, so did their God rejoice over them."



<P>[Isa. 62:5]&nbsp; Here they had no want of corn and wine; for in this

place



<P>they met with abundance of what they had sought for in all



<P>their pilgrimage.&nbsp; [Isa. 62:8]&nbsp; Here they heard voices from



<P>out of the city, loud voices, saying, "`Say ye to the daughter of Zion,



<P>Behold, thy salvation cometh!&nbsp; Behold, his reward is with him!'



<P>Here all the inhabitants of the country called them, `The holy people,



<P>The redeemed of the Lord, Sought out'", etc.&nbsp; [Isa. 62:11,12]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{383}



<P>Now as they walked in this land, they had more rejoicing than in parts



<P>more remote from the kingdom to which they were bound; and drawing near



<P>to the city, they had yet a more perfect view thereof.&nbsp; It was

builded



<P>of pearls and precious stones, also the street thereof was paved



<P>with gold; so that by reason of the natural glory of the city,



<P>and the reflection of the sunbeams upon it, Christian with desire



<P>fell sick; Hopeful also had a fit or two of the same disease.



<P>Wherefore, here they lay by it a while, crying out, because of



<P>their pangs, If ye find my beloved, tell him that I am sick of love.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{384}



<P>But, being a little strengthened, and better able to bear



<P>their sickness, they walked on their way, and came yet nearer



<P>and nearer, where were orchards, vineyards, and gardens,



<P>and their gates opened into the highway.&nbsp; Now, as they came up



<P>to these places, behold the gardener stood in the way,



<P>to whom the Pilgrims said, Whose goodly vineyards and gardens are these?



<P>He answered, They are the King's, and are planted here



<P>for his own delight, and also for the solace of pilgrims.



<P>So the gardener had them into the vineyards, and bid them



<P>refresh themselves with the dainties.&nbsp; [Deut. 23:24]



<P>He also showed them there the King's walks, and the arbours where



<P>he delighted to be; and here they tarried and slept.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{385}



<P>Now I beheld in my dream that they talked more in their sleep



<P>at this time than ever they did in all their journey;



<P>and being in a muse thereabout, the gardener said even to me,



<P>Wherefore musest thou at the matter?&nbsp; It is the nature of the fruit



<P>of the grapes of these vineyards to go down so sweetly



<P>as to cause the lips of them that are asleep to speak.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{386}



<P>So I saw that when they awoke, they addressed themselves to go up



<P>to the city; but, as I said, the reflection of the sun upon the city



<P>(for the city was pure gold) was so extremely glorious



<P>that they could not, as yet, with open face behold it, but through



<P>an instrument made for that purpose.&nbsp; So I saw, that as I went

on,



<P>there met them two men, in raiment that shone like gold;



<P>lso their faces shone as the light.&nbsp; [Rev. 21:18, 2 Cor. 3:18]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{387}



<P>These men asked the Pilgrims whence they came; and they told them.



<P>They also asked them where they had lodged, what difficulties



<P>and dangers, what comforts and pleasures they had met in the way;



<P>and they told them.&nbsp; Then said the men that met them, You have



<P>but two difficulties more to meet with, and then you are in the city.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{388}



<P>Christian then, and his companion, asked the men to go along with them;



<P>so they told them they would.&nbsp; But, said they, you must obtain

it



<P>by your own faith.&nbsp; So I saw in my dream that they went on together,



<P>until they came in sight of the gate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{389}



<P>&lt;Death>



<P>Now, I further saw, that betwixt them and the gate was a river,



<P>but there was no bridge to go over: the river was very deep.



<P>At the sight, therefore, of this river, the Pilgrims were much stunned;



<P>but the men that went in with them said, You must go through,



<P>or you cannot come at the gate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{390}



<P>&lt;Death is not welcome to nature, though by it we pass out of this

world



<P>into glory*>



<P>The Pilgrims then began to inquire if there was no other way



<P>to the gate; to which they answered, Yes; but there hath not any,



<P>save two, to wit, Enoch and Elijah, been permitted to tread that path



<P>since the foundation of the world, nor shall, until the last trumpet



<P>shall sound.&nbsp; [1 Cor. 15:51,52]&nbsp; The Pilgrims then,



<P>especially Christian, began to despond in their minds,



<P>and looked this way and that, but no way could be found by them



<P>by which they might escape the river.&nbsp; Then they asked the men



<P>if the waters were all of a depth.&nbsp; &lt;Angels help us not comfortably



<P>through death> They said:&nbsp; No; yet they could not help them



<P>in that case; for, said they, you shall find it deeper or shallower



<P>as you believe in the King of the place.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>*In the Resurrection of the Righteous.&nbsp; [Rev. 20:4-6]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{391}



<P>They then addressed themselves to the water and, entering,



<P>Christian began to sink, and crying out to his good friend Hopeful,



<P>he said, I sink in deep waters; the billows go over my head,



<P>all his waves go over me! Selah.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{392}



<P>&lt;Christian's conflict at the hour of death>



<P>Then said the other, Be of good cheer, my brother, I feel the bottom,



<P>and it is good.&nbsp; Then said Christian, Ah! my friend,



<P>the sorrows of death hath compassed me about; I shall not see the land



<P>that flows with milk and honey; and with that a great darkness



<P>and horror fell upon Christian, so that he could not see before him.



<P>Also here he in great measure lost his senses, so that he could neither



<P>remember nor orderly talk of any of those sweet refreshments



<P>that he had met with in the way of his pilgrimage.&nbsp; But all the

words



<P>that he spake still tended to discover that he had horror of mind,



<P>and heart fears that he should die in that river, and never obtain



<P>entrance in at the gate.&nbsp; Here also, as they that stood by perceived,



<P>he was much in the troublesome thoughts of the sins that he



<P>had committed, both since and before he began to be a pilgrim.



<P>It was also observed that he was troubled with apparitions



<P>of hobgoblins and evil spirits, for ever and anon he would



<P>intimate so much by words.&nbsp; Hopeful, therefore, here had much ado



<P>to keep his brother's head above water; yea, sometimes he would be



<P>quite gone down, and then, ere a while, he would rise up again



<P>half dead.&nbsp; Hopeful also would endeavour to comfort him, saying,



<P>Brother, I see the gate, and men standing by to receive us:



<P>but Christian would answer, It is you, it is you they wait for;



<P>you have been Hopeful ever since I knew you.&nbsp; And so have you,



<P>said he to Christian.&nbsp; Ah! brother! said he, surely if I was right



<P>he would now arise to help me; but for my sins he hath brought me



<P>into the snare, and hath left me.&nbsp; Then said Hopeful, My brother,



<P>you have quite forgot the text, where it is said of the wicked,



<P>"There are no bands in their death, but their strength is firm.



<P>They are not in trouble as other men, neither are they plagued



<P>like other men.&nbsp; [Ps. 73:4,5]&nbsp; These troubles and distresses

that you



<P>go through in these waters are no sign that God hath forsaken you;



<P>but are sent to try you, whether you will call to mind that which



<P>heretofore you have received of his goodness, and live upon him



<P>in your distresses.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{393}



<P>&lt;Christian delivered from his fears in death>



<P>Then I saw in my dream, that Christian was as in a muse a while.



<P>To whom also Hopeful added this word, Be of good cheer,



<P>Jesus Christ maketh thee whole; and with that Christian brake out



<P>with a loud voice, Oh, I see him again! and he tells me,



<P>"When thou passest through the waters, I will be with thee,



<P>and through the rivers, they shall not overflow thee."&nbsp; [Isa. 43:2]



<P>Then they both took courage, and the enemy was after that as still



<P>as a stone, until they were gone over.&nbsp; Christian therefore



<P>presently found ground to stand upon, and so it followed that



<P>the rest of the river was but shallow.&nbsp; Thus they got over.



<P>&lt;The angels do wait for them, so soon as they are passed out



<P>of this world>&nbsp; Now, upon the bank of the river, on the other side,



<P>they saw the two shining men again, who there waited for them;



<P>wherefore, being come out of the river, they saluted them, saying,



<P>We are ministering spirits, sent forth to minister for those that



<P>shall be heirs of salvation.&nbsp; Thus they went along towards the

gate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{394}



<P>&lt;They have put off mortality>&nbsp; Now you must note that the city

stood



<P>upon a mighty hill, but the Pilgrims went up that hill with ease,



<P>because they had these two men to lead them up by the arms;



<P>also, they had left their mortal garments behind them in the river,



<P>for though they went in with them, they came out without them.



<P>They, therefore, went up here with much agility and speed,



<P>though the foundation upon which the city was framed was higher than



<P>the clouds.&nbsp; They therefore went up through the regions of the

air,



<P>sweetly talking as they went, being comforted, because they safely



<P>got over the river, and had such glorious companions to attend them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Now, now, look how the holy pilgrims ride,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Clouds are their chariots, angels are their

guide:



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Who would not here for him all hazards run,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; That thus provides for his when this world's

done?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{395}



<P>The talk they had with the Shining Ones was about the glory



<P>of the place; who told them that the beauty and glory of it



<P>was inexpressible.&nbsp; There, said they, is the Mount Zion,



<P>the heavenly Jerusalem, the innumerable company of angels,



<P>and the spirits of just men made perfect.&nbsp; [Heb. 12:22-24]



<P>You are going now, said they, to the paradise of God, wherein you shall



<P>see the tree of life, and eat of the never-fading fruits thereof;



<P>and when you come there, you shall have white robes given you,



<P>and your walk and talk shall be every day with the King,



<P>even all the days of eternity.&nbsp; [Rev. 2:7, 3:4, 21:4,5]



<P>There you shall not see again such things as you saw when you were in



<P>the lower region upon the earth, to wit, sorrow, sickness, affliction,



<P>and death, for the former things are passed away.&nbsp; You are now

going



<P>to Abraham, to Isaac, and Jacob, and to the prophets--men that God



<P>hath taken away from the evil to come, and that are now resting



<P>upon their beds, each one walking in his righteousness.&nbsp; [Isa.

57:1,2,



<P>65:17]&nbsp; The men then asked, What must we do in the holy place?



<P>To whom it was answered, You must there receive the comforts



<P>of all your toil, and have joy for all your sorrow; you must reap



<P>what you have sown, even the fruit of all your prayers, and tears,



<P>and sufferings for the King by the way.&nbsp; [Gal. 6:7]&nbsp; In that

place



<P>you must wear crowns of gold, and enjoy the perpetual sight and vision



<P>of the Holy One, for there you shall see him as he is.&nbsp; [1 John

3:2]



<P>There also you shall serve him continually with praise, with shouting,



<P>and thanksgiving, whom you desired to serve in the world,



<P>though with much difficulty, because of the infirmity of your flesh.



<P>There your eyes shall be delighted with seeing, and your ears with



<P>hearing the pleasant voice of the Mighty One.&nbsp; There you shall



<P>enjoy your friends again that are gone thither before you;



<P>and there you shall with joy receive, even every one that follows



<P>into the holy place after you.&nbsp; There also shall you be clothed



<P>with glory and majesty, and put into an equipage fit to ride out



<P>with the King of Glory.&nbsp; When he shall come with sound of trumpet



<P>in the clouds, as upon the wings of the wind, you shall come with him;



<P>and when he shall sit upon the throne of judgment; you shall sit by

him;



<P>yea, and when he shall pass sentence upon all the workers of iniquity,



<P>let them be angels or men, you also shall have a voice in that judgment,



<P>because they were his and your enemies.&nbsp; [1 Thes. 4:13-16, Jude

1:14,



<P>Dan. 7:9,10, 1 Cor. 6:2,3]&nbsp; Also, when he shall again return to



<P>the city, you shall go too, with sound of trumpet, and be ever with

him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{396}



<P>Now while they were thus drawing towards the gate, behold a company



<P>of the heavenly host came out to meet them; to whom it was said,



<P>by the other two Shining Ones, These are the men that have



<P>loved our Lord when they were in the world, and that have left all



<P>for his holy name; and he hath sent us to fetch them, and we have



<P>brought them thus far on their desired journey, that they may go in



<P>and look their Redeemer in the face with joy.&nbsp; Then the heavenly

host



<P>gave a great shout, saying, "Blessed are they which are called unto



<P>the marriage supper of the Lamb."&nbsp; [Rev. 19:9]&nbsp; There came

out also



<P>at this time to meet them, several of the King's trumpeters,



<P>clothed in white and shining raiment, who, with melodious noises,



<P>and loud, made even the heavens to echo with their sound.



<P>These trumpeters saluted Christian and his fellow with ten thousand



<P>welcomes from the world; and this they did with shouting,



<P>and sound of trumpet.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{397}



<P>This done, they compassed them round on every side; some went before,



<P>some behind, and some on the right hand, some on the left,



<P>(as it were to guard them through the upper regions),



<P>continually sounding as they went, with melodious noise,



<P>in notes on high:&nbsp; so that the very sight was, to them that could



<P>behold it, as if heaven itself was come down to meet them.&nbsp; Thus,



<P>therefore, they walked on together; and as they walked,



<P>ever and anon these trumpeters, even with joyful sound,



<P>would, by mixing their music with looks and gestures,



<P>still signify to Christian and his brother, how welcome they were



<P>into their company, and with what gladness they came to meet them;



<P>and now were these two men, as it were, in heaven, before they came



<P>at it, being swallowed up with the sight of angels, and with hearing



<P>of their melodious notes.&nbsp; Here also they had the city itself in

view,



<P>and they thought they heard all the bells therein to ring,



<P>to welcome them thereto.&nbsp; But above all, the warm and joyful thoughts



<P>that they had about their own dwelling there, with such company,



<P>and that for ever and ever.&nbsp; Oh, by what tongue or pen can



<P>their glorious joy be expressed!&nbsp; And thus they came up to the

gate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{398}



<P>Now, when they were come up to the gate, there was written over



<P>it in letters of gold, "Blessed are they that do his commandments,



<P>that they may have right to the tree of life, and may enter in



<P>through the gates into the city."&nbsp; [Rev. 22:14]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{399}



<P>Then I saw in my dream that the Shining Men bid them call at the gate;



<P>the which, when they did, some looked from above over the gate, to wit,



<P>Enoch, Moses, and Elijah, &amp;c., to whom it was said, These pilgrims



<P>are come from the City of Destruction, for the love that they bear



<P>to the King of this place; and then the Pilgrims gave in unto them



<P>each man his certificate, which they had received in the beginning;



<P>those, therefore, were carried in to the King, who, when he had



<P>read them, said, Where are the men?&nbsp; To whom it was answered,



<P>They are standing without the gate.&nbsp; The King then commanded



<P>to open the gate, "That the righteous nation," said he,



<P>"which keepeth the truth, may enter in."&nbsp; [Isa. 26:2]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{400}



<P>Now I saw in my dream that these two men went in at the gate: and lo,



<P>as they entered, they were transfigured, and they had raiment put on



<P>that shone like gold.&nbsp; There was also that met them with



<P>harps and crowns, and gave them to them--the harps to praise withal,



<P>and the crowns in token of honour.&nbsp; Then I heard in my dream



<P>that all the bells in the city rang again for joy, and that



<P>it was said unto them, "ENTER YE INTO THE JOY OF YOUR LORD."



<P>I also heard the men themselves, that they sang with a loud voice,



<P>saying, "BLESSING AND HONOUR, AND GLORY, AND POWER, BE UNTO HIM



<P>THAT SITTETH UPON THE THRONE, AND UNTO THE LAMB, FOR EVER AND EVER."



<P>[Rev. 5:13]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{401}



<P>Now, just as the gates were opened to let in the men,



<P>I looked in after them, and, behold, the City shone like the sun;



<P>the streets also were paved with gold, and in them walked many men,



<P>with crowns on their heads, palms in their hands, and golden harps



<P>to sing praises withal.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{402}



<P>There were also of them that had wings, and they answered one another



<P>without intermission, saying, "Holy, holy, holy is the Lord."



<P>[Rev. 4:8]&nbsp; And after that they shut up the gates; which,



<P>when I had seen, I wished myself among them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{403}



<P>&lt;Ignorance comes up to the river>



<P>Now while I was gazing upon all these things, I turned my head



<P>to look back, and saw Ignorance come up to the river side;



<P>but he soon got over, and that without half that difficulty which



<P>the other two men met with.&nbsp; &lt;Vain-hope does ferry him over>



<P>For it happened that there was then in that place, one Vain-hope,



<P>a ferryman, that with his boat helped him over; so he,



<P>as the other I saw, did ascend the hill, to come up to the gate,



<P>only he came alone; neither did any man meet him with



<P>the least encouragement.&nbsp; When he was come up to the gate,



<P>he looked up to the writing that was above, and then began to knock,



<P>supposing that entrance should have been quickly administered to him;



<P>but he was asked by the men that looked over the top of the gate,



<P>Whence came you, and what would you have?&nbsp; He answered,



<P>I have eat and drank in the presence of the King, and he has taught



<P>in our streets.&nbsp; Then they asked him for his certificate,



<P>that they might go in and show it to the King; so he fumbled



<P>in his bosom for one, and found none.&nbsp; Then said they, Have you

none?



<P>But the man answered never a word.&nbsp; So they told the King,



<P>but he would not come down to see him, but commanded



<P>the two Shining Ones that conducted Christian and Hopeful to the City,



<P>to go out and take Ignorance, and bind him hand and foot,



<P>and have him away.&nbsp; Then they took him up, and carried him



<P>through the air to the door that I saw in the side of the hill,



<P>and put him in there.&nbsp; Then I saw that there was a way to hell,



<P>even from the gates of heaven, as well as from the City of Destruction.



<P>So I awoke, and behold it was a dream.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>p201--p250</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B><FONT SIZE=+2></FONT></B>



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Page201--Page250</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>{201}



<P>&lt;One good sign of grace>



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; A work of grace in the soul discovereth itself,



<P>either to him that hath it, or to standers by.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To him that hath it thus:&nbsp; It gives him conviction of sin,



<P>especially of the defilement of his nature and the sin of unbelief,



<P>(for the sake of which he is sure to be damned, if he findeth not mercy



<P>at God's hand, by faith in Jesus Christ [John 16:8, Rom. 7:24,



<P>John 16:9, Mark 16:16]).&nbsp; This sight and sense of things worketh

in him



<P>sorrow and shame for sin; he findeth, moreover, revealed in him



<P>the Saviour of the world, and the absolute necessity of closing with

him



<P>for life, at the which he findeth hungerings and thirstings after him;



<P>to which hungerings, &amp;c., the promise is made.&nbsp; [Ps. 38:18,

Jer. 31:19,



<P>Gal. 2:16, Acts 4:12, Matt. 5:6, Rev. 21:6]&nbsp; Now, according to



<P>the strength or weakness of his faith in his Saviour,



<P>so is his joy and peace, so is his love to holiness, so are his desires



<P>to know him more, and also to serve him in this world.



<P>But though I say it discovereth itself thus unto him,



<P>yet it is but seldom that he is able to conclude that this



<P>is a work of grace; because his corruptions now, and his abused reason,



<P>make his mind to misjudge in this matter; therefore,



<P>in him that hath this work, there is required a very sound judgement



<P>before he can, with steadiness, conclude that this is a work of grace.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{202}



<P>To others, it is thus discovered:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>1.&nbsp; By an experimental confession of his faith in Christ.



<P>[Rom. 10:10, Phil. 1:27, Matt. 5:19]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>2.&nbsp; By a life answerable to that confession; to wit, a life of

holiness,



<P>heart-holiness, family-holiness, (if he hath a family),



<P>and by conversation-holiness in the world which, in the general,



<P>teacheth him, inwardly, to abhor his sin, and himself for that,



<P>in secret; to suppress it in his family and to promote holiness



<P>in the world; not by talk only, as a hypocrite or talkative person



<P>may do, but by a practical subjection, in faith and love,



<P>to the power of the Word.&nbsp; [John 14:15, Ps. 50:23, Job 42:5-6,



<P>Eze. 20:43]&nbsp; And now, Sir, as to this brief description



<P>of the work of grace, and also the discovery of it,



<P>if you have aught to object, object; if not, then give me leave



<P>to propound to you a second question.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{203}



<P>TALK.&nbsp; Nay, my part is not now to object, but to hear;



<P>let me, therefore, have your second question.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Another good sign of grace>



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; It is this:&nbsp; Do you experience this first part



<P>of this description of it? and doth your life and conversation



<P>testify the same? or standeth your religion in word or in tongue,



<P>and not in deed and truth?&nbsp; Pray, if you incline to answer me in

this,



<P>say no more than you know the God above will say Amen to;



<P>and also nothing but what your conscience can justify you in;



<P>for not he that commendeth himself is approved, but whom



<P>the Lord commendeth.&nbsp; Besides, to say I am thus and thus,



<P>when my conversation, and all my neighbours, tell me I lie,



<P>is great wickedness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Talkative not pleased with Faithful's question>



<P>{204}



<P>TALK.&nbsp; Then Talkative at first began to blush; but, recovering

himself,



<P>thus he replied:&nbsp; You come now to experience, to conscience, and

God;



<P>and to appeal to him for justification of what is spoken.



<P>This kind of discourse I did not expect; nor am I disposed



<P>to give an answer to such questions, because I count not myself



<P>bound thereto, unless you take upon you to be a catechiser, and,



<P>though you should so do, yet I may refuse to make you my judge.



<P>But, I pray, will you tell me why you ask me such questions?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{205}



<P>&lt;The reasons why Faithful put to him that question>



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; Because I saw you forward to talk, and because I knew not



<P>that you had aught else but notion.&nbsp; Besides, to tell you all the

truth,



<P>I have heard of you, that you are a man whose religion lies in talk,



<P>and that your conversation gives this your mouth-profession the lie.



<P>&lt;Faithful's plain dealing with Talkative>&nbsp; They say, you are

a spot



<P>among Christians; and that religion fareth the worse



<P>for your ungodly conversation; that some have already stumbled at your



<P>wicked ways, and that more are in danger of being destroyed thereby;



<P>your religion, and an ale-house, and covetousness, and uncleanness,



<P>and swearing, and lying, and vain-company keeping, &amp;c.,



<P>will stand together.&nbsp; The proverb is true of you which is said



<P>of a whore, to wit, that she is a shame to all women;



<P>so are you a shame to all professors.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Talkative flings away from Faithful>



<P>TALK.&nbsp; Since you are ready to take up reports and to judge



<P>so rashly as you do, I cannot but conclude you are some



<P>peevish or melancholy man, not fit to be discoursed with; and so adieu.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{206}



<P>&lt;A good riddance>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then came up Christian, and said to his brother,



<P>I told you how it would happen:&nbsp; your words and his lusts



<P>could not agree; he had rather leave your company than reform his life.



<P>But he is gone, as I said; let him go, the loss is no man's but his

own;



<P>he has saved us the trouble of going from him; for he continuing



<P>(as I suppose he will do) as he is, he would have been but a blot in



<P>our company:&nbsp; besides, the apostle says, "From such withdraw thyself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; But I am glad we had this little discourse with him;



<P>it may happen that he will think of it again:&nbsp; however,



<P>I have dealt plainly with him, and so am clear of his blood,



<P>if he perisheth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{207}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; You did well to talk so plainly to him as you did;



<P>there is but little of this faithful dealing with men now-a-days,



<P>and that makes religion to stink so in the nostrils of many, as it doth;



<P>for they are these talkative fools whose religion is only in word,



<P>and are debauched and vain in their conversation, that



<P>(being so much admitted into the fellowship of the godly)



<P>do puzzle the world, blemish Christianity, and grieve the sincere.



<P>I wish that all men would deal with such as you have done:



<P>then should they either be made more conformable to religion,



<P>or the company of saints would be too hot for them.&nbsp; Then did



<P>Faithful say,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; How Talkative at first lifts up his plumes!



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; How bravely doth he speak!&nbsp; How he presumes



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; To drive down all before him!&nbsp; But so

soon



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; As Faithful talks of heart-work, like the moon



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; That's past the full, into the wane he goes.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And so will all, but he that HEART-WORK knows.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{208}



<P>Thus they went on talking of what they had seen by the way,



<P>and so made that way easy which would otherwise, no doubt,



<P>have been tedious to them; for now they went through a wilderness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{209}



<P>&lt;Evangelist overtakes them again>



<P>Now, when they were got almost quite out of this wilderness,



<P>Faithful chanced to cast his eye back, and espied one coming after them,



<P>and he knew him.&nbsp; Oh! said Faithful to his brother, who comes yonder?



<P>Then Christian looked, and said, It is my good friend Evangelist.



<P>Ay, and my good friend too, said Faithful, for it was he that set me



<P>in the way to the gate.&nbsp; Now was Evangelist come up to them,



<P>and thus saluted them:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{210}



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; Peace be with you, dearly beloved; and peace be to your

helpers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;They are glad at the sight of him>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Welcome, welcome, my good Evangelist, the sight of thy countenance



<P>brings to my remembrance thy ancient kindness and unwearied labouring



<P>for my eternal good.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; And a thousand times welcome, said good Faithful.&nbsp;

Thy company,



<P>O sweet Evangelist, how desirable it is to us poor pilgrims!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; Then said Evangelist, How hath it fared with you, my friends,



<P>since the time of our last parting?&nbsp; What have you met with,



<P>and how have you behaved yourselves?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{211}



<P>Then Christian and Faithful told him of all things that had happened



<P>to them in the way; and how, and with what difficulty,



<P>they had arrived at that place.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{212}



<P>&lt;His exhortation to them>



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; Right glad am I, said Evangelist, not that you have met



<P>with trials, but that you have been victors; and for that you have,



<P>notwithstanding many weaknesses, continued in the way to this very day.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I say, right glad am I of this thing, and that for mine own sake



<P>and yours.&nbsp; I have sowed, and you have reaped:&nbsp; and the day

is coming,



<P>when both he that sowed and they that reaped shall rejoice together;



<P>that is, if you hold out:&nbsp; "for in due season ye shall reap,



<P>if ye faint not."&nbsp; [John 4:36, Gal. 6:9]&nbsp; The crown is before

you,



<P>and it is an incorruptible one; so run, that you may obtain it.



<P>[1 Cor. 9:24-27]&nbsp; Some there be that set out for this crown,



<P>and, after they have gone far for it, another comes in,



<P>and takes it from them:&nbsp; hold fast, therefore, that you have;



<P>let no man take your crown.&nbsp; [Rev. 3:11]&nbsp; You are not yet

out



<P>of the gun-shot of the devil; you have not resisted unto blood,



<P>striving against sin; let the kingdom be always before you,



<P>and believe steadfastly concerning things that are invisible.



<P>Let nothing that is on this side the other world get within you;



<P>and, above all, look well to your own hearts, and to the lusts thereof,



<P>"for they are deceitful above all things, and desperately wicked";



<P>set your faces like a flint; you have all power in heaven and earth



<P>on your side.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{213}



<P>&lt;They do thank him for his exhortation>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then Christian thanked him for his exhortation; but told

him,



<P>withal, that they would have him speak further to them for their help



<P>the rest of the way, and the rather, for that they well knew



<P>that he was a prophet, and could tell them of things that might



<P>happen unto them, and also how they might resist and overcome them.



<P>To which request Faithful also consented.&nbsp; So Evangelist began



<P>as followeth:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;He predicteth what troubles they shall meet with in Vanity Fair,



<P>and encourageth them to steadfastness>



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; My sons, you have heard, in the words of the truth of the

gospel,



<P>that you must, through many tribulations, enter into



<P>the kingdom of heaven.&nbsp; And, again, that in every city



<P>bonds and afflictions abide in you; and therefore you cannot expect



<P>that you should go long on your pilgrimage without them,



<P>in some sort or other.&nbsp; You have found something of the truth



<P>of these testimonies upon you already, and more will immediately follow;



<P>for now, as you see, you are almost out of this wilderness,



<P>and therefore you will soon come into a town that you will by and by



<P>see before you; and in that town you will be hardly beset with enemies,



<P>who will strain hard but they will kill you; and be you sure



<P>that one or both of you must seal the testimony which you hold,



<P>with blood; but be you faithful unto death, and the King will give you



<P>a crown of life.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{214}



<P>&lt;He whose lot it will be there to suffer,



<P>will have the better of his brother>



<P>He that shall die there, although his death will be unnatural,



<P>and his pain perhaps great, he will yet have the better of his fellow;



<P>not only because he will be arrived at the Celestial City soonest,



<P>but because he will escape many miseries that the other will meet with



<P>in the rest of his journey.&nbsp; But when you are come to the town,



<P>and shall find fulfilled what I have here related,



<P>then remember your friend, and quit yourselves like men,



<P>and commit the keeping of your souls to your God in well-doing,



<P>as unto a faithful Creator.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{215}



<P>Then I saw in my dream, that when they were got out of the wilderness,



<P>they presently saw a town before them, and the name of that town



<P>is Vanity; and at the town there is a fair kept, called Vanity Fair:



<P>it is kept all the year long.&nbsp; It beareth the name of Vanity Fair



<P>because the town where it is kept is lighter than vanity;



<P>and, also because all that is there sold, or that cometh thither,



<P>is vanity.&nbsp; As is the saying of the wise, "all that cometh is vanity."



<P>[Eccl. 1; 2:11,17; 11:8; Isa. 11:17]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{216}



<P>This fair is no new-erected business, but a thing of ancient standing;



<P>I will show you the original of it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;The antiquity of this fair>



<P>Almost five thousand years agone, there were pilgrims walking



<P>to the Celestial City, as these two honest persons are:



<P>and Beelzebub, Apollyon, and Legion, with their companions,



<P>perceiving by the path that the pilgrims made, that their way



<P>to the city lay through this town of Vanity, they contrived here



<P>to set up a fair; a fair wherein, should be sold all sorts of vanity,



<P>and that it should last all the year long:&nbsp; therefore at this fair



<P>are all such merchandise sold, as houses, lands, trades, places,



<P>honours, preferments, titles, countries, kingdoms, lusts, pleasures,



<P>and delights of all sorts, as whores, bawds, wives, husbands, children,



<P>masters, servants, lives, blood, bodies, souls, silver, gold, pearls,



<P>precious stones, and what not.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And, moreover, at this fair there is at all times to be seen



<P>juggling cheats, games, plays, fools, apes, knaves, and rogues,



<P>and that of every kind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Here are to be seen, too, and that for nothing, thefts, murders,



<P>adulteries, false swearers, and that of a blood-red colour.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{217}



<P>&lt;The streets of this fair>



<P>And as in other fairs of less moment, there are the several



<P>rows and streets, under their proper names, where such and such wares



<P>are vended; so here likewise you have the proper places, rows,



<P>streets, (viz. countries and kingdoms), where the wares of this fair



<P>are soonest to be found.&nbsp; Here is the Britain Row, the French Row,



<P>the Italian Row, the Spanish Row, the German Row, where several sorts



<P>of vanities are to be sold.&nbsp; But, as in other fairs, some one commodity



<P>is as the chief of all the fair, so the ware of Rome and her merchandise



<P>is greatly promoted in this fair; only our English nation,



<P>with some others, have taken a dislike thereat.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{218}



<P>&lt;Christ went through this fair>



<P>Now, as I said, the way to the Celestial City lies just through



<P>this town where this lusty fair is kept; and he that will go



<P>to the city, and yet not go through this town, must needs go out



<P>of the world.&nbsp; [1 Cor. 5:10]&nbsp; &lt;Christ bought nothing in
