this fair>



<P>The Prince of princes himself, when here, went through this town



<P>to his own country, and that upon a fair day too; yea, and as I think,



<P>it was Beelzebub, the chief lord of this fair, that invited him



<P>to buy of his vanities; yea, would have made him lord of the fair,



<P>would he but have done him reverence as he went through the town.



<P>[Matt. 4:8, Luke 4:5-7]&nbsp; Yea, because he was such a person of honour,



<P>Beelzebub had him from street to street, and showed him



<P>all the kingdoms of the world in a little time, that he might,



<P>if possible, allure the Blessed One to cheapen and buy some



<P>of his vanities; but he had no mind to the merchandise,



<P>and therefore left the town, without laying out so much



<P>as one farthing upon these vanities.&nbsp; This fair, therefore,



<P>is an ancient thing, of long standing, and a very great fair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{219}



<P>&lt;The pilgrims enter the fair>



<P>Now these pilgrims, as I said, must needs go through this fair.



<P>&lt;The fair in a hubbub about them>&nbsp; Well, so they did:&nbsp;

but, behold,



<P>even as they entered into the fair, all the people in the fair



<P>were moved, and the town itself as it were in a hubbub about them;



<P>and that for several reasons:&nbsp; for--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{220}



<P>&lt;The first cause of the hubbub>



<P>First, The pilgrims were clothed with such kind of raiment



<P>as was diverse from the raiment of any that traded in that fair.



<P>The people, therefore, of the fair, made a great gazing upon them:



<P>some said they were fools, some they were bedlams,



<P>and some they are outlandish men.&nbsp; [1 Cor. 2:7-8]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{221}



<P>&lt;Second cause of the hubbub>



<P>Secondly, And as they wondered at their apparel, so they did likewise



<P>at their speech; for few could understand what they said;



<P>they naturally spoke the language of Canaan, but they that kept the

fair



<P>were the men of this world; so that, from one end of the fair



<P>to the other, they seemed barbarians each to the other.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{222}



<P>&lt;Third cause of the hubbub>



<P>Thirdly, But that which did not a little amuse the merchandisers was,



<P>that these pilgrims set very light by all their wares; they cared not



<P>so much as to look upon them; and if they called upon them to buy,



<P>they would put their fingers in their ears, and cry,



<P>Turn away mine eyes from beholding vanity, and look upwards,



<P>signifying that their trade and traffic was in heaven.&nbsp; [Ps. 119:37,



<P>Phil. 3:19-20]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{223}



<P>&lt;Fourth cause of the hubbub>



<P>One chanced mockingly, beholding the carriage of the men,



<P>to say unto them, What will ye buy?&nbsp; But they, looking gravely

upon him,



<P>answered, "We buy the truth."&nbsp; [Prov. 23:23]&nbsp; &lt;They are

mocked>



<P>At that there was an occasion taken to despise the men the more;



<P>some mocking, some taunting, some speaking reproachfully,



<P>and some calling upon others to smite them.&nbsp; &lt;The fair in a

hubbub>



<P>At last things came to a hubbub and great stir in the fair,



<P>insomuch that all order was confounded.&nbsp; Now was word presently

brought



<P>to the great one of the fair, who quickly came down,



<P>and deputed some of his most trusty friends to take these men



<P>into examination, about whom the fair was almost overturned.



<P>&lt;They are examined>&nbsp; So the men were brought to examination;



<P>and they that sat upon them, asked them whence they came,



<P>whither they went, and what they did there, in such an unusual garb?



<P>&lt;They tell who they are, and whence they came>&nbsp; The men told

them



<P>that they were pilgrims and strangers in the world, and that they



<P>were going to their own country, which was the heavenly Jerusalem,



<P>[Heb. 11:13-16] and that they had given no occasion to the men



<P>of the town, nor yet to the merchandisers, thus to abuse them,



<P>and to let them in their journey, except it was for that,



<P>when one asked them what they would buy, they said they would buy



<P>the truth.&nbsp; &lt;They are not believed>&nbsp; But they that were

appointed to



<P>examine them did not believe them to be any other than bedlams and mad,



<P>or else such as came to put all things into a confusion in the fair.



<P>&lt;They are put in the cage>&nbsp; Therefore they took them and beat

them,



<P>and besmeared them with dirt, and then put them into the cage,



<P>that they might be made a spectacle to all the men of the fair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Behold Vanity Fair! the Pilgrims there



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Are chain'd and stand beside:



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Even so it was our Lord pass'd here,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And on Mount Calvary died.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{224}



<P>&lt;Their behaviour in the cage>



<P>There, therefore, they lay for some time, and were made the objects



<P>of any man's sport, or malice, or revenge, the great one of the fair



<P>laughing still at all that befell them.&nbsp; But the men being patient,



<P>and not rendering railing for railing, but contrariwise, blessing,



<P>and good words for bad, and kindness for injuries done,



<P>some men in the fair that were more observing, and less prejudiced



<P>than the rest, began to check and blame the baser sort



<P>for their continual abuses done by them to the men; they, therefore,



<P>in angry manner, let fly at them again, counting them as bad



<P>as the men in the cage, and telling them that they seemed confederates,



<P>and should be made partakers of their misfortunes.



<P>&lt;The men of the fair do fall out among themselves about these two

men>



<P>The other replied that, for aught they could see, the men were quiet,



<P>and sober, and intended nobody any harm; and that there were many



<P>that traded in their fair that were more worthy to be put into the cage,



<P>yea, and pillory too, than were the men they had abused.&nbsp; Thus,



<P>after divers words had passed on both sides, the men behaving themselves



<P>all the while very wisely and soberly before them,



<P>they fell to some blows among themselves, and did harm one to another.



<P>&lt;They are made the authors of this disturbance>&nbsp; Then were these



<P>two poor men brought before their examiners again, and there charged



<P>as being guilty of the late hubbub that had been in the fair.



<P>&lt;They are led up and down the fair in chains, for a terror to others>



<P>So they beat them pitifully, and hanged irons upon them,



<P>and led them in chains up and down the fair, for an example and a terror



<P>to others, lest any should speak in their behalf, or join themselves



<P>unto them.&nbsp; &lt;Some of the men of the fair won to them>



<P>But Christian and Faithful behaved themselves yet more wisely,



<P>and received the ignominy and shame that was cast upon them,



<P>with so much meekness and patience, that it won to their side,



<P>though but few in comparison of the rest, several of the men



<P>in the fair.&nbsp; This put the other party yet into greater rage,



<P>insomuch that they concluded the death of these two men.



<P>&lt;Their adversaries resolve to kill them>&nbsp; Wherefore they threatened,



<P>that the cage nor irons should serve their turn, but that they



<P>should die, for the abuse they had done, and for deluding the men



<P>of the fair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then were they remanded to the cage again, until further order should



<P>be taken with them.&nbsp; So they put them in, and made their feet fast



<P>in the stocks.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{225}



<P>Here, therefore, they called again to mind what they had heard



<P>from their faithful friend Evangelist, and were the more confirmed



<P>in their way and sufferings by what he told them would happen to them.



<P>They also now comforted each other, that whose lot it was to suffer,



<P>even he should have the best of it; therefore each man secretly wished



<P>that he might have that preferment:&nbsp; but committing themselves

to



<P>the all-wise disposal of Him that ruleth all things, with much content,



<P>they abode in the condition in which they were, until they should be



<P>otherwise disposed of.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{226}



<P>&lt;They are again put into the cage, and after brought to trial>



<P>Then a convenient time being appointed, they brought them forth



<P>to their trial, in order to their condemnation.&nbsp; When the time

was come,



<P>they were brought before their enemies and arraigned.&nbsp; The judge's

name



<P>was Lord Hate-good.&nbsp; Their indictment was one and the same in substance,



<P>though somewhat varying in form, the contents whereof were this:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{227}



<P>&lt;Their indictment>



<P>"That they were enemies to and disturbers of their trade;



<P>that they had made commotions and divisions in the town,



<P>and had won a party to their own most dangerous opinions,



<P>in contempt of the law of their prince."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Now, FAITHFUL, play the man, speak for thy

God:



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Fear not the wicked's malice; nor their rod:



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Speak boldly, man, the truth is on thy side:



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Die for it, and to life in triumph ride.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{228}



<P>&lt;Faithful's answer for himself>



<P>Then Faithful began to answer, that he had only set himself against



<P>that which hath set itself against Him that is higher than the highest.



<P>And, said he, as for disturbance, I make none, being myself



<P>a man of peace; the parties that were won to us, were won by beholding



<P>our truth and innocence, and they are only turned from the worse



<P>to the better. And as to the king you talk of, since he is Beelzebub,



<P>the enemy of our Lord, I defy him and all his angels.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{229}



<P>Then proclamation was made, that they that had aught to say



<P>for their lord the king against the prisoner at the bar,



<P>should forthwith appear and give in their evidence. So there came in



<P>three witnesses, to wit, Envy, Superstition, and Pickthank.



<P>They were then asked if they knew the prisoner at the bar;



<P>and what they had to say for their lord the king against him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{230}



<P>&lt;Envy begins>



<P>Then stood forth Envy, and said to this effect:&nbsp; My Lord,



<P>I have known this man a long time, and will attest upon my oath



<P>before this honourable bench, that he is--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>JUDGE.&nbsp; Hold!&nbsp; Give him his oath.&nbsp; (So they sware him.)&nbsp;

Then he said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>ENVY.&nbsp; My Lord, this man, notwithstanding his plausible name,



<P>is one of the vilest men in our country.&nbsp; He neither regardeth



<P>prince nor people, law nor custom; but doth all that he can



<P>to possess all men with certain of his disloyal notions,



<P>which he in the general calls principles of faith and holiness.



<P>And, in particular, I heard him once myself affirm that Christianity



<P>and the customs of our town of Vanity were diametrically opposite,



<P>and could not be reconciled.&nbsp; By which saying, my Lord,



<P>he doth at once not only condemn all our laudable doings,



<P>but us in the doing of them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>JUDGE.&nbsp; Then did the Judge say to him, Hast thou any more to say?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>ENVY.&nbsp; My Lord, I could say much more, only I would not be tedious



<P>to the court.&nbsp; Yet, if need be, when the other gentlemen have given

in



<P>their evidence, rather than anything shall be wanting



<P>that will despatch him, I will enlarge my testimony against him.



<P>So he was bid to stand by.&nbsp; Then they called Superstition,



<P>and bid him look upon the prisoner.&nbsp; They also asked, what he could

say



<P>for their lord the king against him.&nbsp; Then they sware him; so he

began.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{231}



<P>&lt;Superstition follows>



<P>SUPER.&nbsp; My Lord, I have no great acquaintance with this man,



<P>nor do I desire to have further knowledge of him; however, this I know,



<P>that he is a very pestilent fellow, from some discourse that,



<P>the other day, I had with him in this town; for then, talking with him,



<P>I heard him say, that our religion was naught, and such by which



<P>a man could by no means please God.&nbsp; Which sayings of his, my Lord,



<P>your Lordship very well knows, what necessarily thence will follow,



<P>to wit, that we do still worship in vain, are yet in our sins,



<P>and finally shall be damned; and this is that which I have to say.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{232}



<P>Then was Pickthank sworn, and bid say what he knew,



<P>in behalf of their lord the king, against the prisoner at the bar.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Pickthank's testimony>



<P>Pick. My Lord, and you gentlemen all, This fellow I have known



<P>of a long time, and have heard him speak things that ought not



<P>to be spoke; for he hath railed on our noble prince Beelzebub,



<P>and hath spoken contemptibly of his honourable friends,



<P>&lt;Sins are all lords and great ones> whose names are



<P>the Lord Old Man, the Lord Carnal Delight, the Lord Luxurious,



<P>the Lord Desire of Vain Glory, my old Lord Lechery, Sir Having Greedy,



<P>with all the rest of our nobility; and he hath said, moreover,



<P>That if all men were of his mind, if possible, there is not



<P>one of these noblemen should have any longer a being in this town.



<P>Besides, he hath not been afraid to rail on you, my Lord,



<P>who are now appointed to be his judge, calling you an ungodly villain,



<P>with many other such like vilifying terms, with which



<P>he hath bespattered most of the gentry of our town.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{233}



<P>When this Pickthank had told his tale, the Judge directed his speech



<P>to the prisoner at the bar, saying, Thou runagate, heretic, and traitor,



<P>hast thou heard what these honest gentlemen have witnessed against thee?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; May I speak a few words in my own defence?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>JUDGE.&nbsp; Sirrah! sirrah! thou deservest to live no longer,



<P>but to be slain immediately upon the place; yet, that all men may see



<P>our gentleness towards thee, let us hear what thou, vile runagate,



<P>hast to say.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{234}



<P>&lt;Faithful's defence of himself>



<P>FAITH.&nbsp; 1.&nbsp; I say, then, in answer to what Mr. Envy hath spoken,



<P>I never said aught but this, That what rule, or laws, or customs,



<P>or people, were flat against the Word of God, are diametrically opposite



<P>to Christianity.&nbsp; If I have said amiss in this, convince me of

my error,



<P>and I am ready here before you to make my recantation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{235}



<P>2.&nbsp; As to the second, to wit, Mr. Superstition, and his charge



<P>against me, I said only this, That in the worship of God



<P>there is required a Divine faith; but there can be no Divine faith



<P>without a Divine revelation of the will of God.&nbsp; Therefore,



<P>whatever is thrust into the worship of God that is not agreeable



<P>to Divine revelation, cannot be done but by a human faith,



<P>which faith will not be profitable to eternal life.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{236}



<P>3. As to what Mr. Pickthank hath said, I say (avoiding terms,



<P>as that I am said to rail, and the like) that the prince of this town,



<P>with all the rabblement, his attendants, by this gentleman named,



<P>are more fit for a being in hell, than in this town and country:



<P>and so, the Lord have mercy upon me!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{237}



<P>&lt;The Judge's speech to the jury>



<P>Then the Judge called to the jury (who all this while stood by,



<P>to hear and observe):&nbsp; Gentlemen of the jury, you see this man



<P>about whom so great an uproar hath been made in this town.



<P>You have also heard what these worthy gentlemen have witnessed



<P>against him.&nbsp; Also you have heard his reply and confession.



<P>It lieth now in your breasts to hang him or save his life;



<P>but yet I think meet to instruct you into our law.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{238}



<P>There was an Act made in the days of Pharaoh the Great,



<P>servant to our prince, that lest those of a contrary religion



<P>should multiply and grow too strong for him, their males should be



<P>thrown into the river.&nbsp; [Exo. 1:22]&nbsp; There was also an Act

made



<P>in the days of Nebuchadnezzar the Great, another of his servants,



<P>that whosoever would not fall down and worship his golden image,



<P>should be thrown into a fiery furnace.&nbsp; [Dan. 3:6]&nbsp; There

was also



<P>an Act made in the days of Darius, that whoso, for some time,



<P>called upon any god but him, should be cast into the lions' den.



<P>[Dan. 6]&nbsp; Now the substance of these laws this rebel has broken,



<P>not only in thought, (which is not to be borne), but also



<P>in word and deed; which must therefore needs be intolerable.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{239}



<P>For that of Pharaoh, his law was made upon a supposition,



<P>to prevent mischief, no crime being yet apparent;



<P>but here is a crime apparent.&nbsp; For the second and third,



<P>you see he disputeth against our religion; and for the treason



<P>he hath confessed, he deserveth to die the death.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{240}



<P>&lt;The jury and their names>



<P>Then went the jury out, whose names were, Mr. Blind-man, Mr. No-good,



<P>Mr. Malice, Mr. Love-lust, Mr. Live-loose, Mr. Heady, Mr. High-mind,



<P>Mr. Enmity, Mr. Liar, Mr. Cruelty, Mr. Hate-light, and Mr. Implacable;



<P>who every one gave in his private verdict against him among themselves,



<P>and afterwards unanimously concluded to bring him in guilty



<P>before the Judge.&nbsp; &lt;Everyones private verdict>&nbsp; And first,



<P>among themselves, Mr. Blind-man, the foreman, said,



<P>I see clearly that this man is a heretic.&nbsp; Then said Mr. No-good,



<P>Away with such a fellow from the earth.&nbsp; Ay, said Mr. Malice,



<P>for I hate the very looks of him.&nbsp; Then said Mr. Love-lust,



<P>I could never endure him.&nbsp; Nor I, said Mr. Live-loose,



<P>for he would always be condemning my way.&nbsp; Hang him, hang him,



<P>said Mr. Heady.&nbsp; A sorry scrub, said Mr. High-mind.



<P>My heart riseth against him, said Mr. Enmity.&nbsp; He is a rogue,



<P>said Mr. Liar.&nbsp; Hanging is too good for him, said Mr. Cruelty.



<P>Let us despatch him out of the way, said Mr. Hate-light.



<P>Then said Mr. Implacable, Might I have all the world given me,



<P>I could not be reconciled to him; &lt;They conclude to bring him in

guilty



<P>of death> therefore, let us forthwith bring him in guilty of death.



<P>And so they did; therefore he was presently condemned to be had



<P>from the place where he was, to the place from whence he came,



<P>and there to be put to the most cruel death that could be invented.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{241}



<P>&lt;The cruel death of Faithful>



<P>They therefore brought him out, to do with him according to their law;



<P>and, first, they scourged him, then they buffeted him,



<P>then they lanced his flesh with knives; after that, they stoned him



<P>with stones, then pricked him with their swords; and, last of all,



<P>they burned him to ashes at the stake.&nbsp; Thus came Faithful to his

end.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{242}



<P>&lt;A chariot and horses wait to take away Faithful>



<P>Now I saw that there stood behind the multitude a chariot



<P>and a couple of horses, waiting for Faithful, who (so soon as



<P>his adversaries had despatched him) was taken up into it,



<P>and straightway was carried up through the clouds,



<P>with sound of trumpet, the nearest way to the Celestial Gate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Brave FAITHFUL, bravely done in word

and deed;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Judge, witnesses, and jury have, instead



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Of overcoming thee, but shown their rage:



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When they are dead, thou'lt live from

age to age*.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>*In the New Heaven and New Earth.&nbsp; {footnote from one edition}

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{243}



<P>&lt;Christian is still alive>



<P>But as for Christian, he had some respite, and was remanded



<P>back to prison.&nbsp; So he there remained for a space;



<P>but He that overrules all things, having the power of their rage



<P>in his own hand, so wrought it about, that Christian for that time



<P>escaped them, and went his way.&nbsp; And as he went, he sang, saying--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;The song that Christian made of Faithful after his death>



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Well, Faithful, thou hast faithfully profest



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Unto thy Lord; with whom thou shalt be blest,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When faithless ones, with all their vain delights,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Are crying out under their hellish plights:



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Sing, Faithful, sing, and let thy name survive;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; For though they kill'd thee, thou art yet alive!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{244}



<P>&lt;Christian has another companion>



<P>Now I saw in my dream, that Christian went not forth alone,



<P>for there was one whose name was Hopeful (being made so



<P>by the beholding of Christian and Faithful in their words and behaviour,



<P>in their sufferings at the fair), who joined himself unto him, and,



<P>entering into a brotherly covenant, told him that he would be



<P>his companion.&nbsp; Thus, one died to bear testimony to the truth,



<P>and another rises out of his ashes, to be a companion with Christian



<P>in his pilgrimage.&nbsp; &lt;There are more of the men of the fair will

follow>



<P>This Hopeful also told Christian, that there were many more



<P>of the men in the fair, that would take their time and follow after.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{245}



<P>&lt;They overtake By-ends>



<P>So I saw that quickly after they were got out of the fair,



<P>they overtook one that was going before them, whose name was By-ends:



<P>so they said to him, What countryman, Sir? and how far go you this way?



<P>He told them that he came from the town of Fair-speech,



<P>and he was going to the Celestial City (but told them not his name).

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>From Fair-speech! said Christian.&nbsp; Is there any good that lives

there?



<P>[Prov. 26:25]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; Yes, said By-ends, I hope.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Pray, Sir, what may I call you? said Christian.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;By-ends loath to tell his name>



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; I am a stranger to you, and you to me:&nbsp; if you be

going



<P>this way, I shall be glad of your company; if not, I must be content.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; This town of Fair-speech, said Christian, I have heard of;



<P>and, as I remember, they say it is a wealthy place.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; Yes, I will assure you that it is; and I have very many



<P>rich kindred there.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{246}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Pray, who are your kindred there? if a man may be so bold.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; Almost the whole town; and in particular, my Lord Turn-about,



<P>my Lord Time-server, my Lord Fair-speech, (from whose ancestors



<P>that town first took its name), also Mr. Smooth-man,



<P>Mr. Facing-both-ways, Mr. Any-thing; and the parson of our parish,



<P>Mr. Two-tongues, was my mother's own brother by father's side;



<P>and to tell you the truth, I am become a gentleman of good quality,



<P>yet my great-grandfather was but a waterman, looking one way



<P>and rowing another, and I got most of my estate by the same occupation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Are you a married man?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;The wife and kindred of By-ends>



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; Yes, and my wife is a very virtuous woman,



<P>the daughter of a virtuous woman; she was my Lady Feigning's daughter,



<P>therefore she came of a very honourable family, and is arrived



<P>to such a pitch of breeding, that she knows how to carry it to all,



<P>even to prince and peasant.&nbsp; &lt;Where By-ends differs from others



<P>in religion>&nbsp; It is true we somewhat differ in religion



<P>from those of the stricter sort, yet but in two small points:



<P>first, we never strive against wind and tide; secondly,



<P>we are always most zealous when religion goes in his silver slippers;



<P>we love much to walk with him in the street, if the sun shines,



<P>and the people applaud him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{247}



<P>Then Christian stepped a little aside to his fellow, Hopeful,



<P>saying, It runs in my mind that this is one By-ends of Fair-speech;



<P>and if it be he, we have as very a knave in our company as dwelleth



<P>in all these parts.&nbsp; Then said Hopeful, Ask him; methinks he should

not



<P>be ashamed of his name.&nbsp; So Christian came up with him again, and

said,



<P>Sir, you talk as if you knew something more than all the world doth;



<P>and if I take not my mark amiss, I deem I have half a guess of you:



<P>Is not your name Mr. By-ends, of Fair-speech?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; This is not my name, but indeed it is a nick-name



<P>that is given me by some that cannot abide me:&nbsp; and I must be content



<P>to bear it as a reproach, as other good men have borne theirs before

me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{247}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; But did you never give an occasion to men to call you



<P>by this name?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;How By-ends got his name>



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; Never, never! The worst that ever I did to give them



<P>an occasion to give me this name was, that I had always the luck



<P>to jump in my judgment with the present way of the times,



<P>whatever it was, and my chance was to get thereby; but if things



<P>are thus cast upon me, let me count them, a blessing;



<P>but let not the malicious load me therefore with reproach.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{248}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I thought, indeed, that you were the man that I heard of;



<P>and to tell you what I think, I fear this name belongs to you



<P>more properly than you are willing we should think it doth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;He desires to keep company with Christian>



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; Well, if you will thus imagine, I cannot help it;



<P>you shall find me a fair company-keeper, if you will still admit me



<P>your associate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; If you will go with us, you must go against wind and tide;



<P>the which, I perceive, is against your opinion; you must also own



<P>religion in his rags, as well as when in his silver slippers;



<P>and stand by him, too, when bound in irons, as well as when



<P>he walketh the streets with applause.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; You must not impose, nor lord it over my faith;



<P>leave me to my liberty, and let me go with you.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Not a step further, unless you will do in what I propound

as we.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then said By-ends, I shall never desert my old principles,



<P>since they are harmless and profitable.&nbsp; If I may not go with you,



<P>I must do as I did before you overtook me, even go by myself,



<P>until some overtake me that will be glad of my company.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{249}



<P>&lt;By-ends and Christian part>



<P>Now I saw in my dream that Christian and Hopeful forsook him,



<P>and kept their distance before him; but one of them looking back,



<P>saw three men following Mr. By-ends, and behold, as they



<P>came up with him, he made them a very low conge {conge'};



<P>and they also gave him a compliment.&nbsp; &lt;He has new companions>



<P>The men's names were Mr. Hold-the-world, Mr. Money-love,



<P>and Mr. Save-all; men that Mr. By-ends had formerly been



<P>acquainted with; for in their minority they were schoolfellows,



<P>and were taught by one Mr. Gripe-man, a schoolmaster in Love-gain,



<P>which is a market town in the county of Coveting, in the north.



<P>This schoolmaster taught them the art of getting, either by violence,



<P>cozenage, flattery, lying, or by putting on the guise of religion;



<P>and these four gentlemen had attained much of the art of their master,



<P>so that they could each of them have kept such a school themselves.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{250}



<P>Well, when they had, as I said, thus saluted each other,



<P>Mr. Money-love said to Mr. By-ends, Who are they upon the road



<P>before us? (for Christian and Hopeful were yet within view).

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;By-ends' character of the pilgrims>



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; They are a couple of far countrymen, that, after their

mode,



<P>are going on pilgrimage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>MONEY-LOVE.&nbsp; Alas!&nbsp; Why did they not stay, that we might have

had



<P>their good company? for they, and we, and you, Sir, I hope,



<P>are all going on pilgrimage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>BY-ENDS.&nbsp; We are so, indeed; but the men before us are so rigid,



<P>and love so much their own notions, and do also so lightly esteem



<P>the opinions of others, that let a man be never so godly,



<P>yet if he jumps not with them in all things, they thrust him



<P>quite out of their company.



<P>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B><FONT SIZE=+2></FONT></B>



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Page 51--Page100</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>{51}



<P>&lt;Evangelist convinces Christian of his error>



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; Then, said Evangelist, stand still a little, that I may

show thee



<P>the words of God.&nbsp; So he stood trembling.&nbsp; Then said Evangelist,



<P>"See that ye refuse not him that speaketh.&nbsp; For if they escaped

not



<P>who refused him that spake on earth, much more shall not we escape,



<P>if we turn away from him that speaketh from heaven."&nbsp; [Heb. 12:25]



<P>He said, moreover, "Now the just shall live by faith:&nbsp; but if any

man



<P>draw back, my soul shall have no pleasure in him."&nbsp; [Heb. 10:38]



<P>He also did thus apply them:&nbsp; Thou art the man that art running

into



<P>this misery; thou hast begun to reject the counsel of the Most High,



<P>and to draw back thy foot from the way of peace, even almost



<P>to the hazarding of thy perdition.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{52}



<P>Then Christian fell down at his feet as dead, crying, "Woe is me,



<P>for I am undone!"&nbsp; At the sight of which Evangelist caught him



<P>by the right hand, saying, "All manner of sin and blasphemies



<P>shall be forgiven unto men." [Matt. 12:31, Mark 3:28]



<P>"Be not faithless, but believing."&nbsp; [John 20:27]&nbsp; Then did

Christian



<P>again a little revive, and stood up trembling, as at first,



<P>before Evangelist.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{53}



<P>&lt;Mr. Worldly Wiseman described by Evangelist>



<P>Then Evangelist proceeded, saying, Give more earnest heed



<P>to the things that I shall tell thee of.&nbsp; I will now show thee



<P>who it was that deluded thee, and who it was also to whom he sent thee.



<P>--The man that met thee is one Worldly Wiseman, and rightly is he



<P>so called; partly, because he savoureth only the doctrine of this world,



<P>[1 John 4:5] (therefore he always goes to the town of Morality



<P>to church):&nbsp; and partly because he loveth that doctrine best,



<P>for it saveth him best from the cross.&nbsp; [Gal 6:12]&nbsp; And because



<P>he is of this carnal temper, therefore he seeketh to pervert my ways,



<P>though right.&nbsp; Now there are three things in this man's counsel,



<P>that thou must utterly abhor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Evangelist discovers the deceit of Mr. Worldly Wiseman>

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>1.&nbsp; His turning thee out of the way.



<P>2.&nbsp; His labouring to render the cross odious to thee.&nbsp; And,



<P>3.&nbsp; His setting thy feet in that way that leadeth unto



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; the administration of death.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{54}



<P>First, Thou must abhor his turning thee out of the way;



<P>and thine own consenting thereunto:&nbsp; because this is to reject



<P>the counsel of God for the sake of the counsel of a Worldly Wiseman.



<P>The Lord says, "Strive to enter in at the strait gate" [Luke 13:24],



<P>the gate to which I sent thee; for "strait is the gate that leadeth



<P>unto life, and few there be that find it." [Matt. 7:14]



<P>From this little wicket-gate, and from the way thereto,



<P>hath this wicked man turned thee, to the bringing of thee



<P>almost to destruction; hate, therefore, his turning thee out of the

way,



<P>and abhor thyself for hearkening to him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{55}



<P>Secondly, Thou must abhor his labouring to render the cross



<P>odious unto thee; for thou art to prefer it "before the treasures



<P>in Egypt."&nbsp; [Heb. 11:25,26]&nbsp; Besides the King of glory hath

told thee,



<P>that he that "will save his life shall lose it."&nbsp; [Mark 8:35;



<P>John 12:25; Matt. 10:39]&nbsp; And, "He that cometh after me,



<P>and hateth not his father, and mother, and wife, and children,



<P>and brethren, and sisters, yea, and his own life also,



<P>he cannot be my disciple."&nbsp; [Luke 14:26]&nbsp; I say, therefore,



<P>for man to labour to persuade thee, that that shall be thy death,



<P>without which, THE TRUTH hath said, thou canst not have eternal life;



<P>this doctrine thou must abhor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{56}



<P>Thirdly, Thou must hate his setting of thy feet in the way that leadeth



<P>to the ministration of death.&nbsp; And for this thou must consider

to whom



<P>he sent thee, and also how unable that person was to deliver thee



<P>from thy burden.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{57}



<P>&lt;The bond-woman>



<P>He to whom thou wast sent for ease, being by name Legality,



<P>is the son of the bond-woman which now is, and is in bondage



<P>with her children [Gal 4:21-27]; and is, in a mystery, this Mount Sinai,



<P>which thou hast feared will fall on thy head.&nbsp; Now, if she,



<P>with her children, are in bondage, how canst thou expect by them



<P>to be made free?&nbsp; This Legality, therefore, is not able to set

thee free



<P>from thy burden.&nbsp; No man was as yet ever rid of his burden by him;

no,



<P>nor ever is like to be:&nbsp; ye cannot be justified by the works of

the law;



<P>for by the deeds of the law no man living can be rid of his burden:



<P>therefore, Mr. Worldly Wiseman is an alien, and Mr. Legality is a cheat;



<P>and for his son Civility, notwithstanding his simpering looks,



<P>he is but a hypocrite and cannot help thee.&nbsp; Believe me,



<P>there is nothing in all this noise, that thou hast heard of these



<P>sottish men, but a design to beguile thee of thy salvation,



<P>by turning thee from the way in which I had set thee.&nbsp; After this,



<P>Evangelist called aloud to the heavens for confirmation of what



<P>he had said:&nbsp; and with that there came words and fire



<P>out of the mountain under which poor Christian stood, that made



<P>the hair of his flesh stand up.&nbsp; The words were thus pronounced:



<P>`As many as are of the works of the law are under the curse;



<P>for it is written, Cursed is every one that continueth not in all things



<P>which are written in the book of the law to do them.'&nbsp; [Gal. 3:10]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{58}



<P>Now Christian looked for nothing but death, and began



<P>to cry out lamentably; even cursing the time in which he met



<P>with Mr. Worldly Wiseman; still calling himself a thousand fools



<P>for hearkening to his counsel; he also was greatly ashamed to think



<P>that this gentleman's arguments, flowing only from the flesh,



<P>should have the prevalency with him as to cause him to forsake



<P>the right way.&nbsp; This done, he applied himself again to Evangelist



<P>in words and sense as follow:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{59}



<P>&lt;Christian inquires if he may yet be happy>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Sir, what think you?&nbsp; Is there hope?&nbsp; May I now

go back



<P>and go up to the wicket-gate?&nbsp; Shall I not be abandoned for this,



<P>and sent back from thence ashamed?&nbsp; I am sorry I have hearkened

to



<P>this man's counsel.&nbsp; But may my sin be forgiven?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Evangelist comforts him>



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; Then said Evangelist to him, Thy sin is very great,



<P>for by it thou hast committed two evils:&nbsp; thou hast forsaken the

way



<P>that is good, to tread in forbidden paths; yet will the man at the gate



<P>receive thee, for he has goodwill for men; only, said he,



<P>take heed that thou turn not aside again, `lest thou perish from



<P>the way, when his wrath is kindled but a little.'&nbsp; [Ps. 2:12]



<P>Then did Christian address himself to go back; and Evangelist,



<P>after he had kissed him, gave him one smile, and bid him God-speed.



<P>So he went on with haste, neither spake he to any man by the way;



<P>nor, if any asked him, would he vouchsafe them an answer.



<P>He went like one that was all the while treading on forbidden ground,



<P>and could by no means think himself safe, till again he was got



<P>into the way which he left, to follow Mr. Worldly Wiseman's counsel.



<P>So, in process of time, Christian got up to the gate.



<P>Now, over the gate there was written, `Knock, and it shall be opened



<P>unto you.' [Matt 7:8]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{60}



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "He that will enter in must first without



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Stand knocking at the Gate, nor need he doubt



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; That is A KNOCKER but to enter in;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; For God can love him, and forgive his sin."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He knocked, therefore, more than once or twice, saying--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "May I now enter here?&nbsp; Will he within



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Open to sorry me, though I have been



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; An undeserving rebel?&nbsp; Then shall I



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Not fail to sing his lasting praise on high."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At last there came a grave person to the gate, named Good-will,



<P>who asked who was there? and whence he came? and what he would have?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{61}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Here is a poor burdened sinner.&nbsp; I come from



<P>the City of Destruction, but am going to Mount Zion,



<P>that I may be delivered from the wrath to come.&nbsp; I would therefore,



<P>Sir, since I am informed that by this gate is the way thither,



<P>know if you are willing to let me in?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;The gate will be opened to broken-hearted sinners>



<P>GOOD-WILL.&nbsp; I am willing with all my heart, said he; and with that



<P>he opened the gate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{62}



<P>So when Christian was stepping in, the other gave him a pull.



<P>Then said Christian, What means that?&nbsp; The other told him.



<P>A little distance from this gate, there is erected a strong castle,



<P>of which Beelzebub is the captain; &lt;Satan envies those that enter



<P>the strait gate> from thence, both he and them that are with him



<P>shoot arrows at those that come up to this gate, if haply they may die



<P>before they can enter in.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Christian entered the gate with joy and trembling>



<P>Then said Christian, I rejoice and tremble.&nbsp; So when he was got

in,



<P>the man of the gate asked him who directed him thither?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{63}



<P>&lt;Talk between Good-will and Christian>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Evangelist bid me come hither, and knock, (as I did);



<P>and he said that you, Sir, would tell me what I must do.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>GOOD-WILL.&nbsp; An open door is set before thee, and no man can shut

it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Now I begin to reap the benefits of my hazards.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>GOOD-WILL.&nbsp; But how is it that you came alone?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Because none of my neighbours saw their danger, as I saw

mine.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>GOOD-WILL.&nbsp; Did any of them know of your coming?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes; my wife and children saw me at the first, and called

after me



<P>to turn again; also, some of my neighbours stood crying



<P>and calling after me to return; but I put my fingers in my ears,



<P>and so came on my way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>GOOD-WILL.&nbsp; But did none of them follow you, to persuade you to

go back?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, both Obstinate and Pliable; but when they saw



<P>that they could not prevail, Obstinate went railing back,



<P>but Pliable came with me a little way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>GOOD-WILL.&nbsp; But why did he not come through?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{64}



<P>&lt;A man may have company when he sets out for heaven,



<P>and yet go thither alone>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; We, indeed, came both together, until we came



<P>at the Slough of Despond, into the which we also suddenly fell.



<P>And then was my neighbour, Pliable, discouraged, and would not



<P>venture further.&nbsp; Wherefore, getting out again on that side



<P>next to his own house, he told me I should possess the brave country



<P>alone for him; so he went his way, and I came mine--



<P>he after Obstinate, and I to this gate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>GOOD-WILL.&nbsp; Then said Good-will, Alas, poor man! is the celestial

glory



<P>of so small esteem with him, that he counteth it not worth



<P>running the hazards of a few difficulties to obtain it?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{65}



<P>&lt;Christian accuses himself before the man at the gate>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Truly, said Christian, I have said the truth of Pliable,



<P>and if I should also say all the truth of myself, it will appear



<P>there is no betterment betwixt him and myself.&nbsp; It is true,



<P>he went back to his own house, but I also turned aside to go



<P>in the way of death, being persuaded thereto by the carnal arguments



<P>of one Mr. Worldly Wiseman.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>GOOD-WILL.&nbsp; Oh, did he light upon you?&nbsp; What! he would have

had you



<P>a sought for ease at the hands of Mr. Legality.&nbsp; They are, both

of them,



<P>a very cheat.&nbsp; But did you take his counsel?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, as far as I durst; I went to find out Mr. Legality,



<P>until I thought that the mountain that stands by his house



<P>would have fallen upon my head; wherefore there I was forced to stop.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>GOOD-WILL.&nbsp; That mountain has been the death of many,



<P>and will be the death of many more; it is well you escaped being



<P>by it dashed in pieces.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Why, truly, I do not know what had become of me there,



<P>had not Evangelist happily met me again, as I was musing in the midst



<P>of my dumps; but it was God's mercy that he came to me again, for else



<P>I had never come hither.&nbsp; But now I am come, such a one as I am,



<P>more fit, indeed, for death, by that mountain, than thus to stand



<P>talking with my lord; but, oh, what a favour is this to me,



<P>that yet I am admitted entrance here!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{66}



<P>&lt;Christian comforted again>



<P>GOOD-WILL.&nbsp; We make no objections against any, notwithstanding

all



<P>that they have done before they came hither.&nbsp; They are in no wise



<P>cast out [John vi.37]; and therefore, good Christian, come a little

way



<P>with me, and I will teach thee about the way thou must go.



<P>&lt;Christian directed yet on his way>&nbsp; Look before thee;



<P>dost thou see this narrow way?&nbsp; THAT is the way thou must go;



<P>it was cast up by the patriarchs, prophets, Christ, and his apostles;



<P>and it is as straight as a rule can make it.&nbsp; This is the way



<P>thou must go.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{67}



<P>&lt;Christian afraid of losing his way>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; But, said Christian, are there no turnings or windings by

which



<P>a stranger may lose his way?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>GOOD-WILL.&nbsp; Yes, there are many ways butt down upon this,



<P>and they are crooked and wide.&nbsp; But thus thou mayest distinguish



<P>the right from the wrong, the right only being straight and narrow.



<P>[Matt 7:14]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{68}



<P>&lt;Christian weary of his burden>



<P>Then I saw in my dream that Christian asked him further



<P>if he could not help him off with his burden that was upon his back;



<P>for as yet he had not got rid thereof, nor could he by any means



<P>get it off without help.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;There is no deliverance from guilt and burden of sin,



<P>but by the death and blood of Christ>



<P>He told him, As to thy burden, be content to bear it, until thou comest



<P>to the place of deliverance; for there it will fall from thy back



<P>of itself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{69}



<P>Then Christian began to gird up his loins, and to address himself



<P>to his journey.&nbsp; So the other told him, That by that he was gone



<P>some distance from the gate, he would come at the house



<P>of the Interpreter, at whose door he should knock, and he would show

him



<P>excellent things.&nbsp; Then Christian took his leave of his friend,



<P>and he again bid him God-speed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{70}



<P>&lt;Christian comes to the house of the interpreter>



<P>Then he went on till he came to the house of the Interpreter,



<P>where he knocked over and over; at last one came to the door,



<P>and asked who was there.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{71}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Sir, here is a traveller, who was bid by an acquaintance



<P>of the good-man of this house to call here for my profit;



<P>I would therefore speak with the master of the house.



<P>So he called for the master of the house, who, after a little time,



<P>came to Christian, and asked him what he would have.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;He is entertained>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Sir, said Christian, I am a man that am come from



<P>the City of Destruction, and am going to the Mount Zion;



<P>and I was told by the man that stands at the gate, at the head



<P>of this way, that if I called here, you would show me excellent things,



<P>such as would be a help to me in my journey.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{72}



<P>&lt;Illumination>



<P>INTER.&nbsp; Then said the Interpreter, Come in; I will show that



<P>which will be profitable to thee.&nbsp; So he commanded his man



<P>to light the candle, and bid Christian follow him:&nbsp; so he had him



<P>into a private room, &lt;Christian sees a grave picture> and bid his

man



<P>open a door; the which when he had done, Christian saw the picture



<P>of a very grave person hang up against the wall; and this was



<P>the fashion of it.&nbsp; &lt;The fashion of the picture>&nbsp; It had

eyes



<P>lifted up to heaven, the best of books in his hand, the law of truth



<P>was written upon his lips, the world was behind his back.



<P>It stood as if it pleaded with men, and a crown of gold did hang



<P>over his head.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then said Christian, What meaneth this?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{73}



<P>&lt;The meaning of the picture>



<P>INTER.&nbsp; The man whose picture this is, is one of a thousand;



<P>he can beget children [1 Cor. 4:15], travail in birth with children



<P>[Gal. 4:19], and nurse them himself when they are born.



<P>And whereas thou seest him with his eyes lift up to heaven,



<P>the best of books in his hand, and the law of truth writ on his lips,



<P>it is to show thee that his work is to know and unfold dark things



<P>to sinners; even as also thou seest him stand as if he pleaded with

men:



<P>and whereas thou seest the world as cast behind him, and that a crown



<P>hangs over his head, that is to show thee that slighting and despising



<P>the things that are present, for the love that he hath



<P>to his Master's service, he is sure in the world that comes next



<P>to have glory for his reward.&nbsp; &lt;Why he showed him the picture

first>



<P>Now, said the Interpreter, I have showed thee this picture first,



<P>because the man whose picture this is, is the only man whom



<P>the Lord of the place whither thou art going, hath authorised to be



<P>thy guide in all difficult places thou mayest meet with in the way;



<P>wherefore, take good heed to what I have shewed thee, and bear well



<P>in thy mind what thou hast seen, lest in thy journey thou meet with



<P>some that pretend to lead thee right, but their way goes down to death.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{74}



<P>Then he took him by the hand, and led him into a very large parlour



<P>that was full of dust, because never swept; the which after he had



<P>reviewed a little while, the Interpreter called for a man to sweep.



<P>Now, when he began to sweep, the dust began so abundantly to fly about,



<P>that Christian had almost therewith been choked.&nbsp; Then said



<P>the Interpreter to a damsel that stood by, Bring hither the water,



<P>and sprinkle the room; the which, when she had done,



<P>it was swept and cleansed with pleasure.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{75}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then said Christian, What means this?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>INTER.&nbsp; The Interpreter answered, This parlour is the heart of

a man



<P>that was never sanctified by the sweet grace of the gospel;



<P>the dust is his original sin and inward corruptions, that have defiled



<P>the whole man.&nbsp; He that began to sweep at first, is the Law;



<P>but she that brought water, and did sprinkle it, is the Gospel.



<P>Now, whereas thou sawest, that so soon as the first began to sweep,



<P>the dust did so fly about that the room by him could not be cleansed,



<P>but that thou wast almost choked therewith; this is to shew thee,



<P>that the law, instead of cleansing the heart (by its working) from sin,



<P>doth revive, put strength into, and increase it in the soul,



<P>even as it doth discover and forbid it, for it doth not give power



<P>to subdue.&nbsp; [Rom. 7:6; 1 Cor. 15:56; Rom. 5:20]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{76}



<P>Again, as thou sawest the damsel sprinkle the room with water,



<P>upon which it was cleansed with pleasure; this is to show thee,



<P>that when the gospel comes in the sweet and precious influences thereof



<P>to the heart, then, I say, even as thou sawest the damsel lay the dust



<P>by sprinkling the floor with water, so is sin vanquished and subdued,



<P>and the soul made clean through the faith of it, and consequently fit



<P>for the King of glory to inhabit.&nbsp; [John 15:3; Eph. 5:26; Acts

15:9;



<P>Rom. 16:25,26; John 15:13]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{77}



<P>I saw, moreover, in my dream, that the Interpreter took him by the hand,



<P>and had him into a little room, where sat two little children,



<P>each one in his chair.&nbsp; &lt;He showed him passion and patience>



<P>The name of the eldest was Passion, and the name of the other Patience.



<P>Passion seemed to be much discontented; but Patience was very quiet.



<P>Then Christian asked, What is the reason of the discontent of Passion?



<P>&lt;Passion will have all now.&nbsp; Patience is for waiting>



<P>The Interpreter answered, The Governor of them would have him stay



<P>for his best things till the beginning of the next year;



<P>but he will have all now:&nbsp; but Patience is willing to wait.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Passion has his desire>



<P>Then I saw that one came to Passion, and brought him a bag of treasure,



<P>and poured it down at his feet, the which he took up



<P>and rejoiced therein, and withal laughed Patience to scorn.



<P>&lt;And quickly lavishes all away>&nbsp; But I beheld but a while,



<P>and he had lavished all away, and had nothing left him but rags.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{78}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then said Christian to the Interpreter, Expound this matter



<P>more fully to me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;The matter expounded>



<P>INTER.&nbsp; So he said, These two lads are figures:&nbsp; Passion,

of the men



<P>of this world; and Patience, of the men of that which is to come;



<P>for as here thou seest, Passion will have all now this year,



<P>that is to say, in this world; so are the men of this world,



<P>they must have all their good things now, they cannot stay



<P>till next year, that is until the next world, for their portion of good.



<P>&lt;The worldly man for a bird in the hand>&nbsp; That proverb,



<P>`A bird in the hand is worth two in the bush', is of more authority



<P>with them than are all the Divine testimonies of the good of the world



<P>to come.&nbsp; But as thou sawest that he had quickly lavished all away,



<P>and had presently left him nothing but rags; so will it be with all



<P>such men at the end of this world.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Patience has the best wisdom>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then said Christian, Now I see that Patience has the best

wisdom,



<P>and that upon many accounts.&nbsp; First, because he stays



<P>for the best things.&nbsp; Second, and also because he will have



<P>the glory of his, when the other has nothing but rags.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{79}



<P>&lt;Things that are first must give place; but things that are last



<P>are lasting>



<P>INTER.&nbsp; Nay, you may add another, to wit, the glory of the next

world



<P>will never wear out; but these are suddenly gone.&nbsp; Therefore Passion



<P>had not so much reason to laugh at Patience, because he had



<P>his good things first, as Patience will have to laugh at Passion,



<P>because he had his best things last; for first must give place to last,



<P>because last must have his time to come; but last gives place



<P>to nothing; for there is not another to succeed.&nbsp; He, therefore,



<P>that hath his portion first, must needs have a time to spend it;



<P>but he that hath his portion last, must have it lastingly;



<P>&lt;Dives had his good things first> therefore it is said of Dives,



<P>"Thou in thy life-time receivedst thy good things, and likewise



<P>Lazarus evil things; but now he is comforted, and thou art tormented."



<P>[Luke 16:25]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then I perceive it is not best to covet things that are now,



<P>but to wait for things to come.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;The first things are but temporal>



<P>INTER.&nbsp; You say the truth:&nbsp; "For the things which are seen

are temporal;



<P>but the things which are not seen are eternal."&nbsp; [2 Cor. 4:18]



<P>But though this be so, yet since things present and our fleshly appetite



<P>are such near neighbours one to another; and again,



<P>because things to come, and carnal sense, are such strangers



<P>one to another; therefore it is, that the first of these so suddenly



<P>fall into amity, and that distance is so continued between the second.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{80}



<P>Then I saw in my dream that the Interpreter took Christian by the hand,



<P>and led him into a place where was a fire burning against a wall,



<P>and one standing by it, always casting much water upon it, to quench

it;



<P>yet did the fire burn higher and hotter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then said Christian, What means this?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{81}



<P>The Interpreter answered, This fire is the work of grace that is



<P>wrought in the heart; he that casts water upon it, to extinguish



<P>and put it out, is the Devil; but in that thou seest the fire



<P>notwithstanding burn higher and hotter, thou shalt also see



<P>the reason of that.&nbsp; So he had him about to the backside of the

wall,



<P>where he saw a man with a vessel of oil in his hand, of the which



<P>he did also continually cast, but secretly, into the fire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then said Christian, What means this?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{82}



<P>The Interpreter answered, This is Christ, who continually,



<P>with the oil of his grace, maintains the work already begun



<P>in the heart:&nbsp; by the means of which, notwithstanding what the

devil



<P>can do, the souls of his people prove gracious still.&nbsp; [2 Cor.

12:9]



<P>And in that thou sawest that the man stood behind the wall to maintain



<P>the fire, that is to teach thee that it is hard for the tempted



<P>to see how this work of grace is maintained in the soul.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I saw also, that the Interpreter took him again by the hand,



<P>and led him into a pleasant place, where was builded a stately palace,



<P>beautiful to behold; at the sight of which Christian



<P>was greatly delighted.&nbsp; He saw also, upon the top thereof,



<P>certain persons walking, who were clothed all in gold.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then said Christian, May we go in thither?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{83}



<P>&lt;The valiant man>



<P>Then the Interpreter took him, and led him up towards the door



<P>of the palace; and behold, at the door stood a great company of men,



<P>as desirous to go in; but durst not.&nbsp; There also sat a man



<P>at a little distance from the door, at a table-side, with a book



<P>and his inkhorn before him, to take the name of him that should



<P>enter therein; he saw also, that in the doorway stood many men in armour



<P>to keep it, being resolved to do the men that would enter



<P>what hurt and mischief they could.&nbsp; Now was Christian somewhat

in amaze.



<P>At last, when every man started back for fear of the armed men,



<P>Christian saw a man of a very stout countenance come up to the man



<P>that sat there to write, saying, Set down my name, Sir:&nbsp; the which



<P>when he had done, he saw the man draw his sword, and put a helmet



<P>upon his head, and rush toward the door upon the armed men, who laid



<P>upon him with deadly force; but the man, not at all discouraged,



<P>fell to cutting and hacking most fiercely.&nbsp; So after he had received



<P>and given many wounds to those that attempted to keep him out,



<P>he cut his way through them all [Acts 14:.22], and pressed forward



<P>into the palace, at which there was a pleasant voice heard from those



<P>that were within, even of those that walked upon the top of the palace,



<P>saying--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Come in, come in;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Eternal glory thou shalt win."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So he went in, and was clothed with such garments as they.



<P>Then Christian smiled and said; I think verily I know the meaning



<P>of this.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{84}



<P>&lt;Despair like an iron cage>



<P>Now, said Christian, let me go hence.&nbsp; Nay, stay, said the Interpreter,



<P>till I have shewed thee a little more, and after that thou shalt go



<P>on thy way.&nbsp; So he took him by the hand again, and led him into



<P>a very dark room, where there sat a man in an iron cage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Now the man, to look on, seemed very sad; he sat with his eyes



<P>looking down to the ground, his hands folded together,



<P>and he sighed as if he would break his heart.&nbsp; Then said Christian,



<P>What means this?&nbsp; At which the Interpreter bid him talk with the

man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then said Christian to the man, What art thou?&nbsp; The man answered,



<P>I am what I was not once.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{85}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; What wast thou once?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>MAN.&nbsp; The man said, I was once a fair and flourishing professor,



<P>both in mine own eyes, and also in the eyes of others; I once was,



<P>as I thought, fair for the Celestial City, and had then even joy



<P>at the thoughts that I should get thither.&nbsp; [Luke 8:13]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Well, but what art thou now?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>MAN.&nbsp; I am now a man of despair, and am shut up in it,



<P>as in this iron cage.&nbsp; I cannot get out.&nbsp; Oh, now I cannot!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; But how camest thou in this condition?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>MAN.&nbsp; I left off to watch and be sober.&nbsp; I laid the reins,



<P>upon the neck of my lusts; I sinned against the light of the Word



<P>and the goodness of God; I have grieved the Spirit, and he is gone;



<P>I tempted the devil, and he is come to me; I have provoked God to anger,



<P>and he has left me:&nbsp; I have so hardened my heart, that I cannot

repent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{86}



<P>Then said Christian to the Interpreter, But is there no hope for such



<P>a man as this?&nbsp; Ask him, said the Interpreter.&nbsp; Nay, said

Christian,



<P>pray, Sir, do you.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>INTER.&nbsp; Then said the Interpreter, Is there no hope, but you must

be



<P>kept in the iron cage of despair?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>MAN.&nbsp; No, none at all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>INTER.&nbsp; Why, the Son of the Blessed is very pitiful.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>MAN.&nbsp; I have crucified him to myself afresh [Heb. 6:6];



<P>I have despised his person [Luke 19:14]; I have despised



<P>his righteousness; I have "counted his blood an unholy thing";



<P>I have "done despite to the Spirit of grace".&nbsp; [Heb. 10:28-29]



<P>Therefore I have shut myself out of all the promises,



<P>and there now remains to me nothing but threatenings,



<P>dreadful threatenings, fearful threatenings, of certain judgement



<P>and fiery indignation, which shall devour me as an adversary.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{87}



<P>INTER.&nbsp; For what did you bring yourself into this condition?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>MAN.&nbsp; For the lusts, pleasures, and profits of this world;



<P>in the enjoyment of which I did then promise myself much delight;



<P>but now every one of those things also bite me, and gnaw me



<P>like a burning worm.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>INTER.&nbsp; But canst thou not now repent and turn?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{88}



<P>MAN.&nbsp; God hath denied me repentance.&nbsp; His Word gives me no

encouragement



<P>to believe; yea, himself hath shut me up in this iron cage;



<P>nor can all the men in the world let me out.&nbsp; O eternity, eternity!



<P>how shall I grapple with the misery that I must meet with in eternity!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>INTER.&nbsp; Then said the Interpreter to Christian, Let this man's

misery



<P>be remembered by thee, and be an everlasting caution to thee.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Well, said Christian, this is fearful!&nbsp; God help me

to watch and



<P>be sober, and to pray that I may shun the cause of this man's misery!



<P>Sir, is it not time for me to go on my way now?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>INTER.&nbsp; Tarry till I shall show thee one thing more, and then thou

shalt



<P>go on thy way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{89}



<P>So he took Christian by the hand again, and led him into a chamber,



<P>where there was one rising out of bed; and as he put on his raiment,



<P>he shook and trembled.&nbsp; Then said Christian, Why doth this man



<P>thus tremble?&nbsp; The Interpreter then bid him tell to Christian



<P>the reason of his so doing.&nbsp; So he began and said, This night,



<P>as I was in my sleep, I dreamed, and behold the heavens grew



<P>exceeding black; also it thundered and lightened in most fearful wise,



<P>that it put me into an agony; so I looked up in my dream,



<P>and saw the clouds rack at an unusual rate, upon which I heard



<P>a great sound of a trumpet, and saw also a man sit upon a cloud,



<P>attended with the thousands of heaven; they were all in flaming fire:



<P>also the heavens were in a burning flame.&nbsp; I heard then a voice

saying,



<P>"Arise, ye dead, and come to judgement"; and with that the rocks rent,



<P>the graves opened, and the dead that were therein came forth.



<P>Some of them were exceeding glad, and looked upward; and some sought



<P>to hide themselves under the mountains.&nbsp; [1 Cor. 15:52; 1 Thes.

4:16;



<P>Jude 14; John 5:28,29; 2 Thes. 1:7,8; Rev. 20:11-14; Isa. 26:21;



<P>Micah 7:16,17; Ps. 95:1-3;&nbsp; Dan. 7:10]&nbsp; Then I saw the man



<P>that sat upon the cloud open the book, and bid the world draw near.



<P>Yet there was, by reason of a fierce flame which issued out



<P>and came from before him, a convenient distance betwixt him and them,



<P>as betwixt the judge and the prisoners at the bar.&nbsp; [Mal. 3:2,3;



<P>Dan. 7:9,10]&nbsp; I heard it also proclaimed to them that attended

on



<P>the man that sat on the cloud, Gather together the tares, the chaff,



<P>and stubble, and cast them into the burning lake.&nbsp; [Matt. 3:12;

13:30;



<P>Mal. 4:1]&nbsp; And with that, the bottomless pit opened, just whereabout



<P>I stood; out of the mouth of which there came, in an abundant manner,



<P>smoke and coals of fire, with hideous noises.&nbsp; It was also said



<P>to the same persons, "Gather my wheat into the garner."&nbsp; [Luke

3:17]



<P>And with that I saw many catched up and carried away into the clouds,



<P>but I was left behind.&nbsp; [1 Thes. 4:16,17]&nbsp; I also sought to

hide myself,



<P>but I could not, for the man that sat upon the cloud still kept his

eye



<P>upon me; my sins also came into my mind; and my conscience did accuse

me



<P>on every side.&nbsp; [Rom. 3:14,15]&nbsp; Upon this I awaked from my

sleep.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{90}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; But what is it that made you so afraid of this sight?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>MAN.&nbsp; Why, I thought that the day of judgement was come,



<P>and that I was not ready for it:&nbsp; but this frighted me most,



<P>that the angels gathered up several, and left me behind;



<P>also the pit of hell opened her mouth just where I stood.



<P>My conscience, too, afflicted me; and, as I thought, the Judge



<P>had always his eye upon me, shewing indignation in his countenance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{91}



<P>Then said the Interpreter to Christian, Hast thou considered



<P>all these things?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, and they put me in hope and fear.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>INTER.&nbsp; Well, keep all things so in thy mind that they may be as

a goad



<P>in thy sides, to prick thee forward in the way thou must go.



<P>Then Christian began to gird up his loins, and to address himself



<P>to his journey.&nbsp; Then said the Interpreter, The Comforter be always



<P>with thee, good Christian, to guide thee in the way that leads



<P>to the City.&nbsp; So Christian went on his way, saying--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Here I have seen things rare and profitable;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Things pleasant, dreadful, things to make me

stable



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; In what I have begun to take in hand;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Then let me think on them, and understand



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Wherefore they showed me were, and let me be



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Thankful, O good Interpreter, to thee."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{92}



<P>Now I saw in my dream, that the highway up which Christian was to go,



<P>was fenced on either side with a wall, and that wall



<P>was called Salvation.&nbsp; [Isa. 26:1]&nbsp; Up this way, therefore,



<P>did burdened Christian run, but not without great difficulty,



<P>because of the load on his back.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{93}



<P>He ran thus till he came at a place somewhat ascending,



<P>and upon that place stood a cross, and a little below, in the bottom,



<P>a sepulchre.&nbsp; So I saw in my dream, that just as Christian came

up



<P>with the cross, his burden loosed from off his shoulders,



<P>and fell from off his back, and began to tumble, and so continued to

do,



<P>till it came to the mouth of the sepulchre, where it fell in,



<P>and I saw it no more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{94}



<P>&lt;When God Releases us of our guilt and burden we are as those



<P>that leap for joy>



<P>Then was Christian glad and lightsome, and said, with a merry heart,



<P>"He hath given me rest by his sorrow, and life by his death."



<P>Then he stood still awhile to look and wonder; for it was



<P>very surprising to him, that the sight of the cross should thus



<P>ease him of his burden.&nbsp; He looked therefore, and looked again,



<P>even till the springs that were in his head sent the waters down



<P>his cheeks.&nbsp; [Zech. 12:10]&nbsp; Now, as he stood looking and weeping,



<P>behold three Shining Ones came to him and saluted him with



<P>"Peace be unto thee".&nbsp; So the first said to him, "Thy sins be



<P>forgiven thee" [Mark 2:5]; the second stripped him of his rags,



<P>and clothed him with change of raiment [Zech. 3:4]; the third also



<P>set a mark on his forehead, and gave him a roll with a seal upon it,



<P>which he bade him look on as he ran, and that he should give it in



<P>at the Celestial Gate.&nbsp; [Eph. 1:13]&nbsp; So they went their way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Who's this? the Pilgrim.&nbsp; How! 'tis very

true,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Old things are past away, all's become new.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Strange! he's another man, upon my word,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; They be fine feathers that make a fine bird.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;A Christian can sing though alone, when God doth give him



<P>the joy of his heart>



<P>Then Christian gave three leaps for joy, and went on singing--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Thus far I did come laden with my sin;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Nor could aught ease the grief that I was in



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Till I came hither:&nbsp; What a place is this!



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Must here be the beginning of my bliss?



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Must here the burden fall from off my back?



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Must here the strings that bound it to me crack?



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Blest cross! blest sepulchre! blest rather

be



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The Man that there was put to shame for me!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{95}



<P>&lt;Simple, Sloth, and Presumption>



<P>I saw then in my dream, that he went on thus, even until



<P>he came at a bottom, where he saw, a little out of the way,



<P>three men fast asleep, with fetters upon their heels.&nbsp; The name

of



<P>the one was Simple, another Sloth, and the third Presumption.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{96}



<P>Christian then seeing them lie in this case went to them,



<P>if peradventure he might awake them, and cried, You are like them



<P>that sleep on the top of a mast, for the Dead Sea is under you--



<P>a gulf that hath no bottom.&nbsp; [Prov. 23:34]&nbsp; Awake, therefore,



<P>and come away; be willing also, and I will help you off with your irons.



<P>He also told them, If he that "goeth about like a roaring lion"



<P>comes by, you will certainly become a prey to his teeth.&nbsp; [1 Pet.

5:8]



<P>&lt;There is no persuasion will do, if God openeth not the eyes>



<P>With that they looked upon him, and began to reply in this sort:



<P>Simple said, "I see no danger"; Sloth said, "Yet a little more sleep";



<P>and Presumption said, "Every fat must stand upon its own bottom;



<P>what is the answer else that I should give thee?"&nbsp; And so they

lay down



<P>to sleep again, and Christian went on his way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{97}



<P>Yet was he troubled to think that men in that danger should so



<P>little esteem the kindness of him that so freely offered to help them,



<P>both by awakening of them, counselling of them, and proffering



<P>to help them off with their irons.&nbsp; And as he was troubled thereabout,



<P>he espied two men come tumbling over the wall on the left hand



<P>of the narrow way; and they made up apace to him.&nbsp; The name of

the one



<P>was Formalist, and the name of the other Hypocrisy.&nbsp; So, as I said,



<P>they drew up unto him, who thus entered with them into discourse.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{98}



<P>&lt;Christian talked with them>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Gentlemen, whence came you, and whither go you?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FORM. and HYP.&nbsp; We were born in the land of Vain-glory,



<P>and are going for praise to Mount Zion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Why came you not in at the gate which standeth at



<P>the beginning of the way?&nbsp; Know you not that it is written,



<P>that he that cometh not in by the door, "but climbeth up some other

way,



<P>the same is a thief and a robber?"&nbsp; [John 10:1]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FORM. and HYP.&nbsp; They said, That to go to the gate for entrance

was,



<P>by all their countrymen, counted too far about; and that, therefore,



<P>their usual way was to make a short cut of it, and to climb over



<P>the wall, as they had done.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; But will it not be counted a trespass against the Lord of

the city



<P>whither we are bound, thus to violate his revealed will?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{99}



<P>&lt;They that come into the way, but not by the door,



<P>think that they can say something in vindication of their own practice>



<P>FORM. and HYP.&nbsp; They told him, that, as for that, he needed not



<P>to trouble his head thereabout; for what they did they had custom for;



<P>and could produce, if need were, testimony that would witness it



<P>for more than a thousand years.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; But, said Christian, will your practice stand a trial at

law?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FORM. and HYP.&nbsp; They told him, That custom, it being of



<P>so long a standing as above a thousand years, would, doubtless,



<P>now be admitted as a thing legal by any impartial judge; and besides,



<P>said they, if we get into the way, what's matter which way we get in?



<P>if we are in, we are in; thou art but in the way, who, as we perceive,



<P>came in at the gate; and we are also in the way, that came tumbling



<P>over the wall; wherein, now, is thy condition better than ours?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I walk by the rule of my Master; you walk by the rude working



<P>of your fancies.&nbsp; You are counted thieves already, by the Lord



<P>of the way; therefore, I doubt you will not be found true men at



<P>the end of the way.&nbsp; You come in by yourselves, without his direction;



<P>and shall go out by yourselves, without his mercy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{100}



<P>To this they made him but little answer; only they bid him



<P>look to himself.&nbsp; Then I saw that they went on every man in his

way



<P>without much conference one with another, save that these two men



<P>told Christian, that as to laws and ordinances, they doubted not



<P>but they should as conscientiously do them as he; therefore, said they,



<P>we see not wherein thou differest from us but by the coat that is on



<P>thy back, which was, as we trow, given thee by some of thy neighbours,



<P>to hide the shame of thy nakedness.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>p101--p150</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Page101--Page150</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>{101}



<P>&lt;Christian has got his Lord's coat on his back, and is



<P>comforted therewith; he is comforted, also, with his mark and his roll>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; By laws and ordinances you will not be saved, since you



<P>came not in by the door.&nbsp; [Gal. 2:16]&nbsp; And as for this coat

that is on



<P>my back, it was given me by the Lord of the place whither I go;



<P>and that, as you say, to cover my nakedness with.&nbsp; And I take it

as



<P>a token of his kindness to me; for I had nothing but rags before.



<P>And besides, thus I comfort myself as I go:&nbsp; Surely, think I,



<P>when I come to the gate of the city, the Lord thereof will know me



<P>for good since I have this coat on my back--a coat that he gave me



<P>freely in the day that he stripped me of my rags.&nbsp; I have, moreover,



<P>a mark in my forehead, of which, perhaps, you have taken no notice,



<P>which one of my Lord's most intimate associates fixed there in the day



<P>that my burden fell off my shoulders.&nbsp; I will tell you, moreover,



<P>that I had then given me a roll, sealed, to comfort me by reading



<P>as I go on the way; I was also bid to give it in at the Celestial Gate,



<P>in token of my certain going in after it; all which things, I doubt,



<P>you want, and want them because you came not in at the gate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{102}



<P>&lt;Christian has talk with himself>



<P>To these things they gave him no answer; only they looked upon



<P>each other, and laughed.&nbsp; Then, I saw that they went on all,



<P>save that Christian kept before, who had no more talk but with himself,



<P>and that sometimes sighingly, and sometimes comfortably;



<P>also he would be often reading in the roll that one of the Shining Ones



<P>gave him, by which he was refreshed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{103}



<P>&lt;He comes to the Hill Difficulty>



<P>I beheld, then, that they all went on till they came to the foot



<P>of the Hill Difficulty; at the bottom of which was a spring.
