<P>There were also in the same place two other ways besides that



<P>which came straight from the gate; one turned to the left hand,



<P>and the other to the right, at the bottom of the hill;



<P>but the narrow way lay right up the hill, and the name of the going

up



<P>the side of the hill is called Difficulty.&nbsp; Christian now went



<P>to the spring, and drank thereof, to refresh himself [Isa. 49:10],



<P>and then began to go up the hill, saying--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "The hill, though high, I covet to ascend,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The difficulty will not me offend;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; For I perceive the way to life lies here.



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Come, pluck up heart, let's neither faint nor

fear;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Better, though difficult, the right way to

go,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Than wrong, though easy, where the end is woe."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{104}



<P>&lt;The danger of turning out of the way>



<P>The other two also came to the foot of the hill; but when they saw



<P>that the hill was steep and high, and that there were two other ways



<P>to go, and supposing also that these two ways might meet again,



<P>with that up which Christian went, on the other side of the hill,



<P>therefore they were resolved to go in those ways.&nbsp; Now the name

of one



<P>of these ways was Danger, and the name of the other Destruction.



<P>So the one took the way which is called Danger, which led him into



<P>a great wood, and the other took directly up the way to Destruction,



<P>which led him into a wide field, full of dark mountains,



<P>where he stumbled and fell, and rose no more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Shall they who wrong begin yet rightly end?



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Shall they at all have safety for their friend?



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; No, no; in headstrong manner they set out,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And headlong will they fall at last no doubt."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{105}



<P>&lt;A ward of grace>



<P>I looked, then, after Christian, to see him go up the hill,



<P>where I perceived he fell from running to going, and from going



<P>to clambering upon his hands and his knees, because of the steepness



<P>of the place.&nbsp; Now, about the midway to the top of the hill



<P>was a pleasant arbour, made by the Lord of the hill for the refreshing



<P>of weary travellers; thither, therefore, Christian got, where also



<P>he sat down to rest him.&nbsp; Then he pulled his roll out of his bosom,



<P>and read therein to his comfort; he also now began afresh to take



<P>a review of the coat or garment that was given him as he stood



<P>by the cross.&nbsp; Thus pleasing himself awhile, he at last fell



<P>into a slumber, and thence into a fast sleep, which detained him



<P>in that place until it was almost night; and in his sleep,



<P>his roll fell out of his hand.&nbsp; &lt;He that sleeps is a loser>



<P>Now, as he was sleeping, there came one to him, and awaked him, saying,



<P>Go to the ant, thou sluggard; consider her ways and be wise.



<P>[Prov. 6:6]&nbsp; And with that Christian started up, and sped him on



<P>his way, and went apace, till he came to the top of the hill.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{106}



<P>&lt;Christian meets with Mistrust and Timorous>



<P>Now, when he was got up to the top of the hill, there came two men



<P>running to meet him amain; the name of the one was Timorous,



<P>and of the other, Mistrust; to whom Christian said, Sirs,



<P>what's the matter?&nbsp; You run the wrong way.&nbsp; Timorous answered,



<P>that they were going to the City of Zion, and had got up



<P>that difficult place; but, said he, the further we go, the more danger



<P>we meet with; wherefore we turned, and are going back again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Yes, said Mistrust, for just before us lie a couple of lions in the

way,



<P>whether sleeping or waking we know not, and we could not think,



<P>if we came within reach, but they would presently pull us in pieces.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{107}



<P>&lt;Christian shakes off fear>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then said Christian, You make me afraid, but whither shall

I fly



<P>to be safe?&nbsp; If I go back to mine own country, that is prepared



<P>for fire and brimstone, and I shall certainly perish there.



<P>If I can get to the Celestial City, I am sure to be in safety there.



<P>I must venture.&nbsp; To go back is nothing but death; to go forward



<P>is fear of death, and life-everlasting beyond it.&nbsp; I will yet



<P>go forward.&nbsp; So Mistrust and Timorous ran down the hill,



<P>and Christian went on his way.&nbsp; &lt;Christian missed his roll



<P>wherein he used to take comfort>&nbsp; But, thinking again of what



<P>he had heard from the men, he felt in his bosom for his roll,



<P>that he might read therein, and be comforted; but he felt,



<P>and found it not.&nbsp; Then was Christian in great distress,



<P>and knew not what to do; for he wanted that which used to relieve him,



<P>and that which should have been his pass into the Celestial City.



<P>&lt;He is perplexed for his roll>&nbsp; Here, therefore, he begun to

be



<P>much perplexed, and knew not what to do.&nbsp; At last he bethought

himself



<P>that he had slept in the arbour that is on the side of the hill;



<P>and, falling down upon his knees, he asked God's forgiveness for that



<P>his foolish act, and then went back to look for his roll.



<P>But all the way he went back, who can sufficiently set forth the sorrow



<P>of Christian's heart?&nbsp; Sometimes he sighed, sometimes he wept,



<P>and oftentimes he chid himself for being so foolish to fall asleep



<P>in that place, which was erected only for a little refreshment



<P>for his weariness.&nbsp; Thus, therefore, he went back, carefully looking



<P>on this side and on that, all the way as he went, if happily he might



<P>find his roll, that had been his comfort so many times in his journey.



<P>He went thus, till he came again within sight of the arbour



<P>where he sat and slept; but that sight renewed his sorrow the more,



<P>by bringing again, even afresh, his evil of sleeping into his mind.



<P>[Rev. 2:5; 1 Thes. 5:7,8]&nbsp; &lt;Christian bewails his foolish sleeping>



<P>Thus, therefore, he now went on bewailing his sinful sleep, saying,



<P>O wretched man that I am that I should sleep in the day-time!



<P>that I should sleep in the midst of difficulty! that I should



<P>so indulge the flesh, as to use that rest for ease to my flesh,



<P>which the Lord of the hill hath erected only for the relief



<P>of the spirits of pilgrims!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{108}



<P>How many steps have I took in vain! Thus it happened to Israel,



<P>for their sin; they were sent back again by the way of the Red Sea;



<P>and I am made to tread those steps with sorrow, which I might have trod



<P>with delight, had it not been for this sinful sleep.&nbsp; How far might

I



<P>have been on my way by this time!&nbsp; I am made to tread those steps



<P>thrice over, which I needed not to have trod but once; yea,



<P>now also I am like to be benighted, for the day is almost spent.



<P>O, that I had not slept!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{109}



<P>&lt;Christian findeth his roll where he lost it>



<P>Now, by this time he was come to the arbour again, where for a while



<P>he sat down and wept; but at last, as Christian would have it,



<P>looking sorrowfully down under the settle, there he espied his roll;



<P>the which he, with trembling and haste, catched up, and put it into



<P>his bosom.&nbsp; But who can tell how joyful this man was when he had

gotten



<P>his roll again! for this roll was the assurance of his life



<P>and acceptance at the desired haven.&nbsp; Therefore he laid it up



<P>in his bosom, gave thanks to God for directing his eye to the place



<P>where it lay, and with joy and tears betook himself again



<P>to his journey.&nbsp; But oh, how nimbly now did he go up the rest



<P>of the hill!&nbsp; Yet, before he got up, the sun went down upon Christian;



<P>and this made him again recall the vanity of his sleeping



<P>to his remembrance; and thus he again began to condole with himself:



<P>O thou sinful sleep; how, for thy sake, am I like to be benighted



<P>in my journey!&nbsp; I must walk without the sun; darkness must cover



<P>the path of my feet; and I must hear the noise of the doleful creatures,



<P>because of my sinful sleep.&nbsp; [1 Thes. 5:6,7]&nbsp; Now also he

remembered



<P>the story that Mistrust and Timorous told him of; how they were frighted



<P>with the sight of the lions.&nbsp; Then said Christian to himself again,



<P>These beasts range in the night for their prey; and if they should



<P>meet with me in the dark, how should I shift them?&nbsp; How should

I escape



<P>being by them torn in pieces?&nbsp; Thus he went on his way.



<P>But while he was thus bewailing his unhappy miscarriage,



<P>he lift up his eyes, and behold there was a very stately palace



<P>before him, the name of which was Beautiful; and it stood just by



<P>the highway side.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{110}



<P>So I saw in my dream that he made haste and went forward,



<P>that if possible he might get lodging there.&nbsp; Now, before he had



<P>gone far, he entered into a very narrow passage, which was about



<P>a furlong off the porter's lodge; and looking very narrowly before him



<P>as he went, he espied two lions in the way.&nbsp; Now, thought he,



<P>I see the dangers that Mistrust and Timorous were driven back by.



<P>(The lions were chained, but he saw not the chains.)



<P>Then he was afraid, and thought also himself to go back after them,



<P>for he thought nothing but death was before him.&nbsp; But the porter



<P>at the lodge, whose name is Watchful, perceiving that Christian



<P>made a halt as if he would go back, cried unto him, saying,



<P>Is thy strength so small?&nbsp; [Mark 8:34-37]&nbsp; Fear not the lions,



<P>for they are chained, and are placed there for trial of faith



<P>where it is, and for discovery of those that had none.



<P>Keep in the midst of the path, no hurt shall come unto thee.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Difficulty is behind, Fear is before,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Though he's got on the hill, the lions roar;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; A Christian man is never long at ease,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When one fright's gone, another doth him seize."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{111}



<P>Then I saw that he went on, trembling for fear of the lions,



<P>but taking good heed to the directions of the porter;



<P>he heard them roar, but they did him no harm.&nbsp; Then he clapped



<P>his hands, and went on till he came and stood before the gate



<P>where the porter was.&nbsp; Then said Christian to the porter,



<P>Sir, what house is this?&nbsp; And may I lodge here to-night?



<P>The porter answered, This house was built by the Lord of the hill,



<P>and he built it for the relief and security of pilgrims.



<P>The porter also asked whence he was, and whither he was going.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{112}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I am come from the City of Destruction, and am going



<P>to Mount Zion; but because the sun is now set, I desire, if I may,



<P>to lodge here to-night.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>POR.&nbsp; What is your name?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; My name is now Christian, but my name at the first was Graceless;



<P>I came of the race of Japheth, whom God will persuade to dwell



<P>in the tents of Shem.&nbsp; [Gen. 9:27]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>POR.&nbsp; But how doth it happen that you come so late?&nbsp; The sun

is set.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{113}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I had been here sooner, but that, "wretched man that I am!"



<P>I slept in the arbour that stands on the hillside; nay, I had,



<P>notwithstanding that, been here much sooner, but that, in my sleep.



<P>I lost my evidence, and came without it to the brow of the hill



<P>and then feeling for it, and finding it not, I was forced



<P>with sorrow of heart, to go back to the place where I slept my sleep,



<P>where I found it, and now I am come.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>POR.&nbsp; Well, I will call out one of the virgins of this place, who

will,



<P>if she likes your talk, bring you into the rest of the family,



<P>according to the rules of the house.&nbsp; So Watchful, the porter,



<P>rang a bell, at the sound of which came out at the door of the house,



<P>a grave and beautiful damsel, named Discretion, and asked why



<P>she was called.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{114}



<P>The porter answered, This man is in a journey from



<P>the City of Destruction to Mount Zion, but being weary and benighted,



<P>he asked me if he might lodge here to-night; so I told him I would call



<P>for thee, who, after discourse had with him, mayest do as seemeth



<P>thee good, even according to the law of the house.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{115}



<P>Then she asked him whence he was, and whither he was going,



<P>and he told her.&nbsp; She asked him also how he got into the way;



<P>and he told her.&nbsp; Then she asked him what he had seen and met with



<P>in the way; and he told, her.&nbsp; And last she asked his name; so

he said,



<P>It is Christian, and I have so much the more a desire to lodge



<P>here to-night, because, by what I perceive, this place was built



<P>by the Lord of the hill for the relief and security of pilgrims.



<P>So she smiled, but the water stood in her eyes; and after



<P>a little pause, she said, I will call forth two or three more



<P>of the family.&nbsp; So she ran to the door, and called out Prudence,



<P>Piety, and Charity, who, after a little more discourse with him,



<P>had him into the family; and many of them, meeting him at the threshold



<P>of the house, said, Come in, thou blessed of the Lord;



<P>this house was built by the Lord of the hill, on purpose to entertain



<P>such pilgrims in.&nbsp; Then he bowed his head, and followed them



<P>into the house.&nbsp; So when he was come in and sat down, they gave

him



<P>something to drink, and consented together, that until supper was ready,



<P>some of them should have some particular discourse with Christian,



<P>for the best improvement of time; and they appointed Piety,



<P>and Prudence, and Charity to discourse with him; and thus they began:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{116}



<P>&lt;Piety discourses him>



<P>PIETY.&nbsp; Come, good Christian, since we have been so loving to you,



<P>to receive you in our house this night, let us, if perhaps we may



<P>better ourselves thereby, talk with you of all things



<P>that have happened to you in your pilgrimage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; With a very good will, and I am glad that you are



<P>so well disposed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{117}



<P>PIETY.&nbsp; What moved you at first to betake yourself to a pilgrim's

life?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;How Christian was driven out of his own country>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I was driven out of my native country by a dreadful sound



<P>that was in mine ears:&nbsp; to wit, that unavoidable destruction



<P>did attend me, if I abode in that place where I was.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PIETY.&nbsp; But how did it happen that you came out of your country



<P>this way?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;How he got into the way to Zion>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; It was as God would have it; for when I was under the fears



<P>of destruction, I did not know whither to go; but by chance



<P>there came a man, even to me, as I was trembling and weeping,



<P>whose name is Evangelist, and he directed me to the wicket-gate,



<P>which else I should never have found, and so set me into the way



<P>that hath led me directly to this house.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{118}



<P>PIETY.&nbsp; But did you not come by the house of the Interpreter?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;A rehearsal of what he saw in the way>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, and did see such things there, the remembrance of which



<P>will stick by me as long as I live; especially three things:&nbsp; to

wit,



<P>how Christ, in despite of Satan, maintains his work of grace



<P>in the heart; how the man had sinned himself quite out of hopes



<P>of God's mercy; and also the dream of him that thought in his sleep



<P>the day of judgement was come.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PIETY.&nbsp; Why, did you hear him tell his dream?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, and a dreadful one it was.&nbsp; I thought it made my

heart ache



<P>as he was telling of it; but yet I am glad I heard it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{119}



<P>PIETY.&nbsp; Was that all that you saw at the house of the Interpreter?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; No; he took me and had me where he shewed me a stately palace,



<P>and how the people were clad in gold that were in it; and how



<P>there came a venturous man and cut his way through the armed men



<P>that stood in the door to keep him out, and how he was bid to come in,



<P>and win eternal glory.&nbsp; Methought those things did ravish my heart!



<P>I would have stayed at that good man's house a twelvemonth,



<P>but that I knew I had further to go.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{120}



<P>PIETY.&nbsp; And what saw you else in the way?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Saw! why, I went but a little further, and I saw one,



<P>as I thought in my mind, hang bleeding upon the tree;



<P>and the very sight of him made my burden fall off my back,



<P>(for I groaned under a very heavy burden,) but then it fell down



<P>from off me.&nbsp; It was a strange thing to me, for I never saw



<P>such a thing before; yea, and while I stood looking up,



<P>for then I could not forbear looking, three Shining Ones came to me.



<P>One of them testified that my sins were forgiven me; another stripped

me



<P>of my rags, and gave me this broidered coat which you see;



<P>and the third set the mark which you see in my forehead, and gave me



<P>this sealed roll.&nbsp; (And with that he plucked it out of his bosom.)

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{121}



<P>PIETY.&nbsp; But you saw more than this, did you not?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; The things that I have told you were the best;



<P>yet some other matters I saw, as, namely--I saw three men,



<P>Simple, Sloth, and Presumption, lie asleep a little out of the way,



<P>as I came, with irons upon their heels; but do you think



<P>I could awake them?&nbsp; I also saw Formality and Hypocrisy come



<P>tumbling over the wall, to go, as they pretended, to Zion,



<P>but they were quickly lost, even as I myself did tell them;



<P>but they would not believe.&nbsp; But above all, I found it hard work



<P>to get up this hill, and as hard to come by the lions' mouths,



<P>and truly if it had not been for the good man, the porter that stands



<P>at the gate, I do not know but that after all I might have



<P>gone back again; but now I thank God I am here, and I thank you



<P>for receiving of me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{122}



<P>Then Prudence thought good to ask him a few questions,



<P>and desired his answer to them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Prudence discourses him>



<P>PRUD.&nbsp; Do you not think sometimes of the country from whence you

came?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Christian's thoughts of his native country>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, but with much shame and detestation:&nbsp; "Truly,



<P>if I had been mindful of that country from whence I came out,



<P>I might have had opportunity to have returned; but now I desire



<P>a better country, that is, an heavenly."&nbsp; [Heb. 11:15,16]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PRUD.&nbsp; Do you not yet bear away with you some of the things



<P>that then you were conversant withal?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Christian distasted with carnal cogitations>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, but greatly against my will; especially my inward



<P>and carnal cogitations, with which all my countrymen, as well as myself,



<P>were delighted; but now all those things are my grief;



<P>and might I but choose mine own things, &lt;Christian's choice>



<P>I would choose never to think of those things more; but when I would



<P>be doing of that which is best, that which is worst is with me.



<P>[Rom 7:16-19]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{123}



<P>PRUD.&nbsp; Do you not find sometimes, as if those things were vanquished,



<P>which at other times are your perplexity?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Christian's golden hours>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, but that is seldom; but they are to me golden hours



<P>in which such things happen to me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PRUD.&nbsp; Can you remember by what means you find your annoyances,



<P>at times, as if they were vanquished?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;How Christian gets power against his corruptions>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, when I think what I saw at the cross, that will do it;



<P>and when I look upon my broidered coat, that will do it;



<P>also when I look into the roll that I carry in my bosom,



<P>that will do it; and when my thoughts wax warm about whither I am going,



<P>that will do it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{124}



<P>PRUD.&nbsp; And what is it that makes you so desirous to go to Mount

Zion?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Why Christian would be at Mount Zion>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Why, there I hope to see him alive that did hang dead on

the cross;



<P>and there I hope to be rid of all those things that to this day are

in me



<P>an annoyance to me; there, they say, there is no death; and there I

shall



<P>dwell with such company as I like best.&nbsp; [Isa. 25:8; Rev. 21:4]



<P>For, to tell you truth, I love him, because I was by him eased of



<P>my burden; and I am weary of my inward sickness.&nbsp; I would fain

be where



<P>I shall die no more, and with the company that shall continually cry,



<P>"Holy, Holy, Holy!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{125}



<P>&lt;Charity discourses him>



<P>Then said Charity to Christian, Have you a family?&nbsp; Are you



<P>a married man?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I have a wife and four small children.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHAR.&nbsp; And why did you not bring them along with you?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Christian's love to his wife and children>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Then Christian wept, and said, Oh, how willingly



<P>would I have done it! but they were all of them utterly averse



<P>to my going on pilgrimage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHAR.&nbsp; But you should have talked to them, and have endeavoured



<P>to have shown them the danger of being behind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; So I did; and told them also of what God had shown to me



<P>of the destruction of our city; "but I seemed to them as one



<P>that mocked", and they believed me not.&nbsp; [Gen. 19:14]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHAR.&nbsp; And did you pray to God that he would bless your counsel

to them?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, and that with much affection:&nbsp; for you must think



<P>that my wife and poor children were very dear unto me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHAR.&nbsp; But did you tell them of your own sorrow, and fear



<P>of destruction? for I suppose that destruction was visible enough



<P>to you.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Christian's fears of perishing might be read in his very countenance>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, over, and over, and over.&nbsp; They might also see

my fears



<P>in my countenance, in my tears, and also in my trembling under



<P>the apprehension of the judgement that did hang over our heads;



<P>but all was not sufficient to prevail with them to come with me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHAR.&nbsp; But what could they say for themselves, why they came not?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{126}



<P>&lt;The cause why his wife and children did not go with him>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Why, my wife was afraid of losing this world, and my children



<P>were given to the foolish delights of youth:&nbsp; so what by one thing,



<P>and what by another, they left me to wander in this manner alone.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHAR.&nbsp; But did you not, with your vain life, damp all that you



<P>by words used by way of persuasion to bring them away with you?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{127}



<P>&lt;Christian's good conversation before his wife and children>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Indeed, I cannot commend my life; for I am conscious to myself



<P>of many failings therein; I know also that a man by his conversation



<P>may soon overthrow what by argument or persuasion he doth labour



<P>to fasten upon others for their good.&nbsp; Yet this I can say,



<P>I was very wary of giving them occasion, by any unseemly action,



<P>to make them averse to going on pilgrimage.&nbsp; Yea, for this very

thing



<P>they would tell me I was too precise, and that I denied myself



<P>of things, for their sakes, in which they saw no evil.&nbsp; Nay,



<P>I think I may say, that if what they saw in me did hinder them,



<P>it was my great tenderness in sinning against God, or of doing



<P>any wrong to my neighbour.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Christian clear of their blood if they perish>



<P>CHAR.&nbsp; Indeed Cain hated his brother, "because his own works were

evil,



<P>and his brother's righteous" [1 John 3:12]; and if thy wife and children



<P>have been offended with thee for this, they thereby show themselves



<P>to be implacable to good, and "thou hast delivered thy soul



<P>from their blood".&nbsp; [Ezek. 3:19]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{128}



<P>&lt;What Christian had to his supper>



<P>Now I saw in my dream, that thus they sat talking together



<P>until supper was ready.&nbsp; So when they had made ready,



<P>they sat down to meat.&nbsp; &lt;Their talk at supper time>&nbsp; Now

the table



<P>was furnished "with fat things, and with wine that was well refined":



<P>and all their talk at the table was about the Lord of the hill;



<P>as, namely, about what he had done, and wherefore he did what he did,



<P>and why he had builded that house.&nbsp; And by what they said,



<P>I perceived that he had been a great warrior, and had fought with



<P>and slain "him that had the Power of death", but not without



<P>great danger to himself, which made me love him the more.



<P>[Heb. 2:14,15]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{129}



<P>For, as they said, and as I believe (said Christian), he did it with



<P>the loss of much blood; but that which put glory of grace



<P>into all he did, was, that he did it out of pure love to his country.



<P>And besides, there were some of them of the household that said



<P>they had been and spoke with him since he did die on the cross;



<P>and they have attested that they had it from his own lips,



<P>that he is such a lover of poor pilgrims, that the like is not



<P>to be found from the east to the west.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{130}



<P>&lt;Christ makes princes of beggars>



<P>They, moreover, gave an instance of what they affirmed, and that was,



<P>he had stripped himself of his glory, that he might do this



<P>for the poor; and that they heard him say and affirm,



<P>"that he would not dwell in the mountain of Zion alone."



<P>They said, moreover, that he had made many pilgrims princes,



<P>though by nature they were beggars born, and their original



<P>had been the dunghill.&nbsp; [1 Sam 2:8; Ps. 113:7]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{131}



<P>&lt;Christian's bedchamber>



<P>Thus they discoursed together till late at night; and after



<P>they had committed themselves to their Lord for protection,



<P>they betook themselves to rest:&nbsp; the Pilgrim they laid



<P>in a large upper chamber, whose window opened towards the sun-rising:



<P>the name of the chamber was Peace; where he slept till break of day,



<P>and then he awoke and sang--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "Where am I now?&nbsp; Is this the love and

care



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Of Jesus for the men that pilgrims are?



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Thus to provide! that I should be forgiven!



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; And dwell already the next door to heaven!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{132}



<P>&lt;Christian had into the study, and what he saw there>



<P>So in the morning they all got up; and, after some more discourse,



<P>they told him that he should not depart till they had shown him



<P>the rarities of that place.&nbsp; And first they had him into the study,



<P>where they showed him records of the greatest antiquity; in which,



<P>as I remember my dream, they showed him first the pedigree of the Lord



<P>of the hill, that he was the son of the Ancient of Days, and came by



<P>that eternal generation.&nbsp; Here also was more fully recorded the

acts



<P>that he had done, and the names of many hundreds that he had taken



<P>into his service; and how he had placed them in such habitations that



<P>could neither by length of days, nor decays of nature, be dissolved.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{133}



<P>Then they read to him some of the worthy acts that some of his servants



<P>had done:&nbsp; as, how they had "subdued kingdoms, wrought righteousness,



<P>obtained promises, stopped the mouths of lions, quenched the violence



<P>of fire, escaped the edge of the sword, out of weakness



<P>were made strong, waxed valiant in fight, and turned to flight



<P>the armies of the aliens."&nbsp; [Heb 11:33,34]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{134}



<P>They then read again, in another part of the records of the house,



<P>where it was shewed how willing their Lord was to receive



<P>into his favour any, even any, though they in time past had offered



<P>great affronts to his person and proceedings.&nbsp; Here also were



<P>several other histories of many other famous things, of all which



<P>Christian had a view; as of things both ancient and modern;



<P>together with prophecies and predictions of things that have



<P>their certain accomplishment, both to the dread and amazement



<P>of enemies, and the comfort and solace of pilgrims.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{135}



<P>&lt;Christian had into the armoury>



<P>The next day they took him and had him into the armoury,



<P>where they showed him all manner of furniture, which their Lord



<P>had provided for pilgrims, as sword, shield, helmet, breastplate,



<P>ALL-PRAYER, and shoes that would not wear out.&nbsp; And there was here



<P>enough of this to harness out as many men for the service of their Lord



<P>as there be stars in the heaven for multitude.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{136}



<P>&lt;Christian is made to see ancient things>



<P>They also showed him some of the engines with which some of his servants



<P>had done wonderful things.&nbsp; They shewed him Moses' rod;



<P>the hammer and nail with which Jael slew Sisera; the pitchers, trumpets,



<P>and lamps too, with which Gideon put to flight the armies of Midian.



<P>Then they showed him the ox's goad wherewith Shamgar slew



<P>six hundred men.&nbsp; They showed him also the jaw-bone with which

Samson



<P>did such mighty feats.&nbsp; They showed him, moreover, the sling and

stone



<P>with which David slew Goliath of Gath; and the sword, also,



<P>with which their Lord will kill the Man of Sin, in the day



<P>that he shall rise up to the prey.&nbsp; They showed him, besides,



<P>many excellent things, with which Christian was much delighted.



<P>This done, they went to their rest again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{137}



<P>&lt;Christian showed the Delectable Mountains>



<P>Then I saw in my dream, that on the morrow he got up to go forward;



<P>but they desired him to stay till the next day also;



<P>and then, said they, we will, if the day be clear, show you



<P>the Delectable Mountains, which, they said, would yet further add



<P>to his comfort, because they were nearer the desired haven



<P>than the place where at present he was; so he consented and stayed.



<P>When the morning was up, they had him to the top of the house,



<P>and bid him look south; so he did:&nbsp; and behold, at a great distance,



<P>he saw a most pleasant mountainous country, beautified with woods,



<P>vineyards, fruits of all sorts, flowers also, with springs



<P>and fountains, very delectable to behold.&nbsp; [Isa. 33:16,17]



<P>Then he asked the name of the country.&nbsp; They said it was



<P>Immanuel's Land; and it is as common, said they, as this hill is,



<P>to and for all the pilgrims.&nbsp; And when thou comest there from thence,



<P>said they, thou mayest see to the gate of the Celestial City,



<P>as the shepherds that live there will make appear.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{138}



<P>&lt;Christian sets forward>



<P>Now he bethought himself of setting forward, and they were willing



<P>he should.&nbsp; But first, said they, let us go again into the armoury.



<P>&lt;Christian sent away armed>&nbsp; So they did; and when they came

there,



<P>they harnessed him from head to foot with what was of proof,



<P>lest, perhaps, he should meet with assaults in the way.&nbsp; He being,



<P>therefore, thus accoutred, walketh out with his friends to the gate,



<P>and there he asked the porter if he saw any pilgrims pass by.



<P>Then the porter answered, Yes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{139}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Pray, did you know him? said he.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>POR.&nbsp; I asked him his name, and he told me it was Faithful.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Oh, said Christian, I know him; he is my townsman,



<P>my near neighbour; he comes from the place where I was born.



<P>How far do you think he may be before?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>POR.&nbsp; He is got by this time below the hill.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;How Christian and the Porter greet at parting>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Well, said Christian, good Porter, the Lord be with thee,



<P>and add to all thy blessings much increase, for the kindness that thou



<P>hast showed to me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{140}



<P>&lt;The Valley of Humiliation>



<P>Then he began to go forward; but Discretion, Piety, Charity,



<P>and Prudence would accompany him down to the foot of the hill.



<P>So they went on together, reiterating their former discourses,



<P>till they came to go down the hill.&nbsp; Then said Christian,



<P>As it was difficult coming up, so, so far as I can see, it is dangerous



<P>going down.&nbsp; Yes, said Prudence, so it is, for it is a hard matter



<P>for a man to go down into the Valley of Humiliation, as thou art now,



<P>and to catch no slip by the way; therefore, said they, are we come out



<P>to accompany thee down the hill.&nbsp; So he began to go down,



<P>but very warily; yet he caught a slip or two.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{141}



<P>Then I saw in my dream that these good companions, when Christian



<P>was gone to the bottom of the hill, gave him a loaf of bread,



<P>a bottle of wine, and a cluster of raisins; and then he went on his

way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Christian has no armour for his back>



<P>But now, in this Valley of Humiliation, poor Christian was hard put



<P>to it; for he had gone but a little way, before he espied a foul fiend



<P>coming over the field to meet him; his name is Apollyon.



<P>Then did Christian begin to be afraid, and to cast in his mind



<P>whether to go back or to stand his ground.&nbsp; But he considered again



<P>that he had no armour for his back; and therefore thought



<P>that to turn the back to him might give him the greater advantage



<P>with ease to pierce him with his darts.&nbsp; &lt;Christian's resolution



<P>at the approach of Apollyon>&nbsp; Therefore he resolved to venture



<P>and stand his ground; for, thought he, had I no more in mine eye



<P>than the saving of my life, it would be the best way to stand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{142}



<P>So he went on, and Apollyon met him.&nbsp; Now the monster was



<P>hideous to behold; he was clothed with scales, like a fish,



<P>(and they are his pride,) he had wings like a dragon, feet like a bear,



<P>and out of his belly came fire and smoke, and his mouth was as



<P>the mouth of a lion.&nbsp; When he was come up to Christian, he beheld

him



<P>with a disdainful countenance, and thus began to question with him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{143}



<P>APOL.&nbsp; Whence come you? and whither are you bound?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I am come from the City of Destruction, which is the place



<P>of all evil, and am going to the City of Zion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Discourse betwixt Christian and Apollyon>



<P>APOL.&nbsp; By this I perceive thou art one of my subjects,



<P>for all that country is mine, and I am the prince and god of it.



<P>How is it, then, that thou hast run away from thy king?



<P>Were it not that I hope thou mayest do me more service,



<P>I would strike thee now, at one blow, to the ground.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{144}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I was born, indeed, in your dominions, but your service was

hard,



<P>and your wages such as a man could not live on, "for the wages of sin



<P>is death" [Rom 6:23]; therefore, when I was come to years, I did,



<P>as other considerate persons do, look out, if, perhaps,



<P>I might mend myself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Apollyon's flattery>



<P>APOL.&nbsp; There is no prince that will thus lightly lose his subjects,



<P>neither will I as yet lose thee; but since thou complainest



<P>of thy service and wages, be content to go back:&nbsp; what our country



<P>will afford, I do here promise to give thee.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; But I have let myself to another, even to the King of princes;



<P>and how can I, with fairness, go back with thee?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{145}



<P>&lt;Apollyon undervalues Christ's service>



<P>APOL.&nbsp; Thou hast done in this, according to the proverb,



<P>"Changed a bad for a worse"; but it is ordinary for those that have



<P>professed themselves his servants, after a while to give him the slip,



<P>and return again to me.&nbsp; Do thou so too, and all shall be well.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I have given him my faith, and sworn my allegiance to him;



<P>how, then, can I go back from this, and not be hanged as a traitor?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Apollyon pretends to be merciful>



<P>APOL.&nbsp; Thou didst the same to me, and yet I am willing to pass

by all,



<P>if now thou wilt yet turn again and go back.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{146}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; What I promised thee was in my nonage; and, besides,



<P>I count the Prince under whose banner now I stand is able to absolve

me;



<P>yea, and to pardon also what I did as to my compliance with thee;



<P>and besides, O thou destroying Apollyon! to speak truth,



<P>I like his service, his wages, his servants, his government,



<P>his company, and country, better than thine; and, therefore,



<P>leave off to persuade me further; I am his servant,



<P>and I will follow him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{147}



<P>&lt;Apollyon pleads the grievous ends of Christians, to dissuade Christian



<P>from persisting in his way>



<P>APOL.&nbsp; Consider, again, when thou art in cool blood,



<P>what thou art like to meet with in the way that thou goest.



<P>Thou knowest that, for the most part, his servants come to an ill end,



<P>because they are transgressors against me and my ways.



<P>How many of them have been put to shameful deaths! and, besides,



<P>thou countest his service better than mine, whereas he never came yet



<P>from the place where he is to deliver any that served him



<P>out of their hands; but as for me, how many times, as all the world



<P>very well knows, have I delivered, either by power, or fraud,



<P>those that have faithfully served me, from him and his,



<P>though taken by them; and so I will deliver thee.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; His forbearing at present to deliver them is on purpose



<P>to try their love, whether they will cleave to him to the end;



<P>and as for the ill end thou sayest they come to, that is most glorious



<P>in their account; for, for present deliverance, they do not much



<P>expect it, for they stay for their glory, and then they shall have it



<P>when their Prince comes in his and the glory of the angels.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>APOL.&nbsp; Thou hast already been unfaithful in thy service to him;



<P>and how dost thou think to receive wages of him?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Wherein, O Apollyon! have I been unfaithful to him?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{148}



<P>&lt;Apollyon pleads Christian's infirmities against him>



<P>APOL.&nbsp; Thou didst faint at first setting out, when thou wast



<P>almost choked in the Gulf of Despond; thou didst attempt wrong ways



<P>to be rid of thy burden, whereas thou shouldst have stayed



<P>till thy Prince had taken it off; thou didst sinfully sleep



<P>and lose thy choice thing; thou wast, also, almost persuaded to go back



<P>at the sight of the lions; and when thou talkest of thy journey,



<P>and of what thou hast heard and seen, thou art inwardly desirous



<P>of vain-glory in all that thou sayest or doest.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; All this is true, and much more which thou hast left out;



<P>but the Prince whom I serve and honour is merciful,



<P>and ready to forgive; but, besides, these infirmities possessed me



<P>in thy country, for there I sucked them in; and I have groaned



<P>under them, been sorry for them, and have obtained pardon of my Prince.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{149}



<P>&lt;Apollyon in a rage falls upon Christian>



<P>APOL.&nbsp; Then Apollyon broke out into a grievous rage, saying,



<P>I am an enemy to this Prince; I hate his person, his laws, and people;



<P>I am come out on purpose to withstand thee.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Apollyon, beware what you do; for I am in the King's highway,



<P>the way of holiness; therefore take heed to yourself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>APOL.&nbsp; Then Apollyon straddled quite over the whole breadth of

the way,



<P>and said, I am void of fear in this matter:&nbsp; prepare thyself to

die;



<P>for I swear by my infernal den, that thou shalt go no further;



<P>here will I spill thy soul.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{150}



<P>And with that he threw a flaming dart at his breast; but Christian had



<P>a shield in his hand, with which he caught it, and so prevented



<P>the danger of that.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Christian wounded in his understanding, faith, and conversation>



<P>Then did Christian draw, for he saw it was time to bestir him;



<P>and Apollyon as fast made at him, throwing darts as thick as hail;



<P>by the which, notwithstanding all that Christian could do to avoid it,



<P>Apollyon wounded him in his head, his hand, and foot.



<P>This made Christian give a little back; Apollyon, therefore,



<P>followed his work amain, and Christian again took courage,



<P>and resisted as manfully as he could.&nbsp; This sore combat lasted



<P>for above half a day, even till Christian was almost quite spent;



<P>for you must know that Christian, by reason of his wounds,



<P>must needs grow weaker and weaker.



<P>

<BODY>

<B><FONT SIZE=+2>THE PILGRIM'S PROGRESS</FONT></B>

<UL>

<UL>

<UL>

<UL><B><FONT SIZE=+1>In the Similitude of a Dream</FONT></B></UL>

</UL>

</UL>

</UL>



<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>{10}



<P>As I walked through the wilderness of this world, I lighted on



<P>a certain place where was a Den &lt;The Jail/Gaol>, and I laid me down



<P>in that place to sleep:&nbsp; and, as I slept, I dreamed a dream.



<P>I dreamed, and behold, I saw a man clothed with rags,



<P>standing in a certain place, with his face from his own house,



<P>a book in his hand, and a great burden upon his back.&nbsp; [Isa. 64:6;



<P>Luke 14:33; Ps. 38:4; Hab. 2:2; Acts 16:30,31]&nbsp; I looked,



<P>and saw him open the book, and read therein; and, as he read,



<P>he wept, and trembled; and, not being able longer to contain,



<P>&lt;His Outcry> he brake out with a lamentable cry, saying,



<P>"What shall I do?"&nbsp; [Acts 2:37]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{11}



<P>In this plight, therefore, he went home and refrained himself



<P>as long as he could, that his wife and children should not perceive



<P>his distress; but he could not be silent long, because that



<P>his trouble increased.&nbsp; Wherefore at length he brake his mind



<P>to his wife and children; and thus he began to talk to them:



<P>O my dear wife, said he, and you the children of my bowels,



<P>I, your dear friend, am in myself undone by reason of a burden



<P>that lieth hard upon me; moreover, I am for certain informed that



<P>this our city &lt;This world> will be burned with fire from heaven;



<P>in which fearful overthrow, both myself, with thee my wife,



<P>and you my sweet babes, shall miserably come to ruin,



<P>except (the which yet I see not) some way of escape can be found,



<P>whereby we may be delivered.&nbsp; &lt;He knows not the way yet/He knows

no way



<P>of escape as of yet>&nbsp; At this his relations were sore amazed;



<P>not for that they believed that what he had said to them was true,



<P>but because they thought that some frenzy distemper had got into



<P>his head; therefore, it drawing towards night, and they hoping



<P>that sleep might settle his brains, with all haste they got him to bed.



<P>But the night was as troublesome to him as the day; wherefore,



<P>instead of sleeping, he spent it in sighs and tears.&nbsp; So,



<P>when the morning was come, they would know how he did.



<P>He told them, Worse and worse:&nbsp; he also set to talking to them

again;



<P>but they began to be hardened.&nbsp; &lt;Carnal physic for a sick soul>



<P>They also thought to drive away his distemper by harsh and surly



<P>carriages to him; sometimes they would deride, sometimes they



<P>would chide, and sometimes they would quite neglect him.



<P>Wherefore he began to retire himself to his chamber, to pray for



<P>and pity them, and also to condole his own misery; he would also



<P>walk solitarily in the fields, sometimes reading, and sometimes praying:



<P>and thus for some days he spent his time.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{12}



<P>Now, I saw, upon a time, when he was walking in the fields, that he

was,



<P>as he was wont, reading in his book, and greatly distressed in his mind;



<P>and, as he read, he burst out, as he had done before, crying,



<P>"What shall I do to be saved?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{13}



<P>I saw also that he looked this way and that way, as if he would run;



<P>yet he stood still, because, as I perceived, he could not tell



<P>which way to go.&nbsp; I looked then, and saw a man named Evangelist



<P>coming to him and asked, Wherefore dost thou cry?&nbsp; [Job 33:23]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{14}



<P>He answered, Sir, I perceive by the book in my hand, that I am



<P>condemned to die, and after that to come to judgement [Heb. 9:27];



<P>and I find that I am not willing to do the first [Job 16:21],



<P>nor able to do the second.&nbsp; [Ezek. 22:14]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; CHRISTIAN no sooner leaves the World but meets



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; EVANGELIST, who lovingly him greets



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; With tidings of another:&nbsp; and doth show



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Him how to mount to that from this below.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{15}



<P>Then said Evangelist, Why not willing to die, since this life



<P>is attended with so many evils?&nbsp; The man answered, Because I fear



<P>that this burden is upon my back will sink me lower than the grave,



<P>and I shall fall into Tophet.&nbsp; [Isa. 30:33]&nbsp; And, Sir, if

I be not fit



<P>to go to prison, I am not fit, I am sure, to go to judgement,



<P>and from thence to execution; and the thoughts of these things



<P>make me cry.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{16}



<P>&lt;Conviction of the necessity of flying>



<P>Then said Evangelist, If this be thy condition, why standest thou still?



<P>He answered, Because I know not whither to go.&nbsp; Then he gave him



<P>a parchment roll, and there was written within, Flee from the wrath



<P>to come.&nbsp; [Matt. 3.7]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{17}



<P>The man therefore read it, and looking upon Evangelist very carefully,



<P>said, Whither must I fly?&nbsp; Then said Evangelist, pointing with



<P>his finger over a very wide field, Do you see yonder wicket-gate?



<P>[Matt. 7:13,14]&nbsp; &lt;Christ, and the way to Him cannot be found



<P>without the Word>&nbsp; The man said, No.&nbsp; Then said the other,



<P>Do you see yonder shining light?&nbsp; [Ps. 119:105; 2 Pet. 1:19]&nbsp;

He said,



<P>I think I do.&nbsp; Then said Evangelist, Keep that light in your eye,



<P>and go up directly thereto:&nbsp; so shalt thou see the gate; at which,



<P>when thou knockest, it shall be told thee what thou shalt do.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{18}



<P>So I saw in my dream that the man began to run.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Now, he had not run far from his own door, but his wife and children,



<P>perceiving it, began to cry after him to return; but the man



<P>put his fingers in his ears, and ran on, crying, Life! life!



<P>eternal life! [Luke 14:26]&nbsp; So he looked not behind him,



<P>but fled towards the middle of the plain.&nbsp; [Gen. 19:17]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{19}



<P>&lt;They that fly from the wrath to come, are a gazing-stock of the

world>



<P>The neighbours also came out to see him run [Jer. 20:10]; and,



<P>as he ran, some mocked, others threatened, and some cried after him



<P>to return; and, among those that did so, there were two that resolved



<P>to fetch him back by force.&nbsp; &lt;Obstinate and Pliable follow him>



<P>The name of the one was Obstinate and the name of the other Pliable.



<P>Now, by this time, the man was got a good distance from them; but,



<P>however, they were resolved to pursue him, which they did,



<P>and in a little time they overtook him.&nbsp; Then said the man, Neighbours,



<P>wherefore are ye come?&nbsp; They said, To persuade you to go back with

us.



<P>But he said, That can by no means be; you dwell, said he,



<P>in the City of Destruction, the place also where I was born:



<P>I see it to be so; and, dying there, sooner or later,



<P>you will sink lower than the grave, into a place that burns



<P>with fire and brimstone:&nbsp; be content, good neighbours,



<P>and go along with me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{20}



<P>OBST.&nbsp; What! said Obstinate, and leave our friends and our comforts



<P>behind us?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, said Christian, for that was his name, because that

ALL



<P>which you shall forsake is not worthy to be compared with a little



<P>of that which I am seeking to enjoy [2 Cor. 4:18]; and,



<P>if you will go along with me, and hold it, you shall fare as I myself;



<P>for there, where I go, is enough and to spare.&nbsp; [Luke 15:17]



<P>Come away, and prove my words.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{21}



<P>OBST.&nbsp; What are the things you seek, since you leave all the world



<P>to find them?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I seek an inheritance incorruptible, undefiled, and that



<P>fadeth not away [1 Pet. 1:4], and it is laid up in heaven,



<P>and safe there [Heb. 11:16], to be bestowed, at the time appointed,



<P>on them that diligently seek it.&nbsp; Read it so, if you will, in my

book.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>OBST.&nbsp; Tush! said Obstinate, away with your book; will you



<P>go back with us or no?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; No, not I, said the other, because I have laid my hand



<P>to the plough.&nbsp; [Luke 9:62]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{22}



<P>OBST.&nbsp; Come, then, neighbour Pliable, let us turn again, and go

home



<P>without him; there is a company of these crazy-headed coxcombs, that,



<P>when they take a fancy by the end, are wiser in their own eyes



<P>than seven men that can render a reason.&nbsp; [Prov. 26:16]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PLI.&nbsp; Then said Pliable, Don't revile; if what the good Christian

says



<P>is true, the things he looks after are better than ours:



<P>my heart inclines to go with my neighbour.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>OBST.&nbsp; What! more fools still! Be ruled by me, and go back;



<P>who knows whither such a brain-sick fellow will lead you?&nbsp; Go back,



<P>go back, and be wise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{23}



<P>&lt;Christian and Obstinate pull for Pliable's soul>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Nay, but do thou come with thy neighbour, Pliable;



<P>there are such things to be had which I spoke of, and many



<P>more glorious besides.&nbsp; If you believe not me, read here in this

book;



<P>and for the truth of what is expressed therein, behold, all is confirmed



<P>by the blood of Him that made it.&nbsp; [Heb. 9:17-22; 13:20]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PLI.&nbsp; Well, neighbour Obstinate, said Pliable, &lt;Pliable contented

to go



<P>with Christian> I begin to come to a point; I intend to go along



<P>with this good man, and to cast in my lot with him:&nbsp; but,



<P>my good companion, do you know the way to this desired place?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{24}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I am directed by a man, whose name is Evangelist,



<P>to speed me to a little gate that is before us, where we shall receive



<P>instructions about the way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PLI.&nbsp; Come, then, good neighbour, let us be going.&nbsp; Then they

went



<P>both together.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>OBST.&nbsp; And I will go back to my place, said Obstinate;



<P>&lt;Obstinate goes railing back> I will be no companion of such misled,



<P>fantastical fellows.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{25}



<P>Now, I saw in my dream, that when Obstinate was gone back,



<P>Christian and Pliable went talking over the plain; and thus they began



<P>their discourse.&nbsp; &lt;Talk between Christian and Pliable>

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{26}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Come, neighbour Pliable, how do you do?&nbsp; I am glad you

are



<P>persuaded to go along with me.&nbsp; Had even Obstinate himself but

felt



<P>what I have felt of the powers and terrors of what is yet unseen,



<P>he would not thus lightly have given us the back.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PLI.&nbsp; Come, neighbour Christian, since there are none but us two

here,



<P>tell me now further what the things are, and how to be enjoyed,



<P>whither we are going.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{27}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I can better conceive of them with my mind, than speak of

them



<P>with my tongue &lt;God's things unspeakable>:&nbsp; but yet, since you

are



<P>desirous to know, I will read of them in my book.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PLI.&nbsp; And do you think that the words of your book are certainly

true?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, verily; for it was made by Him that cannot lie.&nbsp;

[Titus 1:2]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PLI.&nbsp; Well said; what things are they?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; There is an endless kingdom to be inhabited, and everlasting

life



<P>to be given us, that we may inhabit that kingdom for ever.&nbsp; [Isa.

45:17;



<P>John 10:28,29]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PLI.&nbsp; Well said; and what else?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; There are crowns and glory to be given us, and garments



<P>that will make us shine like the sun in the firmament of heaven.



<P>[2 Tim. 4:8; Rev. 3:4; Matt. 13:43]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PLI.&nbsp; This is very pleasant; and what else?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; There shall be no more crying, nor Sorrow:&nbsp; for He that

is



<P>owner of the place will wipe all tears from our eyes.&nbsp; [Isa. 25.6-8;



<P>Rev. 7:17, 21:4]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{28}



<P>PLI.&nbsp; And what company shall we have there?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; There we shall be with seraphims and cherubims,



<P>creatures that will dazzle your eyes to look on them.&nbsp; [Isa. 6:2]



<P>There also you shall meet with thousands and ten thousands



<P>that have gone before us to that place; none of them are hurtful,



<P>but loving and holy; every one walking in the sight of God,



<P>and standing in his presence with acceptance for ever.



<P>[1 Thess. 4:16,17; Rev. 5:11]&nbsp; In a word, there we shall see the

elders



<P>with their golden crowns [Rev. 4:4], there we shall see the holy virgins



<P>with their golden harps [Rev. 14:1-5], there we shall see men



<P>that by the world were cut in pieces, burnt in flames, eaten of beasts,



<P>drowned in the seas, for the love that they bare to the Lord



<P>of the place, all well, and clothed with immortality as with a garment.



<P>[John 12:25; 2 Cor. 5:4]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PLI.&nbsp; The hearing of this is enough to ravish one's heart.



<P>But are these things to be enjoyed?&nbsp; How shall we get to be



<P>sharers thereof?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; The Lord, the Governor of the country, hath recorded that



<P>in this book; the substance of which is, If we be truly willing



<P>to have it, he will bestow it upon us freely.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PLI.&nbsp; Well, my good companion, glad am I to hear of these things:



<P>come on, let us mend our pace.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I cannot go so fast as I would, by reason of this burden



<P>that is on my back.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{29}



<P>&lt;The Slough of Despond>



<P>Now I saw in my dream, that just as they had ended this talk



<P>they drew near to a very miry slough, that was in the midst



<P>of the plain; and they, being heedless, did both fall suddenly



<P>into the bog.&nbsp; The name of the slough was Despond.&nbsp; Here,

therefore,



<P>they wallowed for a time, being grievously bedaubed with the dirt;



<P>and Christian, because of the burden that was on his back,



<P>began to sink in the mire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{30}



<P>PLI.&nbsp; Then said Pliable; Ah! neighbour Christian, where are you

now?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Truly, said Christian, I do not know.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PLI.&nbsp; At this Pliable began to be offended, and angrily said



<P>to his fellow, Is this the happiness you have told me all this while

of?



<P>If we have such ill speed at our first setting out, what may we expect



<P>betwixt this and our journey's end?&nbsp; May I get out again with my

life,



<P>you shall possess the brave country alone for me.&nbsp; &lt;It is not

enough



<P>to be pliable>&nbsp; And, with that, he gave a desperate struggle or

two,



<P>and got out of the mire on that side of the slough which was next to



<P>his own house:&nbsp; so away he went, and Christian saw him no more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{31}



<P>Wherefore Christian was left to tumble in the Slough of Despond alone:



<P>but still he endeavoured to struggle to that side of the slough



<P>that was still further from his own house, and next to the wicket-gate;



<P>&lt;Christian in trouble seeks still to get further from his own house>



<P>the which he did, but could not get out, because of the burden



<P>that was upon his back:&nbsp; but I beheld in my dream, that a man



<P>came to him, whose name was Help, and asked him, What he did there?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Sir, said Christian, I was bid go this way by a man



<P>called Evangelist, who directed me also to yonder gate,



<P>that I might escape the wrath to come; and as I was going thither



<P>I fell in here.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{32}



<P>&lt;The Promises>



<P>HELP.&nbsp; But why did not you look for the steps?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Fear followed me so hard, that I fled the next way, and fell

in.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Help lifts him up>



<P>HELP.&nbsp; Then said he, Give me thy hand:&nbsp; so he gave him his

hand,



<P>and he drew him out, and set him upon sound ground, and bid him



<P>go on his way.&nbsp; [Ps. 40:2]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{33}



<P>Then I stepped to him that plucked him out, and said, Sir, wherefore,



<P>since over this place is the way from the City of Destruction



<P>to yonder gate, is it that this plat is not mended, that poor travellers



<P>might go thither with more security?&nbsp; And he said unto me,



<P>&lt;What makes the Slough of Despond> This miry slough is such a place



<P>as cannot be mended; it is the descent whither the scum and filth



<P>that attends conviction for sin doth continually run, and therefore



<P>it is called the Slough of Despond; for still, as the sinner is awakened



<P>about his lost condition, there ariseth in his soul many fears,



<P>and doubts, and discouraging apprehensions, which all of them



<P>get together, and settle in this place.&nbsp; And this is the reason



<P>of the badness of this ground.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{34}



<P>It is not the pleasure of the King that this place should remain so

bad.



<P>[Isa. 35:3,4]&nbsp; His labourers also have, by the direction of



<P>His Majesty's surveyors, been for above these sixteen hundred years



<P>employed about this patch of ground, if perhaps it might



<P>have been mended:&nbsp; yea, and to my knowledge, said he,



<P>here have been swallowed up at least twenty thousand cart-loads,



<P>yea, millions of wholesome instructions, that have at all seasons



<P>been brought from all places of the King's dominions, and they that



<P>can tell, say they are the best materials to make good ground



<P>of the place; if so be, it might have been mended, but it is



<P>the Slough of Despond still, and so will be when they have done



<P>what they can.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{35}



<P>&lt;The promises of forgiveness and acceptance to life by faith in Christ>



<P>True, there are, by the direction of the Law-giver, certain good and



<P>substantial steps, placed even through the very midst of this slough;



<P>but at such time as this place doth much spew out its filth,



<P>as it doth against change of weather, these steps are hardly seen;



<P>or, if they be, men, through the dizziness of their heads, step beside,



<P>and then they are bemired to purpose, notwithstanding the steps



<P>be there; but the ground is good when they are once got in at the gate.



<P>[1 Sam. 12:23]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{36}



<P>&lt;Pliable got home, and is visited of his neighbours>



<P>Now, I saw in my dream, that by this time Pliable was got home



<P>to his house again, so that his neighbours came to visit him;



<P>&lt;His entertainment by them at his return> and some of them called

him



<P>wise man for coming back, and some called him fool for hazarding himself



<P>with Christian:&nbsp; others again did mock at his cowardliness; saying,



<P>Surely, since you began to venture, I would not have been so base



<P>to have given out for a few difficulties.&nbsp; So Pliable sat sneaking



<P>among them.&nbsp; But at last he got more confidence, and then they

all



<P>turned their tales, and began to deride poor Christian behind his back.



<P>And thus much concerning Pliable.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{37}



<P>&lt;Mr. Worldly Wiseman meets with Christian>



<P>Now, as Christian was walking solitarily by himself,



<P>he espied one afar off, come crossing over the field to meet him;



<P>and their hap was to meet just as they were crossing the way of



<P>each other.&nbsp; The gentleman's name that met him was Mr. Worldly

Wiseman,



<P>he dwelt in the town of Carnal Policy, a very great town,



<P>and also hard by from whence Christian came.&nbsp; This man, then,



<P>meeting with Christian, and having some inkling of him,--



<P>for Christian's setting forth from the City of Destruction was



<P>much noised abroad, not only in the town where he dwelt,



<P>but also it began to be the town talk in some other places,--



<P>Mr. Worldly Wiseman, therefore, having some guess of him,



<P>by beholding his laborious going, by observing his sighs and groans,



<P>and the like, began thus to enter into some talk with Christian.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{38}



<P>&lt;Talk betwixt Mr. Worldly Wiseman and Christian>



<P>WORLD.&nbsp; How now, good fellow, whither away after this burdened

manner?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; A burdened manner, indeed, as ever, I think, poor creature

had!



<P>And whereas you ask me, Whither away?&nbsp; I tell you, Sir,



<P>I am going to yonder wicket-gate before me; for there, as I am informed,



<P>I shall be put into a way to be rid of my heavy burden.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>WORLD.&nbsp; Hast thou a wife and children?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes; but I am so laden with this burden that I cannot take



<P>that pleasure in them as formerly; methinks I am as if I had none.



<P>[1 Cor 7:29]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>WORLD.&nbsp; Wilt thou hearken unto me if I give thee counsel?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; If it be good, I will; for I stand in need of good counsel.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{39}



<P>&lt;Mr. Worldly Wiseman's counsel to Christian>



<P>WORLD.&nbsp; I would advise thee, then, that thou with all speed



<P>get thyself rid of thy burden; for thou wilt never be settled



<P>in thy mind till then; nor canst thou enjoy the benefits



<P>of the blessing which God hath bestowed upon thee till then.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; That is that which I seek for, even to be rid of



<P>this heavy burden; but get it off myself, I cannot; nor is there



<P>any man in our country that can take it off my shoulders;



<P>therefore am I going this way, as I told you, that I may be rid of



<P>my burden.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>WORLD.&nbsp; Who bid thee go this way to be rid of thy burden?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; A man that appeared to me to be a very great and



<P>honourable person; his name, as I remember, is Evangelist.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{40}



<P>&lt;Mr. Worldly Wiseman condemned Evangelist's counsel>



<P>WORLD.&nbsp; I beshrew him for his counsel! there is not a more dangerous



<P>and troublesome way in the world than is that unto which he hath



<P>directed thee; and that thou shalt find, if thou wilt be ruled



<P>by his counsel.&nbsp; Thou hast met with something, as I perceive, already;



<P>for I see the dirt of the Slough of Despond is upon thee;



<P>but that slough is the beginning of the sorrows that do attend those



<P>that go on in that way.&nbsp; Hear me, I am older than thou;



<P>thou art like to meet with, in the way which thou goest,



<P>wearisomeness, painfulness, hunger, perils, nakedness, sword, lions,



<P>dragons, darkness, and, in a word, death, and what not!&nbsp; These

things



<P>are certainly true, having been confirmed by many testimonies.



<P>And why should a man so carelessly cast away himself, by giving heed



<P>to a stranger?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;The frame of the heart of a young Christian>



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Why, Sir, this burden upon my back is more terrible to me



<P>than all these things which you have mentioned; nay, methinks I care

not



<P>what I meet with in the way, if so be I can also meet with deliverance



<P>from my burden.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{41}



<P>&lt;Worldly Wiseman does not like that men should be serious



<P>in reading the Bible>



<P>WORLD.&nbsp; How camest thou by the burden at first?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; By reading this book in my hand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>WORLD.&nbsp; I thought so; and it is happened unto thee as to other

weak men,



<P>who, meddling with things too high for them, do suddenly fall



<P>into thy distractions; which distractions do not only unman men,



<P>as thine, I perceive, have done thee, but they run them upon



<P>desperate ventures to obtain they know not what.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I know what I would obtain; it is ease for my heavy burden.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{42}



<P>&lt;Whether Mr. Worldly Wiseman prefers morality before the strait gate>



<P>WORLD.&nbsp; But why wilt thou seek for ease this way, seeing so many

dangers



<P>attend it? especially since, hadst thou but patience to hear me,



<P>I could direct thee to the obtaining of what thou desirest,



<P>without the dangers that thou in this way wilt run thyself into; yea,



<P>and the remedy is at hand.&nbsp; Besides, I will add, that instead of



<P>those dangers, thou shalt meet with much safety, friendship,



<P>and content.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Pray, Sir, open this secret to me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{43}



<P>WORLD.&nbsp; Why, in yonder village--the village is named Morality--



<P>there dwells a gentleman whose name is Legality, a very judicious man,



<P>and a man of very good name, that has skill to help men off



<P>with such burdens as thine are from their shoulders:&nbsp; yea,



<P>to my knowledge, he hath done a great deal of good this way;



<P>ay, and besides, he hath skill to cure those that are somewhat crazed



<P>in their wits with their burdens.&nbsp; To him, as I said, thou mayest

go,



<P>and be helped presently.&nbsp; His house is not quite a mile from this

place,



<P>and if he should not be at home himself, he hath a pretty young man



<P>to his son, whose name is Civility, that can do it (to speak on)



<P>as well as the old gentleman himself; there, I say, thou mayest be



<P>eased of thy burden; and if thou art not minded to go back to



<P>thy former habitation, as, indeed, I would not wish thee,



<P>thou mayest send for thy wife and children to thee to this village,



<P>where there are houses now stand empty, one of which thou mayest have



<P>at reasonable rates; provision is there also cheap and good;



<P>and that which will make thy life the more happy is, to be sure,



<P>there thou shalt live by honest neighbours, in credit and good fashion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{44}



<P>&lt;Christian snared by Worldly Wiseman's words>



<P>Now was Christian somewhat at a stand; but presently he concluded,



<P>if this be true, which this gentleman hath said, my wisest course



<P>is to take his advice; and with that he thus further spoke.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{45}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Sir, which is my way to this honest man's house?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&lt;Mount Sinai>



<P>WORLD.&nbsp; Do you see yonder hill?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, very well.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>WORLD.&nbsp; By that hill you must go, and the first house you come

at



<P>is his.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{46}



<P>&lt;Christian afraid that Mount Sinai would fall on his head>



<P>So Christian turned out of his way to go to Mr. Legality's house



<P>for help; but, behold, when he was got now hard by the hill,



<P>it seemed so high, and also that side of it that was next the wayside



<P>did hang so much over, that Christian was afraid to venture further,



<P>lest the hill should fall on his head; wherefore there he stood still



<P>and wotted not what to do.&nbsp; Also his burden now seemed heavier

to him



<P>than while he was in his way.&nbsp; There came also flashes of fire



<P>out of the hill, that made Christian afraid that he should be burned.



<P>[Ex. 19:16,18]&nbsp; Here, therefore, he sweat and did quake for fear.



<P>[Heb. 12:21]

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; When Christians unto carnal men give ear,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Out of their way they go, and pay for 't dear;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; For Master Worldly Wiseman can but shew



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; A saint the way to bondage and to woe.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{47}



<P>&lt;Evangelist findeth Christian under Mount Sinai, and looketh severely



<P>upon him>



<P>And now he began to be sorry that he had taken Mr. Worldly Wiseman's



<P>counsel.&nbsp; And with that he saw Evangelist coming to meet him;



<P>at the sight also of whom he began to blush for shame.&nbsp; So Evangelist



<P>drew nearer and nearer; and coming up to him, he looked upon him



<P>with a severe and dreadful countenance, and thus began to reason



<P>with Christian.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{48}



<P>&lt;Evangelist reasons afresh with Christian>



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; What dost thou here, Christian? said he:&nbsp; at which

words



<P>Christian knew not what to answer; wherefore at present he stood



<P>speechless before him.&nbsp; Then said Evangelist further,



<P>Art not thou the man that I found crying without the walls



<P>of the City of Destruction?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, dear Sir, I am the man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; Did not I direct thee the way to the little wicket-gate?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Yes, dear Sir, said Christian.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; How is it, then, that thou art so quickly turned aside?



<P>for thou art now out of the way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{49}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; I met with a gentleman so soon as I had got over



<P>the Slough of Despond, who persuaded me that I might,



<P>in the village before me, find a man that would take off my burden.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; What was he?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; He looked like a gentleman, and talked much to me,



<P>and got me at last to yield; so I came hither; but when I beheld



<P>this hill, and how it hangs over the way, I suddenly made a stand



<P>lest it should fall on my head.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; What said that gentleman to you?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; Why, he asked me whither I was going, and I told him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; And what said he then?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>CHR.&nbsp; He asked me if I had a family?&nbsp; And I told him.&nbsp;

But, said I,



<P>I am so loaden with the burden that is on my back, that I cannot



<P>take pleasure in them as formerly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>EVAN.&nbsp; And what said he then?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>{50}



<P>CHR.&nbsp; He bid me with speed get rid of my burden; and I told him



<P>that it was ease that I sought.&nbsp; And said I, I am therefore going



<P>to yonder gate, to receive further direction how I may get to the place



<P>of deliverance.&nbsp; So he said that he would shew me a better way,



<P>and short, not so attended with difficulties as the way, Sir, that you



<P>set me in; which way, said he, will direct you to a gentleman's house



<P>that hath skill to take off these burdens, so I believed him,



<P>and turned out of that way into this, if haply I might be soon eased



<P>of my burden.&nbsp; But when I came to this place, and beheld things



<P>as they are, I stopped for fear (as I said) of danger:



<P>but I now know not what to do.



<P>
